THE BED PAGE.
RATNBOWS AND WITCUES.
I remember, ever so long; ago,
At the other side of the world away
When rain would cease on an April day,
When the mountain mists would roll and rin
And the rainbow ride in the purple skies—
Dow they would say to us, * Run, dear heart.
Out where you see the bright bow start,
And there you will gather a heap of gold,
As much as ever your hands can hold ;
Out of the wood and beyond the gate,
Run for your fortune or you'll be late I *
How we would run 1 I remember still
The dangerous dash down the garden hill
And many a stumble and many a slip,
Our eager eyes on the rainbow-dip ;
Limbs a-weary but never a rest,
Beating hearts on the hopeless quest—
For the further we raced the further passed
The rainbow goal, till we tired at last.
0 golden years! ye are past and gone
With the far-off flash of a distant dream;
But still we are striving and straggling on
Chasing the gold and the rainbow gleam l
I remember, ever so long ago,
At the other side of the world away
As we in our tiny white cots lay'
Half in slumber and half-awake
Watching the nesting swallow take
(A moving shade on the blind so white)
His last trip home to his nest at night—
How they would say at our bedsides: ‘Soon,
Dear little heart, the great red moon
Will climb the sky to her fleecy seat,
Stars at her shoulder and stars at her feet;
And if you should wake to-night, dear heart,
When the night and morning meet and part,
And open your window ever so wide.
You II see where the brave broom-witches ride
Low to the fir-tops and high to the moon
With their peaky hats and their pointed shoon ;
And first of them all your lover so fair,
The moon-wind tossing her red-gold hair 1'
Our childish hopes they are dead lang syne,
But l wait at night with my window wide,
And many a lonely watch is mine
To see my love when the witches ride!
*.B.W.
WILL H. OQILVIB.
The Basis of Art.
Upon this ancient and tough subject a Foreign
Dogmatist called ‘Compton’ supplies something
winch is either a sermon or a text, and the Local
Dogmatist supplements what may be a text if it
isn t a sermon. And possibly a dash of suggestion
may spring when text smites text, or sermon
succeeds in banging the head of sermon. ‘Comp-
ton ’ begins—
Barren as the Burns controversy has been, i
has—hke occasional previous plunges of the ‘Rec
Page into Art polemics—accentuated the cry in'
need of criticism for something or anything liki
system. Argument, without admitted facts or
established generalisations, generally becomes
mere polarisation of personal dogmatisms. Dis-
putants immersed in fathomless incertitudes de-
duce such inferences as are not authenticated by
their unsupported say-so from vast general prin
eiples rapidly manufactured to Ruit the momentary
exigencies of the dispute. There is no canon by
which mouth-filling generalities can be tried, no
accepted principles from which definite con-
clusions can be evolved. (by which,
be it understood, is meant the consideration and
not the practice of Art principles) is apparently
the only realm of intellectual energy where each
man may still be a law unto himself. The writei
who proves Burns a great poet beoause of his
plenitude of ‘moral sense,’ the other writer who
regards him as a mere haggis-eating doggerelist
because of his scantiness in originality, his ad-
mirer who exults in his universality, and his
detractor who sees him tied to the earth, earthy,
all start by assuming that their particular point is
the point by which art in general is to he judged,
itaoh begins in the middle of things with whatever
premises he thinks proper, and ends, still in the
middle of things, with whatever conclusion he
thinks necessary.
[This is interesting ; but how does it apply? Is there
not, for example, an underlying inference that Some-
body here considered • Burns a great poet beoause of his
plenitude of “ moral sense"'? But nobody here so
considered. Is there not an underlying inference that
somebody here regarded Burns •as a mere haggis-
eating doggerelist?’ But nobody here so regarded.
Then there is a direct statement that such persons, wher-
ever they consider or regard, all stark, by assuming that
their particular point, Ac. But the statement itself is
assumption. So far as the Burns controversy is con-
cerned, it is erroneous assumption. Neither of the dis-
putants pretended to do more than voice his own views.
One begins to have a dreadful suspicion that ‘Compton*
has himself started in the middle of things, with the
premisses he thinks proper.]
Any effort to begin at the beginning would in-,
volve the questioning of all these and similar art-
prinoiples. Leaving out altogether the ‘ moral
sense ’ theory of Art (which, by the way, has just
been reinforced by another powerful spirit of
unreason, iolstoi, in his last book) take the pro-
position that true art-work should be ‘ universal.’
Perhaps it is true. But why is it true? Cer-
tainly not because a ‘ Bulletin ’ correspondent
asserts it ex cathedra. Nor yet because any
amount of other authority—all equally dogmatic
and unwarranted—can be got behind it. So far
as I have been able to see, no other grounds have
been hinted at. And yet, if a sound conclusion,
rt must rest upon visible foundations. Clearly,
either about matters of taste we must not dispute,
or if we dispute, we must bring the dispute into
the logical category, and conduct it orderly, ‘by
slow gradation and well-balanced form.'
[Unluckily for ‘Compton,' people will dispute about
matters of taste; and they can’t dispute logically,
because logic has no foothold. All that logio can do is
to argue that, there is no basis for argument. Then logic
is done. Here are two things: Man and a universe,
infinitely varying in space and time. Problem: to find
the spot and the moment when any given impression of
the universe, or part of it, will be received by two men in
exactly the same way, and with exactly the same force.
Why, there ain't any sicli a spot or moment—not
this side of eternity, anyway. Even supposing that
two identical brains ever existed, with two identical
sets of senses, there have never been two iden-
tical environments. So, applied to Taste, logic
has to begin by admitting that it can only get ap-
proximations, never accurate values for comparison.
Outside mathematics, discussion can never be more than
the for-what-it-may-be-worth affirmation of a personal
and exclusive emotion or judgment. So there is nothing
true except to the individual ; and if, for example, a
color-blind individual sees red as green, red is green for
him, and he can call this a Green Page if he chooses, and
be as right as anybody is.]
Nature raised to the manth power’ was the
bold and hopeful epigram by which a Bulletin
contributor once endeavored to express the
true inwardness of art achievement. A taking
pronouncement, its extreme condensation con-
ceals a failure in grasp for which that condensa-
tion is partly responsible. Expanded, it reads
that the object of Art is to pourtray Nature, but
to pourtray her not as she is, but as she appears
to the sensibility-and-imagination- fitted artist.
It is an effort—and a bold and good one—to dis-
tinguish Art from photography. The mind is un-
questionably a higher machinefor depicting Nature
than the camera. But, as usual, its epigram-
matic lucidity is the outcome of abortive general-
isation ; the facts are expressed tersely because half
of them are neglected. There are branches of Art
into winch Nature as subject matter does notenter
It seems quite evident that any sufficient formula
must include music in all its forms, as well as the
imitative arts. The intense similarity between
the phenomena presented and the emotions
excited by the two kinds compel the grouping of
them in one genus. The defiance of Prometheus
or the curse of Lear stirs the same dormant eino-
-rl >nal i rei ! a *. th ® thunder of the Fifth Symphony,
lhe Faculty, whatever it may be,
responds indifferently in the normal man to har-
mony and color—to poetry and song. Nothing
but small, specific differences divide the effects of
one art-manifestation from another, while, as a
group, they are generioally distinct from the
emotions which attend on spotting the winner or
seeing Clem. Hill caught at short-leg.
Tiie notes of the chromatic scale, which are the
elements of music, have no existence apart from
the purposes of the art, in the way in which the
elements of the plastic or verbal arts can be said
to exist. Observe. Of the six hundred billions of
colors in the spectrum, the artist is free to transfer
all, or as many as are practicable, to canvas.
They constitute his material, his vehicle. They
already exist in the external universe, no doubt,
though I am inclined to regard this as an accident
rather than an essential of the art. But from the
innumerable sounds existent “in Nature,” twelve,
arbitrarily picked out in certain arbitrary rela-
tions, form the entire material of the composer.
Music hangs upon this handful of arbitrary
relations. It depends, not upon middlb C being a
note of so many vibrations per second, but
upon B and D being separated by such and
such intervals from it, and upon the intervening
sounds (of which there are millions) being reso-
lutely ignored. This arbitrary suppression of
sound gradations, and the consequent arrival
at the ‘scale’ has no counterpart out-
side the art. The confused noises of the forest
and the street are not music. The voices of
Nature know nothing of diminished sevenths—
there is neither tonic nor dominant in the thunder
or the hurricane.
These entirely human musical intervals, un-
heard of and unguessed at ‘ in Nature ’ until some
unknown benefactor of the race invented them,
are the source and origin of the most amazing
aesthetic influences. The sweetness and lightness
of Mozartian melody spring from this selective
process based on an arithmetical juggle. Beet-
hoven stirs abysmal depths of feeling because he
deals in twelve oarefully-ohosen noises instead of
twelve million; the splendors of Wagnerian
orchestration are due to the fact that each note
vibrates just half as rapidly as its octave.
In all this there is very little reference to
nature, and long-continued settlement of every-
thing by nian, who, finding that certain noisns
please linn, selects and combines them as his
imagination and judgement dictate. That man
himself, with his whole mental bag of tricks, in-
tellect, imagination, and everything else, is part
of nature is true enough. But it does not affect
the point. The ‘ nature ’ which is to be raised
to the manth power is external nature, including
man, where necessary, objectively only. It is to
supply the passive subject for mam’s transform-
ing energy. And my contention is that in music
that imaginative energy uses its own products as
subject Were there no other man but the artist
i O ‘ TiW d Btl f b Jt m " s,c - though there might be
uo Fortunes of the Rougons. 9
an^ I< T’ ? b is c,ear that effects pro-
duced on the faculty (again, whatever
nf L^ Y - u arlBe frotn arl,itrar y combinations
a t kci*! phenomena, prmded by the imagina-
tion and judgement of the composer. The bolder
and subtler the imagination the deeper and more
genuine the effect.
[Now ‘Compton ’ interestingly and obviously ‘falls in ’
And observe, it is possible to say logically and decisively
that he falls in because there is involved-not a question
of Taste but-a question of Fact. Of course Music is
included in the sphere of Art But ‘Nature as subject
matter enters into * Music just as certainly as she
enters into Painting, or Sculpture, or any other
branch of Art. The human eye and ear select
the light - vibrations and sound - vibrations which
they are best fitted to select and that is
all about it. Probably some men’s ears have
more nerve-filaments than other men’s ears, and cer-
tainly other men's ears have nerve-filaments vibrating dif-
ferently from those in some men’sears ; and a man’s Music
is made for him, in the first instance, by the number and
kind of vibrations which his ear selects and conveys to
his brain. As men are more or less built on the same
pattern, their ears often select approximately the same
kind of sound-vibrations, and the hearing-centres in
their brains are often stimulated by those vibrations to
approximately the same reactions. But there is no
ground for affirmation that such-and-such a sound-vibra-
tion does not make Music.—for somebody. The confused
noises of the forest and the street often are Music
—for the man who finds them Music. Chinese
Music is Musio for the Chinese, though 'the
Chinese scale has but five notes. The modern
European system of Music dates from the 17th century ;
but was there no Music before the 17th century? Of
course there was ; there was Music whenever a sound-
vibration harmonised with a nerve-vibration. And the
‘imaginative energy ’ will use its own products as subject
when it can do without sound vibrations, and no sooner.
Now where are we 1]
Reproduction of already existent fact and form
is but one of the accidents of mundane art. j.
prion, any given combination of straight line
with curved is likely to be the copyright of som
alreadyexistingThing. ‘Thoseaccursed ancients
complains the writer, ‘have stolen all my bes
points. The artist and le bon Dieu have had the
same ideas— le bon Dieu had them first. Bot
artist minds—earthly and Cosmic—are emphati
cally creative, and there is incidental plagiarism
between them. It is a Pre-arranged Harmony
nothing more, and indicates neither the fact no
the desirability of imitativeness. In music, th
Composer has had the idea in advance of th
Creator.
The products of the imagination when function
ing in relation to the Faoulty (whatever
that may be once more) are Art.
Imagination is the imprimatur of art. It may
have a material basis or not, but when it has it is
not the material, but what imagination bodiei
forth from it, that constitutes Art. It is concernec
with 1 reduction, not with Re-produotion. Thai;
in certain spheres it can only re-produce is due to
the completeness of production in them. The
Iliing that Exists is an incident, not an essential.
That the idea of Art’s being necessarily either
irue or ‘Moral’ or ‘Universal’ is utterly
foreign to this ooncept goes without saying. Art
stands out of relation with all faculties except
one It owes no horn .ge to either the intellect or
the Moral Sense, save a shifting degree of obliga-
tion to avoid such intellectual repugnance as
would debar a free exercise of the HS«thetio
■Faculty. Some coherence in art work is neces-
sary for the manifestations of its merits. Im-
pressionism too pronounced in painting and in
literature, and musio too avowedly of the future
.®ri Vo 'iM blu^ ed anfl no .n-®atl>etio trace behind!
Ihe L hreo Musketeers' is not wholly reprehensible
intellectually. It hangs together just sufficiently
tor its brilliance to become manifest. To the
question How Much Intellect is wanted in Art
the answer is that the demand is purely subjective,
and differs with each spectator. One man will
see nothing but beauties in ‘East Lynne’ or the
Divine Comedy ’; the judgement of another will
be outraged to the extent of strangling all satisfac-
tion.
Recent economic speculations have familiarised
us with the idea that where there is no Want
there is no Utility—that Utility is not an intrinsic
quality but a relation. So in Aesthetics, we must
be prepared for the conception that Art-merit
depends on the satisfactory fulfilment of a rela-
tion with Faculty. A steam whistle churning
out melodies in the midst of Infinite Space would
be mechanism only. Not till the cherubs and
seraphs came along would it be music. Where
there is no appreciation there is no Art.
P.S.— A local celebrity in these parts can ‘dog-
howl with singular effectiveness, and impart a
most depressing human mournfulness to the
canine yelp. It is Nature raised to the manth
power par excellence. But it is not Music.
[‘Compton’rashly proceeds to build up, after having
pulled down, and we learn that Art, is the product of the
imagination when functioning in relation to a;. This is
very profound—a: is always profound—hut it doesn't help
the anxious enquirer too much. ‘Compton’ seems to
hold that Art is ‘ Nature ’ (and occasionally something else)
* raised to the manlh power.’ Now, what else is there ?
Admit his contention that Man is subject and Nature
object, Man can’t spin his Art out of himself, like old
Jack Robertson’s 1 hical telegraph-wire spider. ‘Comp-
ton pins his fond faith to Musio—in other words, to
sound-vibrations raised to the manth power — selected
and combined by the human brain. But the human
brain merely invented the combination ; it didn't in-
vent the vibrations. These were already existing in.
Nature: Man merely raised them to bis own power.
Obviously the sound-vibrations are external to the
musician ; if they were not, a man stone-deaf might be
a master-composer. ‘Compton’ is really affirming the
local thesis without knowing it. As to the dog-howling
gentleman: it doesn’t follow that because Music is Nature
raised to the manth power, therefore Nature raised to
tit ? manth power is Music. And, finally, that remark
about the relation of Art to Madame N. and Monsieur M.
was never given as a definition—only as a suggestion.

Agnes Ben bam writes—
Your note on Edward Maitland (March 19) will bea
supplement. The candid investigating spirit which di
tinguished the earlier Maitland was characteristic of hi
to the end, but many years before he died hi3 menta
position had entirely changed, and the agnostic author o
‘ The Pilgrim and the Shrine ' wrote in tones of unfalte
ing certainty upon the themes he once thought it im-
possible to arbitrate upon. Kindred souls still find
worth while to f dlow Maitland’s curious researehe
into the mystic Borderland between Flesh and Spiri
His language is always particularly lucid, and his styl
masterly. In the preface to ‘The Soul, and how it fount
me,’ ne says, with a brief touch cf sarcasm, that ‘ nowa
days most people are not only wholly undeveloped as t
the inner regions of their nature, but are proud so to lie.
That little book, the record of strange personal experi
ences, was the prelude to his ‘magnum opus,’ ‘Th
Perfect Way,’ which he wrote in conjunction with Anna
KingSiord. This book is wholly concerned with ‘ th
supreme questions, Whence come we ? What are we
Whither go we?’ and is profoundly mystical, yet no
unintelligible to minds accustomed to wander in simila
fields of thought. To those on more ordinary levels the
appendices should appeal as beautiful expressions o
poetry in prose form. The following verses describe the
state of exalted consciousness and spiritual sympathy
experienced by all poetic souls in moments of rapl
ecstasy—
The personality of the poet is divine; and, being divine,
it hath no limits.
He is supreme and ubiquitous in consciousness: his heart
beats in every element.
The pulses of all the infinite Deep of Heaven vibrate in
his own : and, responding to their strength and their
plenitude, he feels more intensely than other men.
Not merely he sees and examines tlies- rocks and trees;
these variable waters and these glittering peaks:
Not merely he hears this plaintive wind, these rolling
peals: °
But he is all these: and with them—nay, in them—he
rejoices and weeps, and shines and aspires, and sighs
and thunders.
And when he sings, it is not he—the man—whose voice
is heard: it is the voice of all the manifold Nature
herself.
In his verse the Sunshine laughs; the Mountains give
forth their sonorous echoes ; the swifs Lightnings
flash. "
The great continual oadence of universal Life moves and
bpcotnes articulate in human language.’
This tmy be transcendentalism ; but it expresses phases
of emotion sufficiently common in human experience to
be accepted (figuratively at least) by most of us. Eien
Harriet Martineau, a particularly level-headed writerof the
last generation, leaves upon record the ecstasy she ex-
perienced when, looking on the glorious prospect from
a mountain-top, she realised her oneness with the Uni-
verse. But though emotion may bring through sym-
pathy a sense of kinship, intellectual processes are less
easily communicable, and it is to be feared that the bulk
of Hie Perfect Way ’ will remain caviare to the general.
Spiritual knowledge requires to be apprehended by spiri-
tual faculties, and these tatter are. little esteemed or cul-
tivated nowadays. Should anyone feel tempted, how-
ever, to asoertain how much of mystical teaching ma y be
received by the intellect alone, he will find nothing to
offend him in the clear English and scholarly style which
remained to the very last characteristic of Edw. Maitland;
and he may find much to attract.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Not language, but laughter, was given man
to conceal his thoughts. We mask every emotion
with a grin. Man is the laughing animal because
he has so much to hide.
A clever woman knows when modesty is the
best policy. A foolish one forgets that her dispo-
sition should match her complexion with even
nore accuracy than her dress.
“It is my firm belief that in fifty—say a
hundred —years priests and parsons, in common
with other fortune-tellers, will be prosecuted under
the Vagrancy Acts.” —“A Drama in Muslin.”
Fuzz: “How about kissing the court-
Bible ? When five of the filthiest vagrants,
before Melb. City Court lately, asked to be
allowed to 4 kiss the buke ’ and swear away their
souls, the ordinary court-Bible was gingerly handed
to them and it was kissed by the lips of the whole
gang. All in court shuddered, and those who had
not done so before (‘ me too ’) vowed to take the
Scotch oath in future, or blow out a wax-match,
or cut off a rooster’s head. To give an idea of the
loathsomeness of those five creatures, it was in
evidence that one of their number habitually used
their living-room as a —oh, well! let’s change the
subject. ,J
The Song of the Linemen.
O, it’s out in the West, on the plains so wide,
That a man is an ant on the farther side,
Where the winds are hot, and the earth is dry,
And the sunset flames in a blood-red sky ;
Where there’s never a soul on the way to be met,
And there’s drought and hunger and cold and wet
From end to end of the country-side—
That’s where the telegraph linemen ride.
O, it’s up and away while the sun’s abed,
There’s a cross or a break in the lines ahead ;
There’s a fault to find, or a leak to mend,
There’s a mate to be met at the other end ;
There are floods to brave, and there’s horses to
drown
When they pass the word that the lines are
down.
O, it’s up in the heart of the desolate North,
Where the tyrant Sun goes fiercely forth,
Where the cyclone whistles in fiendish glee,
And the floods come down like a mighty sea.
In the thick scrub-ways, where the lawyer clings,
In the dark and the damp, where the tangle springs,
Where the wild blacks lurk and the saurians hide—
That’s where the Telegraph linemen ride.
O, it’s up and away while the Sun’s abed,
There’s a cross or a break in the lines ahead,
There’s a fault to find, or a leak to mend,
There’s a mate to meet at the other end ;
There are floods to brave, and there’s horses to
drown,
When they pass the word that the lines are down.
That’s where the telegraph linemen go
From early morn till the sun is low,
Thro’ the scrubs and gullies, the hills among,
With the clinking tools in their saddles hung,
Under the wires when the great gaunt poles
Sing the sorrows of saddened souls—
Of men who strayed from the tracks and died—
That’s where the telegraph linemen ride.
O it’s up and away while the sun’s a-bed,
There’s a cross or a break in the lines ahead,
There’s a fault to find, or a leak to mend,
There’s a mate to be met at the other end ;
There are floods to brave, and there’s horses to
drown,
When they pass the word that the lines are down.
Q.
FRANCIS KENNA.
From the Siding.
When the sun has been hot on the sand,
And the day has been weary and long,
And the last load is dumped at the siding,
And the team doesn’t want any guiding—
Then the crickets chirp up with a song
And a sweetness comes over the land.
And the plover comes over the plain
With a flap and a check and a run,
While the cool, scented breath of the hollow
Seems to call to the twilight to follow,
And to whisper 44 good-bye ” to the sun;
As he dips in the west once again.
And it’s Oh ! for the turn of the track
And the next hill, but one, to our own,
Where there’s someone who waits for the rattle
Of the waggon ard harness and cattle,
And the clang of the wheel on the stone
As I pass by the way, coming back.
There’s an eye like an emu’s to gleam
With the flash and the flame of a star
When it catches a glimpse thro’ the mallee
On the opposite side of the valley,
Where the great golden wattle-trees are,
Of a man coming home with a team.
NIL.
“In Dreams They Sang.”
When stars are clear
And Night’s great kindly light is beating near,
P rom Vaulted Deep the voices leap and flood the
dreaming ear.
An airy rhyme,
The wings of Music softly beating time,
A falling sweet of fairy feet that trip to cherub
chime.
A battle-song
That rings and rolls the serried hills along,
Mid thunder-peal of giant-heel that grinds the
throat of Wrong.
A gentle theme,
From tender voice a-tune where love-lamps
gleam ...
I would to God my dreams were Life, or Life
were all a dream !
Q.
P. LUFTIG.

Queensland.”

SATURDAY, MAY 7, 1898.
The Fat Man’s Capital.
The struggle of the aggregate Fat Man of
Australia to secure the Federal capital for
his own province is one of the curious side-
issues of the Commonwealth movement. To
Fat Man, the selection of his city as the seat
of Government means much hard cash, and
the Stout Personage is pursuing, that cash
with murder in his eye and a desperate
resolve in his soul. The trouble over the
Federal city lies mostly in New South Wales,
for a number of perfectly obvious reasons.
Hobart, Perth and Adelaide were practically
out of the running from the start, none of
their provinces having sufficient influence to
stand any reasonable chance of grabbing the
coveted distinction. Then Sydney claimed
the right against Melbourne, as the older
city, and the one which smelt a shade better,
and the original capital of the original pro-
vince, and the place with the Beautiful Har-
bor, and the political centre of the State with
the largest population, and for a number of
other reasons. The fact that the Conven-
tion did not definitely locate the capital
in Sydney or its immediate vicinity
was the point at whicli the Corn-
stalk Fat Man and the Federal cause
parted company. Yet the expectation that
the Convention would definitely fix the
capital anywhere was wildly ridiculous, inas-
much as it is impossible to say, till the popu-
lar vote is taken next June, which provinces
are coming into the league and which are not,
.and if the Convention had fixed the capital
in a State which ultimately stood out of the
union, the Commonwealth would find itself
in the curious situation of having its poli-
tical centre in foreign territory. The only
possible course was the one adopted— to wait
till it was decided what States the Common-
wealth was to consist of, and be guided by
the result. But the Cornstalk capitalist
ignored everything in his eager reaching out
for the profits, and as New South Wales was
the only province which came within measur-
able distance of grabbing the coveted dis-
tinction, it is also the only one which is
bitterly disappointed at its failure.
* * * *
To the Lean Man it matters practically
nothing whether his city becomes the Federal
centre or not. But if the capital were
planted in Melbourne, or Adelaide, or
Sydney, or any existing city, the local owner
of dirt and bricks and mortar would profit
materially. The presence of the Governor-
General, and the Federal offices, and the
Federal Parliament, and the hangers-on of
all these institutions, would probably, in a
few years, mean an addition of 40,000 people
to the normal population. This would mean
that the great multitude of empty houses
would be occupied, and when the demand
for house-room once more got ahead of the
supply the Fat Man who owned the premises
would be in a position to raise the rent all
round on the Lean Man who occupied them.
With the increase of rents the value of
houses and allotments would increase also.
The capital value of lands and houses in
Sydney and its suburbs alone has gone down
no less than £22,000.000 in the last four
years—beginning with the year when the
decline of population first commenced. The
capital value of Melbourne and its suburbs
has declined about as heavily since the year
when the slump in population originally set
in. Assuming that the establishment of the
Commonwealth’s political centre in either of
these cities meant 40,000 extra population—
which, though mere guess-work, doesn’t
seem an unreasonable guess—it would re-
pair, and more than repair—the downfall in
either city. It would make the population,
and the consequent demand for houses and
allotments, bigger than it was even in the
glittersome days that preceded the deluge.
It would mean a temporary resurrection of
the boom—and the Fat Man reaches out
after it for all he is worth.
• • * •
The idea of the Fat Man of New South
Wales is to make it a hard-and-fast condi-
tion that the Federal city shall be either in
Sydney or its immediate vicinity, or, at the
very least, in some definite place in New
South Wales where lie can buy up land in
view of the rise ; and, failing that concession,
that his province should stand out. In this
r spect his position is unique. If either
South Australia, Tasmania, or Westralia
attempted to make such a condition the short
and simple answer would be that they could
stand out and be not very much missed.
Even the defection of Victoria, though a
very great disaster, would leave a compara-
tively small gap in the acreage of the Com-
monwealth. But without New South Wales,
a large province and the most populous of the
group, I ederation would be a very lame and
apologetic affair indeed. That province
comes nearer to being absolutely essential
than any other, and for that reason it
is in the best position to make terms.
It is also, by reason of the 80,000 mini-
mum vote required to assure the acceptance
of the Constitution, the one where opposi-
tion will most easily turn the scale, or again
it is in the best position to make terms.
And the Fat Man has, in Sydney alone,
£22,000,000 worth of good personal reasons
for desiring the people of New South Wales
to make these particular terms, and no
others. As he can’t well state these reasons,
the railway question and the control of
©stem rivers serve as plausible excuses ;
also all manner of Fat Men who were never
before suspected of democratic tendencies
are bitterly grieved because the Federal
Senate is based on the equal representation
of the States, and thus violates somewhat
the sacred principle (which no Fat Man was
ever troubled about before) of government
by the majority. But there is no actual
cash in any of these things, and there is
much cash in the question of locating the
Federal city in Sydney or its immediate
vicinity. And The Bulletin uncharitably
guesses that Fat Man is thinking about the
cash which he doesn’t mention a great deal
more than about the sacred democratic
principle which he does.
* • * *
If the present Bill is rejected there is
always a bare possibility of getting together
another Convention, and making the location
of the capital in Sydney or its vicinity an
absolute condition without which N.S. Wales
will not come into the union. There is also
a chance of leaving out of the next Draft
Bui that troublesome clause which declares
that the capital must not be State territory,
and which thus practically shuts out all the
existing cities from the coveted honor and
the loot. As matters are now there is no
absolute certainty that the centre of the
Commonwealth will be in New South Wales-
and if it is there must be a practically new
city built on ground tvliich belongs to the
\ ederal State, and where the State will reap
the profits-not built in the place where it
will help to enhance the value of the Fat
Man s property, and thus enable him to raise
the Lean Man’s rent. Therefore, the position
of Fat Man is perfectly intelligible. He is
sticking closely to the principles by which ho
became Fat. But why certain sections of
the Lean Man community should aid him in
this matter is wholly past understanding.
If the Federal centre was fixed in Sydney
the consequent influx of population would
boom up land values and rents to the old
magnificent figure—and the great aggregate
Lean Man would have to pay these rents? or
a great part of them. There is no guarantee,
after the first influx of population had sub-
sided, and the new capital had found its new
level, that there would be any permanent
increase of work or wages. The influx
wouldn’t be caused by increased manufac-
tures or increased foreign commerce, which
do create an active demand for labor : and
in any case, experience doesn’t that
wages are any higher or the proportion of
unemployed any less m a city of 500,000
people than m one of 400,000. The Lean
Mans share of the benefit would probably
be a half-share in the empty honor of having
the capital in his province ; the Fat Man
would get the other half of the empty honor
and practically all the cash. Therefore,
when the Fat Man calls on him to protest
because the possession of the Federal capital
isn’t assured to his particular city, his logical
answer should be that the capital isn’t his
r, i t r j i ,
funeral. In short, when the Tory who has
many milhons at stake asks the Democrat
who has no money at stake to stand by him
on purely democratic principles, the latter
should regard his sudden ally’s unwonted
anxiety about democratic principles with
grave suspicion.
Coghlan’s Federal Nightmare.
N.S.W. Statistician Coghlan has issued a
definite pronouncement on the financial aspect
of the Federation Bill, and his decision is that
four States (Victoria, S.A., Tasmania and Wes-
tralia) must “ go broke ” under the proposed
system unless the alleged surplus of N.S.W. is
confiscated and divided among them. As
Coghlan himself puts it, the other States can
only “ be saved at the expense of N.S.W., who
will bear the whole burthen of Federation, and
pay for the relief of the other States from
interprovincial duties.” This gloomy prognosti-
cation has created great joy in the camp of the
Little Cornstalk Party, and has gladdened the
Cabbage-Tree Mob beyond expression. And
yet, despite Coghlan’s reputation as a man of
figures, The Bulletin has not the least hesita-
tion in saying that, in this case, his inferences
are wrong and his assumptions too thin, and
that lie arrives by way of an empty theory at a
conclusion that has nothing in it.
• • • •
The Federal financial difficulty begins with
this preliminary trouble. The federating pro-
vinces will hand over to the Commonwealth
their right to levy customs and excise, and
their postal and telegraph revenue, &c. By
this means they surrender, among them,
£7,500,004 of annual income. They will also
be relieved by the Commonwealth of expendi-
ture to the amount of £3,002,650 per annum,
and things will stand as follows (Coghlan’s
figures):
On the surface of things this looks quite simple
and easy of settlement—but it isn’t. For, in
the first place, part of the above revenue arises
from interprovincial duties, and as they are to
be abolished under Federation the States will
give up millions of revenue, but the Com-
monwealth won’t receive 7i millions. There-
fore, the Commonwealth, if it proposes to make
up the loss on interprovincial duties by extra
foreign duties, will have to pile up its tariff
very high. Secondly, the Commonwealth has
to make the tariff uniform in all the States,
and (for five years, at all events) an ac-
count is to be kept of each province’s
share of the revenue, and its correct proportion
of the expenditure, and each province is to get
back its own exact fraction of the surplus.
And if the Federal tariff is made so high that
-Tasmania, which has more than twice as large
a customs revenue per head as N.S.W., can
pay its share of the Federal expenses out of its
own customs and excise receipts, and yet get
back £294,000 a year to keep its finances
square, then N.S.W. will get back no less than
.£2,310,000 a year instead of the £1,057,000
which, so it is alleged, is all the province re-
quires. And as a further trouble, it is just
possible that no tariff which could be invented
would raise enough revenue (after the loss of
the interprovincial duties) to enable Tasmania
to pay its share of the Federal expenditure
out of customs and excise, which Cogh-
lan apparently assumes it must do, and
get back the above-mentioned £294,000. For
a oO per cent, tariff often so far reduces
imports as to yield less than a 30 per cent, one,
and a 500 per cent, tariff is apt to yield nothing
fit fill,
* * * *
Therefore Coghlan’s inference is that the
small provinces, being unable by any imagin-
able lederal tariff to raise enough (after the
loss of the interprovincial duties) to pay their
share of the Federal expenses, and to cover
their local expenses as well, must either default
°V™ ir P ublic (lebt > or else seize the alleged
JN.k.W. surplus to divide among them. And
I hr bui.LKTiN s theory is : (1) That there is no
earthly reason why the Federal expenses should
come out of customs and excise at all ; (2)
that there is no need either to default or
to confiscate the alleged N.S.W. surplus; (3)
that there is no N.S.W. surplus to confiscate,
in any case.
• * * •
Ihe N.S.W. Statistician starts by the as-
sumption, which may or may not be correct,
: 6ral Government will require
£l,/oU,OOO for its own purposes. Then he
assumes, which is altogether incorrect, that:
The Braddon clause of the Bill fixes the customs and
excise rex enue at four times the sum taken by the Govern-
wfonnmii °' v " P ur .P os ? s - FoUr times *1,760,000 are
.i(i,!)0(),000, and this is the minimum sum which the
nnirpH l ! J ajru'f raise to s?Wfe the revenue re-
qin red bj the Central Government.
As a nutter of fact, however, under the
Braddon Blot/ the customs and excise
revenue may be twice or three times or 100
times the amount taken by the Federal Govern-
ment for its own purposes. All that the famous
Blot specifies is that not more than one-fourth
of the customs and excise revenue may betaken
for Federal purposes—it may be any amount
less, however. Therefore, if the Federal ex-
penditure is £1,750,000 a year, the Federal
Government may collect £6,900,000 customs
and excise, and keep £1,750,000 and return
£5,150,000 to the States. Or it may collect
£5,000,000 by a comparatively small tariff, and
return it all, and pay its own expenses out of
direct taxation. Or it may collect £6,000,000
and keep one million, or balf-a-million, or £5,
and return the balance, and pay the rest of its
expenses by direct taxation. * In short, the
States require 4| millions returned to them out
of the customs and excise. That amount can
be returned out of a £5,000,000 tariff, and yet
leave half-a-million towards the Federal ex-
penses, the balance being raised by direct taxa-
tion. And £5,000,000 can be raised with ease,
despite the abolition of interprovincial duties.'
* * * *
( Coghlan’s next point is that the Federal
Government can’t raise money by direct taxa-
tion, for these reasons:—
That any additional direct taxation can be imposed it
would be absurd to suppose. In Tasmania, income-taxes
run from Bd. to Is. ; in Victoria, from 4d. to Is. Id. • in
-Australia, from 4J*>d. to Is. Land-taxes exist in
all the provinces save Western Australia, and their exten-
sion for Federal purposes would be impossible. With a
revenue of £7,000,000 and a surplus of £5,260,000, the
portion coming: to no province except New South Wales
would suffice for its needs, and the other four States
would inevitably come to financial ruin unless the
Federal Government face the situation and levy land, in-
come or property taxes for Federal purposes in addition
to such taxes as already exist.
This off-hand method of settling the question
isn’t evidence, however; it is only the slam-
ming down of a thin assertion which is of no
earthly account. Westralia can impose a land-
tax. Victoria can certainly stand as heavy a
land-tax as N.S.W., which allows room for a
large increase. Hie S.A. tax can likewise
bear levelling up to the N.S.W. standard, or
something near it. At present there is no beer
or tobacco excise either in Tasmania or W.A.
There is no dividend tax in S.A., Westralia
or \ ictoria, and S.A. is the only province
with either a graduated land-tax or an
absentee-tax. There is also no income-tax,
as in Britain, on interest paid to foreign bond-
holders. And Cog hi. an himself shows, in his
“ Seven Colonies,” that Maoriland contrives to
pay about ss. per head per annum more in
direct taxation than the most heavily-taxed of
the Australias, which knocks the alleged im-
possibility on the head considerably. If Vic-
toria chose to levy direct taxation on the Maori-
land basis it could raise another £300,000
a year or even more. Further, Coghlan
swells his Federal expenditure by insisting
that the Commonwealth shall immediately add
£322,000 a year to the military expenditure,
and there is no reason why it should be dead cer-
tain toadd any such sum till things improve. He
leaves out such large possibilities as a 4 percent,
totalisator-tax, a tax on foreign insurance com-
panies, and a saving of anything up to a million
per annum by the conversion of the public
debt—a conversion to even so high a rate as 3|
per cent, would save Tasmania no less than
£50,000 a year and Victoria about £300,000.
Also, he ignores the fact that the trouble all
arises through the abolition of interprovincial
duties ; even if the provinces can’t pay more
direct taxes now, which is bald assertion rest-
ing on no evidence, yet when under Federation
they pay £600,000 a year less in interpro-
vincial duties they will be able to pay
£600,000 more direct taxation with the money.
The Coghlan theory seems to be that, though
they will save a vast amount in interprovincial
duties, they won’t be able to pay anything else
with the money.
* • * »
At all events, if the other provinces, when
the readjustment comes five years after the be-
ginning of the Federal tariff, do annex N.S. W.’s
alleged surplus of £1,253,000, it will be
N.S.W.’s own fault. Only by a wholly dis-
honest system of book-keeping will there be
any such apparent surplus to seize. The pro-
vince is making its accounts square this year
by charging about £BOO,OOO of unproductive
expenditure to loans, and it has also had a
pretty regular deficit, by Coghlan’s own
figures, for a number of years. If its financiers
like to turn honest and pay their way, there
will be no surplus worth mentioning for any-
body to annex, supposing anybody should desire
to annex it. And, in any case, the difficulty is
a good seven years ahead, and seven years
hence things will almost certainly be so much
changed that Coghlan won’t know his own
bad dreams of the year ’9B. If that gentleman
likes to look at things as they were seven years
back, and build an estimate on them as to how
things ought to be but aren’t this year, the re-
sult would be rather astonishing.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
That Con mission.
The Government of N.S.W.—which is mostly
either hostile or lukewarm on the Federal
movement —has appointed a commission con-
sisting of I)r. M‘Laurin, Bruce Smith, and
J. Russell French to consider what effect
Federation will have on the N.S.W. finances.
The Bulletin is anxious to help the Commis-
sion all it can, and, as the first essential is a
clear understanding of the points which have
to be considered before a decision can be arrived
at, it suggests the following questions. The
answers, when carefully thought out and
summed up, will enable the Commission to say
clearly what effect Federation will have on the
cash condit ion of N.S.W., or any other place
(1) What kind of men will form the Commonwealth
Parliament, and, being that kind of men, what kind of
tariff will they naturally produce, and how much will it
yield at first and afterwards, and also later than after-
wards ?
(2) What effect will that tariff have in increasing or
diminishing or diverting the foreign trade of the Com-
monwealth ? . .
(3) What effect will the abolition of interprovmcial
duties have on the same trade?
(4) Will the Commonwealth take over and convert the
public debt, and, if so, what rate of interest will it pa,\,
and how much will it save? When will it do the conver


sion, and will the money market be disturbed at the
time, and, if so, how much, and will the Treasurer at the
time of the conversion make a good job of it, or will he
be an incapable who doesn’t know when to come out of
the wet ?
(5) Will the Commonwealth take over the railways ; if
so, when, and on what terms, and will it also take over
the work of railway construction, and, if it does, what
policy will it follow about a uniform gauge and other
things?
(6) When will war come this way, and will the Common-
wealth win or not, and, if it wins', would the unfederated
provinces have lost, and, if it is licked, how much more
badly would they have been licked, and how much extra
would the performance have cost?
(7) How liiuih will the Federal Government cost, and
how much will be saved on the cost of the Provincial
Governments to make up for it?
(8) Where will the capital be, and what effect will its
being there have on trade and manufactures and on every-
thing else ?
(9) Will the Federal Government be Protectionist,
rreetrade, or Revenue-Tariffist ? Will it pay its own
expenses out of customs and excise, or out of some other
(and what other) form of taxation ? How fast will the
revenue from postal, telegraph and other services in-
crease, so as to help to pay the cost of the Federal
Government ? How much of the increase will be owing
to Federation? What effect will Federation have on
manufactures, agriculture, the railway revenue, land-tax,
income-tax, land revenue, etc. ?
(10) W hat kind of men will be in charge of the various
Provincial Governments, and how will they run the local
finances?
Having considered all these questions, and
answered them all correctly, and summed up
''l? 6 -, a oawers so as to show exactly where
N.S.W. will be at, say, one and five and ten
years after the beginning of Federation, the
next question naturally is :
(11) bat would have happened in the same period of
years if the provinces hadn’t federated ? How would
trade, agriculture, manufactures, and revenue and ex-
penditure have expanded or contracted? What altera-
tions would there have been in the tariffs? How much
would interest rates on the public debt have increased
or diminished? How would the deficits hare got on?
Also, how would everything have got on ?
Y\ hen Reid’s Commission has decided these
things also, and resolved exactly where N.S.
Y\ ales would be financially a certain number of
years hence if it didn't federate, and subtracted
that from where it would be if it did federate,
then it will be able to present a coherent
report. But if it can’t decide these things,
then it is out on a fool’s errand. The one thing
that is absolutely sure is that no one knows for
certain what will happen to the finances if we
do federate, and no one knows a whit better
what will happen to them if we don’t.
The Man Who Died.
James Crotty, of Melbourne, died recently
in the odour of sanctity and affluence, and
a great part of his pile goes the lamentable
way that so much good legal tender has gone.
According to the terms of his will, £lO,OOO of
t rotty’s fortune are left to pile up insensate
brieks-and-mortar at St. Patrick’s unfinished
cathedral, and about another ,£lOO,OOO goes to
the R.C. church for “ religious and charitable
purposes.” The rich man who wants to do
some good after his death, or to get a big ad-
vertisement, or to put a large fire-insurance on
his soul, generally goes this same ungrateful and
melancholy road. He either leaves his pile to
build a big, useless, desolate church for cold dog-
mas to blow through on winter nights; or he dif-
fuses the kind of higher education which consists
in teaching dead languages in a college ; or he
helps some creed to spread its particular brand
of theory about hell, and the old saints who are
dead, and the patriarchs who are dust, and the
Israelites who are very much defunct. That
same £llO,OOO would have carried the Bairns-
dale railway on to Bruthen, or taken the Hope-
toun line a good way towards Mildura, and
either undertaking would have supplied a lot of
work to people who greatly need it, and enabled
other people to find homes in what is now a
wilderness, and permanently enriched the pro-
vince. The same pile would have made 150
miles of road in some place where it is much
required, or irrigated a big stretch of the
immera desert, and thus supplied employ-
ment and enriched the country ; or it would
have lifted off a considerable fraction of the
national deficit—in fact, there are a score of
practical ways in which it might have bene-
fitted the community at large. And most of
these ways would have given the late James
Crotty a far bigger advertisement than the
stereotyped device of helping to build a church
and giving a pot of money to an already
bloated creed, because almost every rich man
goes in for endowing bloated creeds, or build-
ing churches or statues or drinking fountains,
or something equally commonplace. The
two impersonal things to which every
rich man owes a debt of gratitude are
the Church and the State. The Church
tells him about perdition during his life-
time, and as a rule he pays it for doing so.
Jhe State protects his life and his pile, and
runs down the malefactor who gets at him udth
a bad cheque, and safeguards him by police
and soldiery, and does its best by sanitary laws
to keep the fatal microbe off him. But for it
some one would promptly rend his fortune
from him, and leave him with nothing to bestow
on the Church or on anything else. He pays
the State for its services also, but the State
gives him much more tangible value for the
money and often gets less of it. Yet, while the
world is full of grateful old men who leave
their pile to the Church, the grateful millionaire
who tries to give the State a lift with his coin
when he has no more use for it himself is never
heard of. Ihe country is full of dismal churches
—tenanted mostly by the four cold w’inds, and
the moonshine, and the rats —which were built
by the rich ancient who wanted to do good and
didn’t know how. As for the b,it of railway or
the s: retch of new road or the irrigation
channel which he bequeathed to his beloved
country--well, where is it?
Byrnes’s “ Policy.”
Queensland Premier Byrnes’ statement of his
“ policy,” i i lrs recent “ great ” speech at War-
wick, reads very beautifully when summarised
by wire in the southern papers. The Ace’s
cahn, business-like report (which is quite serious,
and not the wild joke of the dog-fight reporter,
as might be supposed) reads thus :
The Cjovei'client would deal with the Queensland
National Bank in a spirit of candor, fearlessness, and jus-
tice. At the s. me time, they would afford the bank, as
now constitul d, all legitimate aid possible. Although
they were all alive to the - importance of a progressive
policy'of public works, they could not, in this stage of
the life of the present Parliament, be fairly asked to com-
mit themselves to any extensive scheme. At the same
time, when the right time arrived, no one would be more
willing than he to give his voice and vote in favor
of a steady, definite policy of expansion. The finances
of the province would be administered with caution,
prudence and economy, but without unnecessary
parsimony. Regarding Federation, he said no doubt
the movement in favor of Australian political unit3’
had considerably increased in volume and importance dur-
ing the past few years, and a definite issue on the subject
would shortly be submitted to the electors of five other
provinces ; but the attitude of Queensland must largely
depend upon the result of the forthcoming Referendum in
N.S.W. If IST. S.VV. decided against the Commonwealth
Bill it would he idle for Queensland to trouble itself fur-
ther on the question until the subject was revived again.
If, on the other hand, N.S.W. decided in favor of that
measure, then the subject of Federation must become a
real live and burning one in theprovince. And if the other
provinces decided to unite upon the lines of the Com-
monwealth Bill, the Parliament and people of Queensland
must be asked, within a reasonable time thereafter,
to express their views on the measure. Refer-
ring to the contemplated changes in the Consti-
tution, if Federation were agreed on, it would
be necessary to alter the present electoral system ; but
until the federation question was settled any proposal to
alter the constitution would be premature. On the
separation question the Government would not in any
way depart from the policy of its predecessors in regard
to the partition of the province. Still, they would view
• favorably any feasible scheme whereby there might be
some further decentralisation of administration intro-
duced into the public departments. Queensland was to
some extent committed to the system of contributing to
the Australian Auxiliary Squadron; but he did not
believe in its underlying principle, as at the end of the
period the provinces were left without either ships or
trained men in return for the money expended. The
Government would aid in placing Queensland meat on
the home, foreign and southern markets.
The meat question is the only one on which
Premier Byrnes gives a straight expression of
opinion. Otherwise his view is that the
new Government believes it ought to stand up
horizontally in a recumbent, perpendicular
attitude at an angle of 43 degrees to the hori-
zon, without in any way disagreeing with the
views of those who hold that it should be side-
ways and upside-down. Also, it feels it to be
its duty to spend large sums of money with
lavish economy, and it is resolved to stand still
with rapid velocity and be suddenly stationary
at great speed, and in the end, when it falls up
a tree and is suffocated, it intends doing its
duty just the same to the bitter end.
The Casual Person with a Gun.
“Hermes” on semi-amateur soldiery :
Apropos Yankee Consul Bull's brag about the stupend-
ous hordes of ’Murkan militia, it is interesting to note
that, according to history, volunteers and "militia gener-
ally crumple up like a bacon-rasher before trained regu-
lars in the open field.
The mighty hordes of Persia were defeated at Marathon
by a numerically insignificant army of Greek hoplites.
The universal success of the Roman meant simply discip-
line against the lack thereof, and when the Goth took
also to regular military training the Roman went under.
Norman discipline defeated the bull-dog courage of the
half-trained Saxons at Hastings and Senlac ; and so on,
right down the roll.
The wise people, however, who work hard at dollar-
making all the week, and only consider military matters
with the fag-end of their intellects, calmly remark that
as long as a man can shoot straight and ride well he is a
match for any “ regular.” And the local historian rises
to remark that ’Murkan militia drove the British out of
the States in the War of Independence ; and that French
volunteers walloped German regulars at Valmy; that
Spanish volunteers and guerillas gave the French a
bad time in the Peninsula; that more 'Murkan militia
dealt out defeat to British veterans at New Orleans, in
1814 ; also, that Boer irregulars played the devil with
Tommy Atkins at Laing’s Nek and Amajuba Hill.
But the American militia did not drive the British Lion
out of the States. That much-abused animal was hauled
out of the States, as he was at the Transvaal, by his own
Parliament, and, though the New England militia was a
first-class territorial force that had fought against the
French, and had had as much practical training as the
best German regiment of to-day, it went down smash
at Bunker’s Hill, Long Island, White Plains, Brooklyn,
and a score of other places. In short, hut for the sup-
port of the French under Rociiambeau, the ’Murkans
would probably have gone under to a mixed force of
British regulars and German mercenaries, the fighting
strength of which at no time exceeded 9000 ! Also, the
12,000 militia who repulsed Pakenham’s 0000 British at
New Orleans were impregnably posted against a force
without artillery, scaling-ladders, or fascines, and sent
forward to certain destruction. In the same war, 4500
British veterans under Ross utterly smashed up 9000
militia strongly posted at Bladenshurg, and then marched
where they liked, till 6400 more militia came along
to be walloped, and walloped they were, to some
purpose. Ross then attacked 15,000 more defending
Baltimore, and drove them into Baltimore with terrific
slaughter, getting killed himself in the process.
If, when the time comes, Australian partially-paid troops
cannot do better than these three militia bodies did it is
utterly impossible for them to do worse.
At Vaux again, previous to Valmy, 1500 Prussian
hussars gave 10,000 French levies a licking that taught
Ddmouriez to keep his raw troops behind entrenchments.
And Valmy itself was won by regiments of the old
monarchial army and National Guard levies—behind en-
trenchments.
As for the boasted Spanish guerillas, Napier, says they
were more trouble to the English then the French. The
only Spanish success was when Castanos, with a mixed
force of 30,000 Spanish regulars and volunteers, defeated
less than half the number of French, while as for Sara-
gossa it was a strong position held by 40,000 Spanish
mixed troops against only 16,500 French regulars till
three-fourths of the defenders were dead. A desperate
defence, truly, but one that again proved the superiority
of regulars over irregulars.
The Boers were seasoned men. Years of war with the
Kaffirs had made excellent hill-soldiers of them, but they
never took a fortified position from the British. At
Laing’s Nek the Boers had superior numbers in a position
of great natural strength ; and at Amajuba Hill it was
the fire of 2000 well-sheltered Boers upon 600 British in
an exposed position, without artillery and with rifles use-
less from sighted for the sea-level. A half-trained
militia force is merely a mob of well-meaning persons
who flap around, blaze away aimlessly, forget the bugle-
calls, retreat when they should advance, charge machine-
guns, and get blown to glory when they should be drink-
ing tea a few miles to the rear. The veteran regular is a
machine. Ten thousand of him hit a blow like one man ;
and work out the plan of their general just as the limbs
obey the brain of the skilful boxer.
That “ Indissoluble ” Union.
“ C.R.” writes from Melbourne in defence of
the Lyne-Want-Hancock theory that the
Federal Union, as provided in the Convention
Bill, is indissoluble :
I notice that you are in favor of “ taking the Federal
Constitution as it is and doing needful repairs after-
wards.” Will you let me point out an error into which
you have very naturally fallen as to the power of amend-
ment. You say all that is required is to amend the word
“ indissoluble” in the preamble of the Bill, and then to
have another amendment declaring the union dissolved.
Apart from other objections to such a course —objections
which would require a detailed argument—let me call
your attention to the fact that the power of amendment
does not apply to the preamble at all. For section 127,
the final section, applies merely to alterations “ of the
Constitution and “ the Constitution ” does not begin
until section IX. of the Bill “ IX. —The Constitution of
the Commonwealth shall be as follows : The Constitution.
This Constitution is divided as follows : Chapter 1. —The
Parliament,” &c. I feel sure that you will not refuse
to withdraw a statement which is calculated to mislead
many of your admirers.
The Bulletin, for one, would be very glad if
the union was indissoluble—but, unluckily,
“ C.R.’s ” theory doesn’t help the matter in any
way. The Convention Bill provides that the
Constitution can be amended by the consent of
the two Federal Houses, of a majority of the
people voting at a national referendum, and
the assent of three out of five States. If this
only applies to that part of the Bill which
begins at clause IX. and is headed “the Con-
stitution ” then the previous portion of the Bill
—the part with the “ indissoluble ” in it—is
free from these elaborate restrictions, and
becomes a common, ordinary law, which can be
amended by the Federal Parliament without
the popular vote, and the majority of States,
and the other frills. It stands on the same
plane as a provincial Act of Parliament, which
doesn’t contain any specific authority for its
own repeal, but which can be repealed, all the
same, whenever the people feel that way.
In any case, the people cannot make any
law that they cannot unmake again. “In-
dissoluble ” is only a word of 12 letters.
The people put it into the Bill. The people, if
they want to, will take it out just as quietly.
There are 3,000,000 people in the five pro-
vinces now, and there will be 30,000,000 by-
and-bye. And the idea of 30,000,000 people,
with the control of the Parliamentary machine
in their own hands, being kept in permanent
subjection by two “ i’s,” two “ s’s,” a couple of
“ l’s,” and a scattering of other letters may be
good parchment law, but it is mighty poor
human nature.
A Study in Bigamy.
An interesting bigamy case came before the
London criminal court recently. The prisoners
at the bar were a man and wife, who married
in 1884, and, finding their union a mistake,
parted three months later on friendly terms.
After a lapse of seven years they both agreed
to get married again. Experience had given
each of them a good idea as to the kind of mate
they wanted, and the new, unholy alliance
gave pleasure to all. “ Circumstances con-
nected with an inquest ” brought the 1884 mar-
riage to light, and some aggressive purist or
other sooled the police on to prosecute the
honest bigamists. Judge Hawkins, who tried
the case, practically commended the prisoners’
action in dissolving their unsuccessful partner-
ship of 14 years ago, “ when there was neither
cruelty to justify a judicial separation, nor
misconduct to warrant a divorce,” and, as
regards the prosecuton for bigamy, “it
seemed to him (Hawkins) that they might have
been left quietly alone.” The Judge declined
to punish them, and they went back to their
respective bigamies with easy minds. In these
occasional acts of unlawful charity Judges
admit the foolishness of adapting marriage laws
to religious prejudice. Bigamy is not bound to
be a worldly sin, but it is always a heinous
olfence in the eyes of a parson, and through
the influence of church superstition and hypo-
crisy it has been ruled a legal offence under
any circumstances. The same church power
that opposes a rational divorce system — i.e.,
divorce by mutual consent —insists upon a pro-
secution for bigamy in a case where the
offenders have been forced into “crime” by
their own sense of decency. The married
couple aforesaid merely divorced themselves in
a reasonable and thoroughly respectable man-
ner, and were, therefore, prohibited from any
further lawful marriage. They might have
made themselves eligible for divorce in 1884 by
one of them committing acts of drunkenness,
cruelty and adultery, and then they could have
lawfully married again. As it is, they are pun-
ished for not committing those sins ; their mar-
riages are illegal and they are for ever rendered
incapable of legitimatising their children. Even
if the adulterous husband were to formally smite
his wife with a meat-axe (which is cruelty) she
could not now sue him for divorce, seeing that
he has connived at her bigamous adultery, and
they have both been guilty of collusion. All
these difficulties are religious creations. The
Queen’s Proctor is a relic of clerical control
over the pair “ whom God has joined together,”
and though they may be a pair of atheists,
with no sort of belief in the Heaven where their
marriage is alleged to have been made, they are
still bound together by a Law that blushes to
own its submission to ancient Papal doctrines.
Ox the N.S.W. State-school-fees question
(N.S.W. is a province where the politicians
subsidise the newspapers with free postage,
but can’t afford to subsidise poor parents with
free education unless they publicly declare
themselves paupers) :
Dear Bulletin. —Re State school-teachers paying up
arrears of scholars’fees rather than risk a departmental
“ wigging ” for not collecting same. They have grown
wiser of late. (Teachers get at loggerheads with parents
quite often enough without having “ fees ” as a bone of
contention.) The cry of “ pauperdom ” raised over the
“ exemption from payment ” regulation, has lost its sting
and the number of “ exempts ” is steadily increasing.
The parent merely asks for a “ form ” and fills it up. In
a big school the teacher knows nothing of the applicant’s
circumstances, and accepts his assurance that he cannot
pay. In small places the teacher has more sense than to
offend the parent by practically calling him a liar, even
though he knows the applicant spends more than the
weekly fees in beer every day. The teacher finds, also,
that filling up a form every three months is less trouble
than rendering weekly accounts and sending in monthly
reports. In cases where the arrears mount up till a
special report is asked for the teacher has to decide
whether he will earn undying local enmity, by recom-
mending prosecution, or perjure his pitiful soul by
reporting the debtor as in indigent circumstances and
recommending that the debt be wiped off. Perjury wins.
Free education is “ granted ” now—it should be a birth-
right.—P.

During the recent hearing, by N.S.W. Full
Court, of a habeas corpus application, certain
circumstances came to light over which their
Honors were moved unto much extra-judicial
pronouncement. The 17-year-old girl (who ap-
peared in court “ attired in an elaborate cos-
tume ”), of whom her mother was seeking to
recover custody, turned out, upon private
examination by the Judges themselves, to be
unwilling to forsake her alleged betrayer, and,
though the horror with which their Honors
referred to the injury inflicted upon “ this
child ” was possibly a little unusual, the
seduction of the young nurse - girl by her
employer in his own house is un-
doubtedly a repulsively - gross feature of
the case. From Chief-Justice Darley’s
opinion, indeed, that a seducer under such
conditions “ ought to be in gaol ” The Bulle-
tin in nowise dissents. But when upon that
opinion is founded the dictum that the “ age of
consent” should here be raised (otherwise than
as between master and servant, or teacher and
pupil) to the English figure of 18, this paper
has once more to call attention to the fact that
the Australian 10 - year - old damsel (as the
Judges, from their criminal-court experience,
really ought to know) is, on the average, fully
as mature as the English girl of 18. Indeed,
there are strong doubts whether, if a young
woman isn’t adult enough for “consent” at
this degree of development, the statutory “age”
might not just as well be raised to 28—or to
80 ! One Australian girl may,, of course, be
more or less developed than another at any
given age, but their Honors-must be much more
“ unspotted from the world ” than said world
gives them credit for if they don’t know that
in her dealings with the other sex the ordinary
16-year Australienne is perfectly well aware how
many beans make five.

N.S.W. politics :
They must bear in mind that the Federation they were
asked to enter was an indissoluble Federation.— Mac-
Laurin, M.L.C.
It is a east-iron constitution.—Jack Want, M.L.C.
He laid stress upon the fact that it was strictly pro-
vided in the Convention Bill that the principle of equal
representation in the Senate should never be amended.
It was utterly impossihle. It would be for ever out of the
power of the people to effect a change. —Blanksby,
M.L.C.
The unanimity of the M.L.C. crowd against
the Convention Bill is truly touching. Also,
its utter denseness is something pathetic. For
all of it, including the pioi James Blanky,
M.L.C., has presumably read Clause 127,
which states distinctly that the Bill may be
amended by a majority of both houses, a
majority vote of the people, and the assent of
three States out of five. Therefore the people
can, if they please, amend the word “ indis-
soluble ” out of the Constitution, and they
having that power the Constitution can’t be
indissoluble, or cast-iron, or any of the other
large, whirling adjectives. Also the Bill doesn’t
say that the principle of equal representation
in the Senate shall never be altered—that
statement is absolutely aud purely the inven-
tion or the bungle of James Blanky, M.L.C.
\\ hat it does say is that no State shall have
its proportionate representation reduced with-
out its own consent. Some day, of course, the
country may get tired of thi3 arrangement,
and decide by a majority, as described above,
to so amend the Constitution that the repre-
sentation of a State can be altered without its
consent—so even this proviso isn’t fixed for
ever. The really amazing foolishness of the
tale told by James Blanky and the other two
M’sL.C. lies in the assumption by a presumably
sane individual that anything can be fixed for
ever—that any remarks printed on a piece of
paper can bind unborn generations for all time.
Surely even so painfully ordinary a person as
Blanky, M.L.C., knows better than that!
“ Scribe ” remonstrates with The Bulletin :
Your correspondent who wrote under the title “Terewth
versus Truth ” talked through his neck about the Melb.
dailies conspiring to suppress the case of the Salvationist
recently charged with “ an abominable and unnatural
horror.” I was the reporter for one of the morning
papers when the case came before the City Court, and, on
my own responsibility, I simply neglected the ease as I
would all such cases unless under special conditions.
During the two years I have done police-court work, fully
a dozen of these cases have cropped up, yet I have only
given two even a mention. One of these two was of pub-
lic interest, because the prisoner was a man who had
made a claim to a big estate of £100,000; the other
because the P.M. ordered a discharge of the prisoner and
the arrest of two witnesses for blackmail. “ Prisoner was
found guilty, and duly sentenced for his crime, but
day’s papers were dumb about the unspeakable
iniquity of the convict.” This is utterly wrong ; all three
Melb. dailies published the case among the “ Found
Guilties ” and the sentence —cut down, of course, to the
bare statement “ William Kennedy, 29, attempted un-
natural offence, nine months imprisonment.” Regarding
the correctness of the other assertions; (1) “ The boy who
appeared in the case was one of a number held in the
Salvation reformatory.” Incorrect. The boy was a com-
plete stranger accosted in the street the night of the
offence by the prisoner. (2) “ Other Salvationist officers
appeared in court for the defence,” etc. Misleading.
Evidence of previous good character was given by one
man ; but, on the other hand, after the evidence at the
City Court, the four Salvationists who had gone bail for
Kennedy dropped him like a hot potato, and he
had to stay in gaol from committal to trial. Finally,
there were three other cases of the same sort heard
at the same sittings and all four were treated
in exactly the same manner by the dailies.
The religion of none of the offenders was published,
and there is no reason why the Salvationist should
have his religion labelled on him when he trans-
gresses any more than any other variety of believer.
Only one man in every 10,000 has the pluck to announce
himself as possessing absolutely no religion, so practically
every offender brings a certain amount of indefinite dis-
grace on some form of superstition. Yet nobody writes
long slabs to The Bulletin accusing the dailies of sup-
pression and conspiracy, and pressmen of bribery and
blackmail because they omit to say that Doolan the bur-
glar was a Roman Catholic, or M'Tavish the garotter a
Presbyterian, or Smith the sneak-thief an Anglican.
[A long and feeble effort to get away from
the point. Salvationist Kennedy was the man
appointed by a religious organisation to direct
the morals of a Boys’ Reformatory, and if his
unnatural offence wasn’t a “ special condition ”
in the eyes of “ Scribe,” it is just about time
that that “responsible” reporter took advice
from his editor re the reporting of horrors.—
Ed. B.]
Alfred Deakin got considerably wet in the
good cause of Federation, at Melbourne, last
Friday. The polite and scholarly Deakin had
just finished a Federal oration when the voice of
Maloney, M.L. A., was heard calling him a liar,
or something like that. Deakin smiled with lofty
scorn, and refused to take any notice of the
“creature,” whereupon Maloney danced with
rage, and asked Alfred to come out and be shot in
a horrid, beastly duel, and then fired a glass of
water into Deakin’s countenance. The rest of the
meeting was mostly hair and scrum and execra-
tion, and a dripping Deakin trying vainly to be
heard above the tumult, and the voice of Maloney
offering to fight him with axes or any other kind
of weapon.
Courthouses, schools, and other public
buildings in N.S. Wales, will be used for record-
ing votes at the Referendum in June. Why
should not such buildings be regularly used for all
Parliamentary elections? Thev are centrally
situated, everybody in the neighborhood knows
the way in and out of them, and they are spacious
enough. In most parts of Europe school-houses
and courthouses are used for these purposes, and
in the Swiss cantons even the churches are pressed
into service. The expense of afterwards setting
the places in order would be only a tithe of that
which the State now incurs in erecting and pulling
down ramshackle and unsatisfactory polling-
booths.
