The “ Idler,” which has been absent from
Sydney long enough to be nearly forgotten, is the
Cartwright Co.’s present effort at Sydney Cri. As
a literary effort the “ Idler ” is a work of high
rank. There is no oppressively wicked individual ;
the villain is just as wicked as any average person
would be if he had the needful inducement. He
skirmishes after his friend’s wife —just as any man
would do, if his enemy’s wife, or the wife of his
casual acquaintance, wasn’t more attractive. And
he strikes a snag which has the advantage of some
novelty. John Harding, in certain bad young
days long before the opening of the play—days
when he and others drank hard and shot liberally
in Golden Gully—had killed Simeon Strong’s
brother, and Simeon’s life is saved by Mark Cross,
and Mark Cross wants to bolt with Harding’s
young wife. Therefore when Simeon finds his
enemy he is persuaded to promise in writing that
he will bury the hatchet 100 fathoms deep by way
of repayment to the man who has saved his life ;
and Cross agrees to swap the letter of release for
Mrs. Harding’s body and soul, the same to be
personally delivered on his premises at 10 p.m.
And then, having parted with the document,
the ladys body and soul are not delivered
after all. That is where the pathos comes
in. There was an agreement that she was to
fall for value received, as the price of her hus-
band’s safety, and, with the dishonesty of her sex,
she refuses to fall after all. Miss Beatrice Lamb,
the comely, motherly lady of the co., is, possibly,
a trifle too placid in the act where she comes to
collect the price and just gets away without
delivering the goods, but in a general way she may
be said to give satisfaction. Cartwright is silent
and subdued and stony in places, but in his best
scene—the one where he persuades the bitter
Ammurikin gentleman to sell his vengeance—he
rises to the occasion just when there is a feeling
that he is going to fall into utter flatness.
Thereafter he goes strong to the end and drops
the petrified stare of the earlier scenes, but in
these early scenes he goes very slow indeed.
Arthur Styan is just a respectable, ordinary per-
son in evening dress and with his hair nicely done ;
and Charles Thursby, as Simeon Strong, calls up
recollections of Dot Boucicault, and suffers some-
what by the comparison. His American accent is
better than Boucicault’s when he has it, but he
drops it so often, and picks it up again so
irregularly, that even his remarkably good acting
Sets obliterated in the struggle at times. Miss
is a charming old lady through-
out, and Story Gofton does good work in a small
character which doesn’t matter much. So far,
the “ Idler ” seems to have brought luck to the
Cartwright co., and it should be good for a pro-
fitable season. Something more might be made
of the scene where the friend’s wife comes to hand
herself over, at 10 p.m. sharp, in exchange for the
valuable letter, but all the same it is a powerful
scene—strong enough to float even a bad play.
And the “Idler” isn’t a bad play, by many
thousand miles
»* * *
That dismal tale of ye olden tyme, “ Vir-
ginius,” didn’t eventuate at Her Majesty’s, after
all. The haggard father in the brief petticoat
who should have cursed Appius Claudius, or some-
thing like that, all down the Sacred-street, or some
such locality, stands postponed. Also the his-
toric river which any active person could throw a
dog across, if he had a dog which he didn’t want,'
still reposes in the property-room. “Hamlet’’
boomed with so much bigger a boom than was
anticipated that he was extended from night to
night, till at last the leggy Latin parent who
should have shrieked for his daughter was crowded
out altogether. Possibly he wasn’t much of a
loss, and, in any case, “ Hamlet ” is a distinct gain.
At time of writing it is understood that the Ghost
stalks on the walls of Elsinore for the last time on
Tuesday night, and the ensuing programme is a
treble bill: “The Color-Sergeant,’’ “A Clerical
Error,” and “ Chatterton.” The Bulletin looks
on all treble bills with an eye of gloom, as an
attempt to make three afternoon bun-teas do in
place of a dinner, and reckons that it might be a
lot better to keep on extending “ Hamlet.” Any-
how, “ Ben-My-Chree ” is promised for Saturday.
* * se *
Every night, at Sydney Lyceum, a big
audience bursts its weskit buttons cheering the
American admiral in the “ White Squadron,” and
its hat, which gets blown out from under the seat
by the breath of popular approval, is stamped
upon by innumerable feet in the confusion. The
American admiral amounts to very little more
than a casual figure-head in the drama, and why
the audience should roar at him in this fashion
E asses understanding. Anyhow, he seems to be
eeping the play going. The present writer tried
one night to get up a counter-demonstration in
favor of Spain—partly because it is the smaller
dog of the two—partly because the old superstition
is more picturesque than the bright new godless
dollar—partly because Spain is of the old Holy
Roman faith, and the Holy Roman faith is the
only one which ever possessed a pleasant smell of
incense, or a stained-glass window that was really
worth looking at—partly because the world owes
a debt of gratitude to the country which never
produced a patent medicine or a hair-restorer,
and where Talmage wasn’t born. But his
protest on behalf of the restful, moss-grown land
didn’t even begin to make itself heard amid the
riot, and he went down badly. Yet the country
which did its best to give the world a rest by
Talmage not being born there deserves better
treatment. And, seeing that bruisers Corbett and
Sullivan have volunteered on the American side,
and the Spaniards are doing some wretchedly bad
shooting, yet the best shooting they know how, at
Sullivan and Corbett, and seeing how highly
advisable it is that the party which is shooting at
Corbett and Sullivan should be encouraged, the
state of mind which Sydney has got into is highly
ridiculous. The “ Shamrock and the Rose ”is the
joint vegetable which is to ensue on Saturday.
** * *
“A Life of Pleasure,” which is remem-
bered in Sydney as a drama with much gas and
glitter and lost girl and pleasant sin in it, is to
appear at Sydney Royal on Saturday “ for a few
nights only.” Despite the utter irrelevance of
the great battle in Burmah, in which the nigger
lies down dead in neat brown rows as a tribute to
British valor, the “ Life of Pleasure ” has always
been a reliable stand-by, and it should be a blessed
relief after the hero in a pot hat. who comes back
and tells how the lunatic asylum was burnt in
America.

The advt. states that their performance must be

A benefit concert for Edgar Straus, the
popular ’cellist, whose portrait appears in this
issue, is fixed for the 11th
inst., at Sydney Town Hall.
All Sydney concert - goers
know Straus, by ear at all
events, and all who enjoy his
acquaintance esteem him
highly. The talented and
kindly young musician is
now prostrated by illness,
and has been compelled to
give up the practice of his
profession for many months
past. Under these circum-
stances, his fellow-artists
propose to give the above-
mentioned concert for his
benefit; and they ask the
music loving public to come,
and get excellent value for
their money, and have the
soul-filling consciousness that
they are doing a kindly deed
thrown in absolutely gratis.
A very large number of seats
has already been taken.

The Sydney Amateur Orchestral Society,
conducted by the enthusiastic Italian, Hazon, will
play at the Town Hall next Wednesday night.
It is the only body of its kind in Australia, and
its conductor—an impulsive, talented beater of
the stick has made a fine orchestra out of Sydney
amateur talent.
.** * *
Melbourne’s musical fraternity, which is
partly sincere and largely otherwise, rallied in
some force at the Town Hall last Saturday after-
noon, to the first Marshall Hall Orchestra concert
of the season. Two Hungarian Dances (Brahms),
the overture to “The Ship of the Fiend”
(M Cunn) and Beethoven’s “ Eroica ” Symphony
made up a soulful and not too long programme,
the conductor’s own little piece of “ Alcestis ”
composition being held over. The audience, un-
less they were artful dissimulators, derived a calm,
superior joy from this hard-working and quite
admirable entertainment. Beethoven, who is apt
to provoke fits of coughing at times, kept them
perfectly quiet for an hour, and Mac’s rolling,
drumsome overture was encored for several popular
reasons. One obvious reason was its terrific
ending in a bang of cymbals that measured
three feet in diameter, or thereabouts. M‘Cunn’s
overture may not be spiritually “great” music
(the writer hasn t looked-up the question
in a musical dictionary), but it is pretty cer-
tain to appear again and get further encores.
Consequently a protest against its moral argu-
ment had better get lodged right away. “ The
bmp of the Fiend ’ is the story of a wife’s marine
elopement with a young man purporting to be her
first lover. As a matter of fiction, he is the D ... 1,
and when he has taken his victim out to sea—far
beyond the reach of scandal—he knocks a hole
through the vessel with his cloven hoof—it is
easy to stamp a hole through the bottoms
of some vessels—and the drum-artist, at the
rear of the orchestra, bangs a brazen death-
knell as aforesaid. The story is interest-
ing, but it sounds absurdly rough on the
lady, and loaves no sort of opening for the Shiels
Divorce Act to come in. What the world really
wants is a great musical representation of a divorce-
suit, with lots of co-respondents, and a fine con-
flict of evidence leading up to a three-fourths ver-
dict for the petitioner.
* * * *
In the evening, the Town Hall was packed
full of shilling-* 1 poppers,” and Phil Newbury’s
venture gave promise of continuing for many
Saturday nights hereafter. The concert co. on
this occasion included Phil Newbury, who sang
his delightful best, Madame Spada, and three
other vocalists, more or less acceptable. The
most acceptable of these three was Bonynge
Kelly, a gentle baritone, lately heard in
Maritama, at the Bijou. Also, the re-
munerative W. E. Nott knocked some respect-
able melody from the municipal organ, and
Hattenbach celloed in a fetching strain and
Benno Scherek played pianoforte accompaniments
like the that he is. Viewed from the
Shilling Pop ” standpoint the inaugural concert
was a well-deserved success. An interval of five
minutes only divided Part 1 from Part 2, and the
various “ turns ” chased one another up and down
stairs on brisk business principles. The time
when a Town Hall concert consisted mainly of
funereal silence would seem to have gone for ever.

The Kellino Family arrived at Melbourne
Opera House on Saturday, when the reputation
they had brought from Sydney and foreign parts
was endorsed by their new admirers. The Craggs,
of yore, gave an astonishing show, and the Frantz
Family took Melbourne by storm. Popular taste
in acrobatics has been shaken up considerably
since the rising generation began to talk, there-
fore the Kellinos were called upon to do some-
thing very bright. They did it in all directions.
The joint and several achievements of those eight
strangers are a feast of surprises which leaves a.
EDGAR STRAUS, the well-known Sydney ’cellist, whose benefit
is fixed for Lith May, at Sydney Town Hall.
sweet taste in the month. They introduce new
effects, picturesque and humorous, and, after a
drop-scene falls on their last act, they take a
ca jl ’* n front; a further call for the author
would seem only a reasonable compliment to
somebody s brain-work. The construction of the
fvellino show is almost worthy of the performance,
and the performance is a credit to all concerned.

The Queenslanders have risen to the
occasion in regard to their brilliant young country-
woman, Florence Schmidt. She has sung to
crowded houses at Toowoomba and Ipswich.
Horn there she went to Maryborough, to fulfil an
en l> a K e ui6nt, and sang to a packed house. Thence
Bundaberg, where there was a record house,
and she had an enthusiastic reception. Warwick
next, hundreds turned away. Here Miss Schmidt
sang nine times, and even then the audience
wouldn’t go away until she had sung “ Killarney.”
Brisbane the following night, and a packed house,
lownsvilleites were giving her a garden party at
latest dates. When she descends upon Rock-
hampton, no doubt that swampy town will rise to
the full height of its responsibilities in having
given birth to a real queen of song. Altogether,
the plucky Queenslander has reason to oongratu :
late herself, as the Continong, Paris, Mark-aisy,
operas, bouquets, dollars, bicycles, carriages and
Sairs, and perhaps princes, to say nothing of
ukes, counts, etc., are now within measureable
distanoe.

Clever S. JE. Sime pictures in Lond. Pick-Mb-Up Chas.
Fulton’s idea of Julius Caesar, as given in Beerbohm
Tree’s recent version of Shakespeare's play. Anybody of
the least acquaintance with the gentleman who overcame
the Nervii cannot doubt that at last he sees the
only genuine J.C. as he lived and walked and carried his
eagle-beak around. Note the thick lip-rim to the purse-
mouth ; the lines of thought (and other things) beneath
the eye-pouch; and the general “Well, young man?”
air of the shrewd, craning physiognomy. There is a
Hebrew financier in Sydney who always takes nine
months’ credit on an unesc-apable debt and 5 per cent,
discount for this tardy “ cash,” and knocks off the odd
pence “ to save the bank hoi her,” and makes the creditor
shout on top of all. Then when he has “borrowed” a
cigar and toddled away chuckling, he looks something
like Sime’s “Julius Caesar’ in a silk hat and black
trousers. Here is a standing pattern for Great Men.

Buffalo Bill is going to hack the Spanish Cubans, if he
doesn't got macheted. His show was one of the most
distinctive sensations ever known in London, and excited
boundless wonder in Paris and Berlin. At Rome he
pitched on the Campagna, in sight of the Coliseum. The
Pope invited his Sioux Indians up to the Sistine Chapel,
where they attended a service, with Michael Angelo’s
“Last Judgment” and ceiling frescoes looking down
upon them.

Dear B.—Come to think it out, the “ immortal Wil-
liams” fell on his nose when he made Hamlet talk about
“ the undiscovered country from whose bourne no
traveller returns,’’ because Hamlet had only just had
with such a traveller an interview upon which the whole
circus hinges. The old man’s ghost has scarcely had
t ime to get outside a saveloy and a pint of beer behind
the scenes ere his son assures us that ghosts never turn
up under any circumstances. It is not altogether un-
pleasant to catch William napping; in fact, I own to a
sinister delight in the conviction that if ordinary scribes
were to perpetrate some of the bosh that is lauded as
Shakespearian they wouldn’t be considered good epmigh
even for Melbourne Paunch. H’m !

nated address) whereon the employes depose what a

Melb. : An In Memoriam of “ Dear Old Greville ”
appears in a morning paper 29th April, fourth anniver-
sary of his demise. The tribute is from “A Melbournite,
who loved him as a daughter.” Harwood’s departure,
and miserable funeral, punctuates it. Greville had a
good, hearty funeral. While the Boom Bust dabbed
Harwood, Coppin and Stewart on the occiputs, Greville
came out triumphant. And then he died—as the Bible
says. His name was John Rogers, but he doffed it, not
to clash with G. H.
Prior to taking to the stage, Miss Eugenie Duggan
(Holloway and Anderson’s leading girl), was a teacher in
a Melb. R.O. school. Her brother, Pat Duggan, was at
that time in Vic. Civil Service, and had he remained
there would now have been secretary to the Lands dept,
with a salary of £6OO. But Pat got badly stage-struck
through his connection with the Rosoian Shakesperian
Society, and went to America to “busk” for his living.
Marcus Valerius Claudian Superbus (with his eyes up-
turned to Heaven) : “ What is this faith ? Much would I
give to know!” A moment after, to unfortunate scene-
shifter : “ What the all-fired dashed sheol do you mean
by shifting that set like that? Dash me if you dashed
fools don’t deliberately try to ruin every dashed scene !’

The WILDCAT
COLUMN
Tasmania’s revenue for April was .£78,053,
which is £9945 better than the returns for April, 1897. In
the four months of the Tasmanian financial year that have
expired the revenue has improved £35,088, as compared
with the first four months of 1897. Of the six Australian
provinces, Raspberry Land, in proportion to its size, is
making by far the best showing in a financial sense. It
has, compared with population, the biggest surplus, and
the biggest revenue increase, and it is paying off its old
deficit faster than any other province. It must also be
remembered to Fysh’s credit that, bad as Tas. finance
still is, he has of late years greatly reduced the expendi-
ture of loan money on unproductive works, as well as the
total loan expenditure ; and if he liked to brace up and
pay for all unproductive public works out of revenue he
has now the money to do it with. Also, Fysh has brains
enough to do it—if he only has the necessary grit as well.
* * * *
The N.S.W. revenue boomed last month—total
receipts, £947,585, as against £823,030 for April, 1897, an
increase of £124,555. The month’s expenditure, however,
was £1,018,707, so the deficit increased by £71,122. Ten
months of the financial year are now gone, and, so far,
the deficit is £387,393. Still, this is £187,461 less shortage
than there was at the same date last year, and consider-
ing the large hunk of land-sale money to come in at the
end of June it begins to look as if Premier Reid might
have his first surplus this year—at least the only objection
to calling it a surplus is that about £BOO,OOO of the year’s
ordinary expenditure is charged to loans so as to keep the
visible outgoings inside the receipts, and the repairs to
the roofs of museums and such things are called “ per-
manent and reproductive works ” to keep the comforting
fiction afloat.
N.S.W. loan expenditure, for the 10 months of
the financial year that have already elapsed, is £1,387,379,
which is £154,816 more than the amount which was
blewed on the alleged “permanent and reproductive
work ”in the same period last year. That extra £154,816
charged to loans fully accounts for the fact that the ordi-
nary expenditure is 169,906 less than it was last year. In
the past 10 months the Reid Government has borrowed
£2,873,319, which is a very large and unhealthy borrow
indeed.
* * * *
Melbourne City Council contemplates buying-
out the three local electric-lighting co’s., and going in for
eleetric-lighting on a comprehensive scale as a municipal
undertaking. To do the thing properly, however, it is
necessary to get the co-operation of the other munici-
palities, for the cheapest and most effective process is to
have one big electric system covering the whole metro-
politan district, and belonging solely to the people. Alel-
bourne wants a County Council which will boss the whole
district from Brighton and Heidelberg to Braybrook and
Paseoe Vale, and absorb all the miserable little “ cities ”
into Melbourne, (and consolidate the debts, and fire-out
the superfluous mayors, and start things afresh.
• * • *
The balance-sheet of the Colonial Mutual Life
Assurance Society for 1897 runs into big figures, and the
net result is only moderately reassuring. Premiums are
£311,400, which is about £4OOO better than last year.
Total income is £397,012, which is also about £4OOO more
than last year. The amount saved and added to the funds
is £112,369, which is quite as good as could be expected.
The last six years’ results have been :
Premiums. Total Income. °
1892 .. ... £312,494 £392,609 £150,236
1893 .. .. 308,729 389,756 139,704
1894 .. _. 305,530 392,483 131,328
1895 .. ... 303,98: 393,686 130,207
1896 .. ... 307,693 392,705 117,137
1897 .. .. 311,400 397,012 112,370
These figures look remarkably prosperous, as times go,
and the funds now amount on paper to an imposing total
of £2,153,342.
Still, the Colonial Mutual’s balance-sheet bristles
with matters to which this paper desires to take excep-
tion. Expenses are a fraction over 20 per cent, on total
income and about 25J per cent, on premiums, both of
which are a shade higher than they were last year, though
they are better than the figures for 1893 or 1894. The
Colonial Alutual, this year, writes off £9129 for losses and
depreciation. This makes £22,429 written off to cover
depreciation and losses and wear and tear of property in
six years. Compared with the £79,000 written off by the
Alutual of Victoria, and the £51,206 (in five years) of the
Alutual Life Association, and the £125,000 provision made
for depreciation by the Aus. Widows’ Fund, all of which
are only about half the size of the Colonial Alutual, this
amount looks miserably inadequate. Also, of the
depreciation above referred to, £4611 is depreciation
of office property (“ office property ” is an item of about
£13,000, which presumably refers to the tables and
chairs, &c.). This leaves only £lB,OOO written in six
years (the worst six years on record) off £2,100,000
worth of property. The idea that this covers more
than a fraction of the drop in values, considering what
a unanimous drop there has been, especially in land
and buildings, where this institution is so heavily inter-
ested, passes belief. In 1895 the Colonial Alutual realised
£4 16s. per cent, on the nominal amount of its funds; in
’96 there was a great fall to £4 ss. ; in ’97 there is another
drop to £4 4s. —at least that is the office estimate,
but on the average funds it seems more like £4 Is.
The Alutual Life Association realises over £4 18s.
per cent, on its investments ; the Widows’ Fund, £4
16s. ; National Mutual, £4 17s. ; Maoriland State Life
Department, about £4 19s. ; A.M.P., about £5. The
discrepancy between these figures and those of the
Colonial Mutual is another item of presumptive
evidence that a lot of its alleged funds are merely
inflated boom-values which ought to be written
down and haven’t been. If it is assumed that assets,
when written down to their real value, should yield
£4 15s. per cent., and all the leading offices quoted
above yield more than that, then the Colonal Mutual
wants to write over £313,000 off its alleged funds to put
things on a sound basis.
The Colonial Mutual still builds its calculations
of future interest (on which calculations the solvency of
every life assurance office depends) on the assumption
that its funds will continue to yield 4 per cent., which,
seeing that the earnings are down to about £4 Is. per
cent., leaves a miserably narrow margin. The A.M.P.—
which earns about £5 per cent.—has reduced its future
estimates to ; the Mutual Life Association, which earns
£4 18s., is making reserves so as to reduce its future
estimates to 3J ; the Alaoriland State department, which
actually earns £4 195., has made reserves equal to a
reduction of its future estimates to £3 12s. 6d. per cent.
Compared with all these offices the Colonial Alutual has
an almost invisible margin of security, and even if it out
its assumed future interest down from 4 to 3 per cent, it
would still have a smaller margin than any of the three
offices mentioned above. And, so far as Tiib Bulletin
knows, not even a mention of any reduction in assumed
future interest has been made.
A rather large sum (.£17,085) of the Colonial
Mutual’s assets consist of “ rents and interests outstand-
ing and accrued.” The C.M. follows the old bad practice
of not showing separately the amount of property
acquired by foreclosure, and dumping together interest
accrued and interest in arrear. And in this matter Tub
Bulletin repeats the words it used with regard to last
year’s balance-sheet. “At the end of 1893 the Colonial
Alutual had the enormous sum of £55,743 interest and
rents outstanding and accrued. This certainly looked
as if a lot of the securities had gone bad to such an extent
that the interest was practically irrecoverable —in which
case the principal is often more or less irrecoverable also.
This was a far worse showing than that of any other large
assurance office in Australasia, and suggested that the
Colonial Alutual was in a worse state than any of
its leading contemporaries. The Bulletin drew
attention pointedly to these alarming figures,
and thereafter the item decreased so rapidly
that it . . . is only £16,178 (the latest figures are £17,085).
Then the Alildura trouble, and a small dispute withfthe
defunct Alercantile Finance, Trustees and Agency Co.
seemed to reveal, as plainly as possible, the astounding
circumstance that, on certain small advances, amounting
to only £31,000, the arrears of interest due to the Colonial
Mutual were no less than £12,750. Tiib Bulletin asked
that society (in its issues of Alay 9 and 23, 1896) if it really
meant the public to believe that, while there was £12,750
owing on £31,000 advances, the remaining £5OOO shown in
the balance-sheet covered all the rents and interest due
and accrued on the other £1,700,000 or so of investments.
This important question evoked no explanation. The
inference that many people will draw, in the absence of
explanation, is that the “ interest and rents, outstanding
and accrued,” as shown in the balance-sheet, are not the
whole of these items (the society, by the way, doesn’t say
that they are), and that the remainder are lumped in with
the advances. If that be really so, no one can tell how
much the remainder is, or how good or how bad things
maybe.”
This year J. F. Levien, M.L.A., who is pre-
sumably still the same Alildura ex-director, and the
gentleman who was in office at the time of the
advances made by the Col. Mutual on Alildura securities,
was re-elected as usual. It was alleged a year ago that in
the Colonial Alutual a proxy given by a policy-holder is
good, not merely for the election for which it was directly
given, but at all subsequent elections, and as often and as
long as the document will hold together. -It was also
alleged that when there was any serious attempt to get
in a new director, and thus get some fresh blood into the
institution, the effort could always be defeated by great
masses of new and middle-aged and old and very ancient
votes hauled out of the safe, so that the old Happy
Family could go right on without interference. Tiie
Bulletin doesn’t know about the truth of this, but it
again asks if it is true. But, somehow, most of the ques-
tions that The Bulletin asks are never answered. Per-
haps they are too beastly disrespectful. Anyhow, the
annual meeting was held the other day (there was no
time for any unpleasant comments on the balance-sheet
till it was over) and the old directors got in again as usual.

Colonial Sugar Refining Co. has a half-year’s
profit of £85,725, as compared with £85,301 and £85,473
for two previous half-years. The usual 10 per cent, divi-
dend absorbs £85,100, and the other £625, added to pre-
vious balance, leaves £89,946 at credit of profit and loss.
This is in addition to £329,012 of reserves and £1,702,000
paid-up capital, so the Colonial Sugar Refining Go. is in a
strong position, and has a decent crust to fall back upon
—if need be. The 10 per cent, dividend has now been
paid regularly for 7i years.
North Shore (Sydney) Ferry Co. half-year’s
profits to 31st Alarch, £3083; 10 per cent, dividend
absorbs £3000; small balance of £B3 to carry forward.
• * * •
The Royal Bank (Melb.) held its meeting the
other day, and the 3 per cent, dividend was declared, and
the directors proudly pointed out that the profits were
increasing so remarkably that the Royal was now earning
over 5 per cent., whereas two years ago it was only
making about 2£. They also stated that, as there was
over £4OOO at credit of profit and loss, they proposed
starting a new reserve fund. Whereupon a wild share-
holder demanded that they should divide all the profits,
and damn the future and the reserve fund, and said that
rather than bother about a reserve he would prefer to see
the Royal go into liquidation. For about a minute it
looked as if it was going to be formally moved that the
shareholders should go out and drink the whole bank on
the spot, but no one backed up the excited boomer, and
the storm subsided.

It is alleged on apparently good authority,
that: (1) The Alaoriland board of the A.AI.P. Society con-
sists of five directors, of whom three form a quorum ; (2)
that the chairman is brother-in-law to one director and
father-in-law to another, so that this family forms the
quorum ; (3) that when a directorship falls vacant, instead
of a new man being elected by local policy-holders, he is
appointed in Sydney on the nomination of the board, and,
as the above-mentioned family really forms the board, it
naturally does the nomination. If this is true it appar-
ently has the power to carefully select the sort of director
who won’t make trouble for the Happy Family. (4) It is
also alleged that one of the auditors is an of one
of the three directors above-mentioned, and if this is true
the further inference can’t be avoided that no mere em-
ploys can afford to audit his boss too severely—in fact, it is
difficult for him to be an independent auditor at all. (5)
It is further alleged that one of the biggest advances
is a loan on mortgage to a relative of one of the
three directors abovetnentioned. The loan is probably
made on good security, but, even if it is, the Happy
Family arrangement (if the alleged Happy Family
is a reality) casts suspicion (perhaps quite undeservedly)
on the transaction.
Apropos of the above, The Bulletin is making
inquiries about an insurance society (not the A.M.P. this
time) where it has reason to believe that one of the direc-
tors is an insolvent, and the board keeps its billet partly
by proxy votes and partly by the simple process of pre-
venting anv would-be director getting a sight of the list
of policy-holders, lest he should collect proxies himself, or
circularise the people interested about the doings of the
management, or otherwise render himself obnoxious.

Subscriber : Would feel inclined, if it were our
funeral, to sell out of the shipping co. you mention. The
other looks good enough to hold.
Judge Hood, of Melbourne, delivered an
astounding decision the other day. Walter Bullock was
a clerk in the dead and disgraceful Alercantile Bank. The
Mercantile held a lot of shares with a call liability on
them, and as it couldn’t legally hold them it did so in-
directly by putting them in Bullock’s name. At the
same time it gave him a guarantee that the bank would
indemnify him against loss. Of course Bullock didn’t
want to take a risk where there was no possible profit,
but if the boom-bank clerk didn’t take such chances on
his miserable salary the boom-bank could always get
another clerk who would. The Mercantile went broke ;
Bullock lost his billet ; he, poor wretch, was made in-
solvent and had his furniture and all his other goods
seized for calls on the shares ; and the Alercantile Bank
didn’t indemnify him as promised. And Judge Hood
decided that Bullock had no cause of action—also, that
the damage was too remote. And yet if Bullock had
owed the money to the bank instead of the bank to Bul-
lock the remoteness of the damage would only have been
a reason for adding on 7 per shent. per annum interest.
And if being ruined by the bank’s breach of contract and
losing everything he possessed in the world isn’t “cause
of action,” what is ?
• * • *
Rupert Clarke to the meeting of shareholders ;
“ My grandfather and my father each held a large stake
in the Colonial Bank. It gives me great pleasure to
assure you that it is my intention to take the same active
interest in the affairs of the bank, and to do all in my
power to promote its prosperity.” There’s a noble un-
selfishness in Clarke the Third’s determination to keep an
eye upon the bank which holds his large stake. The
Bulletin will henceforth think of him as a young man
who is prepared to make great sacrifices for the protec-
tion of his property.

Rap : “ It is not seven years ago since Crotty
(deceased), who has left, among other things, from
£lOO,OOO to £200,000 to the R.C. Church, asked the pre-
sent writer how he could raise £2OO on a thousand Mt.
Lyell shares at 50 per cent, per annum for three months.
A Hebrew to whom the matter was subsequently intro-
duced by another person declined to lend even £5O on the
security, whilst at that very moment he (the Hebrew)
was lending 75 per cent, on the market-value of scrip
(Roundhills), with which the present scribe’s study is now
metaphorically papered. No wonder Crotty didn’t leave
any of his dollars to the Synagogue.”

Perhaps the most serious sufferer by Crotty’s
death is a poor devil who was “ under a cloud ” in Aus-
tralia, and went to England relying on Crotty’s assistance
to float a big N. S. W. venture in which he (the p. devil) was
largely interested. Crotty’s influence was about to be
used on behalf of the clouded one when C. died. It is
odds on that the loss of opportunity to retrieve his good
name will result in Crotty’s prot6g6 joining Crotty at an
early date.

The winding up of the estate of Harrold Bros.,
the big Adelaide firm which exploded three years ago, has
now reached a final stage. Liabilities, £299,173 ss. 3d. ;
assets, £58,259 17s. lOd. ; deficiency, £240,913 7s. sd. The
winding-up proceedings have provided a nice little feast
for the lawyers, and the semi-final meeting last week
was devoted to worrying over the already-well-picked
bone. The estate, when finally wound up, will pay about
6d. in the £l.

The much-paragraphed illegal business of selling
the return halves of N.S.W. railway-tickets does not seem
a pleasant or profitable trade. One agent was fined £l5,
the other day, and another who could not pay a fine of
£2O got three months. Other prosecutions are incubating.
* * « •
Broken Hill Block 14 has had its first serious
“ creep,” or collapse of ground. The area affected is that
adjoining the Broken Hill Proprietary, and the collapse
is probably due to the weakening of the ground through a
fall which occurred in the latter mine some time ago.
Block 14 has always, since Zeb. Lane’s days (and that was
in the very early years of the mine) replaced the ex-
tracted ore by mullock, instead of relying wholly on the
timbering, and this policy has in the past had excellent
results, and has now prevented very' serious disaster. What
any particular “ creep ” means in loss of ore and outlay of
labor nobody can estimate, even roughly, ahead ; but this
one does not seem to be very' bad. Anyhow, work goes
on as usual at the concentrating mill, except that it is
worked two shifts instead of three ; and that is ascribed
not to the fall, but to the fact that the co. has lately pur-
chased some big lots of concentrates. Hitherto its pur-
chases have been confined to crude ore.

A great report comes from the Golconda at
Parkes, N.S.W. :—“The backs are opening up in a highly
satisfactory manner ; a new chute of stone has been found ;
the hanging-wall is beautiful working pick-and-gad
ground,” and the assurance is given that, if it continues
crushing, stuff can be got at a quick and cheap rate.
Well, stranger things have happened at Parkes, where
there is a quartz crushing and mining co. that has never
had a mill of any kind, and no quartz to crush if it had.

Lake George United Mines, N.S.W. : Someone
in Alelb. has worked out the cost to be 3s. a ton more than
the return —total charges, £1 Bs. 3d. per ton ; total real-
ised from a ton of ore, £1 ss. 2d. The month’s production
is valued at £8495 from 6750 tons of ore, running only
1.29 per cent, copper, about ljdwt. gold, and 2|oz. silver.
Cheap mining, with a vengeance 1 The Exploration co.
are great people for low-grade mines, but they cannot
like them as low-grade as all that. Some months ago
men supposed to be well up in copper-mining said these
shares were sure to go to £lO ; they are rapidly coming
down to 10s. When they were over £3, several small co’s
were floated, and there is in the camp the pathetic spec-
tacle of mines, whose shares can be bought at a few
shillings a dozen, hopelessly looking for a 1£ per cent,
copper lode, while there are in other parts of N.S.W. old
abandoned copper-mines whose waste dumps will go far
more than that.

Menzie’s Barossa, S.A.’s only big gold-mine,
cleaned up, the other day, 1500 tons for 30oz. ! The
crushing (the first one) knocked Adelaide mining men
kite high, but directors attribute it to the new plates, in-
experience, and sulphate of copper. The mine had been
much boomed by Alenzie and his partner. The latter
abandoned a press billet to become a “capitalist,” but
probably realises now that there’s more money in writing
about it.
After three months’ battle and persistent pump-
ing, the water is now 100 ft. below the 500 ft. level in the
Tasmania mine, and another big 18dwt. crushing has been
got out —value, £7992.

* • • •
The year ’9B bids fair to turn out the top Q.
output of sugar. The phenomenal rainy season that has
given Southern Queensland two or three floods in succes-
sion on the Southern rivers, has forced the cane to an un-
paralleled luxuriance, and the planters are scratching
their heads and asking themselves, in puzzled wonder,
where can the- cane possibly all get crushed. Also,
couldn’t a few hundred cheap Japanese be landed quietly
somewhere to tide over the labor difficulty. Last year
the cane crop on the Burnett was an almost total failure,
and folks said the sugar-lands were worked out. This
year the cane is so strong and plentiful that thousands of
tons must inevitably be wasted—for lack of crushing-
power.

posed to be blank country. This, if permanent, means
salvation to more than one lingering claim to the east of
the magic horseshoe.

W.A. Market Trust, Ltd.” A later cable says Bottomley
is confident of a recovery, more failures are reported, and
the market is still disturbed. This is as pathetic for
Bottomley as last week’s Bulletin was prophetic about
him. It was one of his schemes to bring about some sort
of an amalgamation between the Auxiliary and Southern
Associated and the well-known Associated Alines of Kal-
goorlie—mines alike in name, but very unlike in value.
In fact, the founders of the Associated group do not hesi-
tate in describing Bottomley’s mines as “cats,” and lost
no time in publishing the failure of the attempt to amal-
gamate. Notlrng is said of the fate of the Northern
“Terrors,” though the chances are they too have been
jumped on, and that the Whittaker-Wright crowd has
done some of the jumping. During the last 10 days all
men who know Kaufmann have fancied that they hear
him swearing at “ sometings."
• * • •
John Thompson, a Coolgardie mining-manager,
interviewed by the British Australasian, says he was still
a firm believer in the field’s prospects, and regretted to see
the parent district lost sight of in the glamor attending
the development of Kalgoorlie. If the B.A. talked to a
Kalgoorlie man, 10 to 1 he would remark that the camp
suffered from the poverty-stricken condition of the Cool-
gardie mines, and he would be a lot nearer the mark than
is John Thompson.
* * • •
A committee of investigation pronounced the
latest gold-rush near Albany, W.A., a rank duffer, and re-
turned much disgusted. Poor sleepy Albany has suffered
much from gold-rushes, and is beginning to ask how they
are brought about. Lots of specimens, having served
their purposes, are parted with there. Many men are
hard-up when.they reach Albany, and are forced to part
with their stock-in-trade—a few “ nice stones.” Writer
advises Albany to keep a permanent committee of in-
vestigation on tap, and to have all rush-makers leg-ironed
until it has reported.

Bayley’s United—a mine quite close to Cool-
gardie—by cyanide : 894 tons, 808 oz. But why don’t they

* * • *
The filter-press system of treating slimes has
been started at Lake View Consols and Brown Hill mines,
Kalgoorlie. The small experimental-plant erected at the
former mine worked successfully, but, naturally, the
keenest interest is taken in the effort now made to treat
thousands of tons monthly. As far as the writer is aware,
filter-press treatment on a big scale has never been tried
elsewhere. Slimes are a trouble all over the world. On
the Rand, enormous heaps have been allowed to accumu-
late, awaiting a process equivalent to cyanide on tailings.
There, several methods have been at work, but the slimes
have to be richer than the average to pay. By the new
method, the slimes are drained, pressed into cakes, and
cyanide-solution forced through by compressed air. Cal-
lahan, the Lake View manager, is now pushing on the
sulphide and telluride plant. Already the mine is the
best-equipped on the field, and, when this plant is finished,
it will be one of the most perfect in the world.

In an appeal last week against a breach-of-
promise verdict for =£7so (obtained by a young
domestic servant against a gilded old gent.) as
excessive in amount, N.S.W. Full Court refused
to disturb the assessment, principally on the
ground, according to Darley, C. J., that plaintiff,
with the view of becoming defendant’s wife, “ had
changed her religion, and had thus incurred the
scorn of her relatives.” But, as this change—even
assuming that the sex ever has any religion beyond
the great cult of husband-catching— was entirely
the woman s own act, performed for her own pro-
fit, it s hard to see why the man should be made
10 pay.

» * • *
An employer’s liability case was heard at
Hobart Supreme Court, the other day, wherein a
*2-a-week laborer sued his boss for compensation
for the mangling of an arm between the cogs of a
wattle-bark chopping machine. It transpired, in
evidence, that the machine could not be stopped
except by shutting off steam at the engine. No
loose pulley, no guard over the rollers, and the
machine was habitually driven for all it was
worth. The Govt, inspector of machinery swore
that the machine was “fairly” safe, if the feeder
was careful; he also stated that he didn’t know
an uncertificated engineer was employed to drive
that particular engine ! The maxim of volenti non
fit injuria was held to apply, and the jury (mostly
employers) verdicted for the employer, though ad-
mitting that there was no evidence that plaintiff
had contributed to the accident by negligence.
Quote the jury’s rider :
They considered that some appliance should be con-
nected with all such machines, to minimise the risk of
accident; but, though they said that, they could not say
that this machine, without such an improvement, was
defective.
Which ought to be a record rider —even for Tas-
mania.

The old Yic. warship Nelson (British
frigate) was sold by auction last week for =£2-100,
the purchaser being a Sydney man, who “intends
breaking her up for the timber and copper.’’
Curious ! But for the high price realised, one
might have almost expected her to go into the
coal-trade.
* * * »
The Nelson, by the way, was a highly
respectable British line-of-battle ship over 00 years
ago. Contrary to a prevalent opinion, she was
never in the command of Admiral Nelson; she
wasn t built till after that hero’s death. The Im-
perial Government presented her to Victoria in
1867. The Nelson, though built for active war
service, never fired an offensive or defensive gun
in her long life.

* * * *
The suicide, in Melbourne, of George
Kisser and his wife ought to serve as an awful
warning for. girls who gather around bicycle-tracks
and gloat upon the legs of “ all the champions.”
Kisser earned a precarious livelihood as a pace-
maker, in which capacity he won the heart of a
pretty governess and married her. They annear
to have lived for ten months upon the girl’s sav-
ings, mostly. Risser’s pacemaking career ended
when cyclist Lesna left Australia, after which he
obtained a b'Het at a French vine-merchant’s
office in Melbourne. When he had lost his billet
and exhausted his wife’s money, and pawned all
her jewellery, the able-bodied pedaller gave him-
self up to despair, and the bride, being evidently
convinced of his inability to make himself useful,
agreed to join him in a double suicide. A tale of
wheel and woe. Poor wretches !

One of the most joyous conflicts on record
took place in Tasmania the other day between a
doctor and a landlady’s daughter and two dogs.
The doctor’s dog was getting the worst of it when,
as alleged at the police-court, he rushed into the
fray, caught up the daughter’s dog, whirled it
round, and slopped it hard down. Then the
daughter flew at the doctor, and the doctor re-
taliated on the daughter, and there was a most
willing go till the lady stood off, and, lowering
her head, butted the medicine-man violently in
the breadbasket. This demoralised the doctor,
and the daughter was led tiway weeping but
victorious. The magistrate fined the unfortunate
doctor £1 with £ ll9s. sd. costs. “ He kicked my
dog.”

Any man with a few more pigs than his
neighbor is made a J.P. in Tas. Literary attain-
ments are never considered ; so the bush Jay-Pay
generally discharges a prisoner off-hand, as the
easiest way out of the difficulty. Bush Tas. J.P.
to drunk and disorderly : “Are ye hungry, my
man?” “ Yes, yeranner.” “ Are ye ther-rersty,
me poo’ fellow ? ” “Yes, yer anner.” To con-
stable : “ Take this man away, an’ put a fade into
him, and then-let him go.” Fact. And, if it was
poor law, it was at least pretty sound benevolence
on the part of the pig-owning J.P.

The R.C.’s are getting a great hold in Fiji
(writes a Bulletin correspondent). They are
building a stone cathedral in Suva, and have
already been two years over it. It’s going to be
the cathedral of the Western Pacific. Sydney
masons superintending, but Heaven knows when
it will be finished. Catholic Polynesian house-
servants are the worst you can engage, and house-
holders are gradually getting rid of them. When
you would expect to find them pipeclaying your
shoes iif the morning, they are at mass, or some-
thing ; and if you kick one out for stealing your
kerosene, the priest will bring him round to you
next day with, “Shure, Joni has repinted, an’ ye
moight thry him agin.”

Grif: “Re Sunday-trading pubs, with three
gates to keep out the law, I know a dozen or more
that only use one gate and have kept out the law
for years. It’s the church-gate-—their servants oil
the gate at 7 o’clock, the boss at 8, the missus at
9, and the son and daughters at 10. They oil
very church-bazaar and rattle. I heard one of
this class say that he shook more dice for more
church-swindles and sold more tickets on Sunday
than any other pub. in the district. N.B. —F'ive
beer-pullers out of six are R.C.—so are the police !

Apropos a recent Bulletin remark, sug-
gested by a missionary meeting observation of
Darley, C.J., that awful horrors existed in way-
back Australia. The other day, a “ respectable ”
young woman sued a “respectable” young man
for the usual 7s. 6d. a week. The young woman,
described as a housemaid, owned up that it was
her second little trouble. She had been living
with her mother recently; her father was away,
and her mother was being “kept” by Billy
somebody. The “ respectable young man ” for
his part suggested that his “ old father ” might
be the father of the stranger; anyway, the girl
and he had gone out together. And this in a
township which is rather proud of its morals.

• * * *
The tick (which has now entered N.S.W.)
presents some curious problems to the scientist.
At Boolburra (Centralia) ticks have been in exist-
ence for two years. Cattle have been subject to
their attacks all that time and yet there is no
fever apparent. Cattle were sent thither, for the
sake of experiment, from other parts. They were
promptly attacked. Skin became raw ; hair fell
off —but there was no fever. Then Boolburra
cattle were sent off to tick-infested Gracemere.
Within a fortnight tick-fever set in and six of
them died. Explanation ?—None.

Touching a delicate ecclesiastical subject :
Went to a Sydney cathedral Sunday night. I don't
wear under-pants, and, in a few minutes, I was horrified
to feel 16 brigades of fleas marching up my ankles, clear-
ing the country as they went. I wriggled and rubbed and
tried to forget the torture in the majestic boom of sacred
music. No go—had to quit; and as I hurried down the
aisle a tall gent got suddenly up, rubbed his legs, and
preceded me to the door. I sympathetically queried on
the steps, “Fleas?”

The attempt to establish the Gee-
Hing at Perth (W.A.) recalls the trouble
at Bendigo a few years ago. Though pro-
fessedly only a benefit association, the out-
side Chows loudly proclaimed the Gee-Hing
a secret society of the most dangerous
type. According to. the plebeian Mon-
golian —for Gee-Hing fnembership is con-
fined to the elite—the initiates are given
to unspeakable iniquity, and the sudden
disappearance of the constable instructed
to investigate was as suspicious as was
the fell ing with an iron bar of Bendigo
pressman Dick Jordon when looking for
Gee-Hing copy in the Chinese camp. De-
tective M‘Manamey, however, who was at
last sent up from Melb., could find nothing
in support of the outsiders’ assertions, and
reported that the Gee-Hing was a per-
fectly harmless institution.
* * * *
Many Melbourne bagnios are just
now being routed out by the police, but
no steps are taken to make the landlords
amenable for their share in the business.
Even under existing legislation, the owner
of a house used as a bagnio is liable to
prosecution but the law has never been
enforced—Fatman is too potent. The fur-
nishing ghoul still battens on his victims,
and the jewels and purple and fine linen
derived from this traffic are still flaunted
at church and conventicle and synagogue.
Miss Sutherland’s denunciation of the
pious owners of bagnio-property is pretty
well forgotten, and matters are ripe for
another kick-up. A little investigation, at
the titles-office, of the ownership of Mel-
bourne brothel - property would 1 provide
some information as interesting as it would
be startling.

* * * •
“ Mac.” to The Bulletin :
Patients and visitors at Melb. Hospital find the whole
place overrun with Danes, Scandinavians, and miscellane-
ous Teutons, who cannot understand three consecutive
words of English or speak one. These foreigners com-
prise the porters, and to elicit any information from most
of them is like attacking the lordly civil servant in a
departmental inquiry office. Those who are civil know
their own tongue only, and those who can speak English
are either surly or wildly excitable. How these fellows
come to be pushed into billets where intelligence and
smartness are the first essentials is incomprehensible, but
probably it is only a result of the old desire for cheapness.
[The hospital authorities, it is stated, prefer
“ Soandies ” on account of their docility, tidiness,
and reliableness. Australians or new-chums ap-
plying for the same class of work are “ bad lots,”
as a rule, looking for a few weeks’ employment to
provide them with more beer-money.—Ed. 13.]
• • • *
There is in Sydney a blind masseur who
is simply coining money. To ladies with unlovely
limbs and graceless figures he is a perfect boon,
because they naturally suppose that what the eye
doesn t see the mouth won’t wax sarcastic over.
As the unlovely ones are in a huge majority, this
consideration might be turned to profitable account
by the Blind Institute.

* * * *
The other day an Australian constable, in
search of a pair of embryo bushrangers, after
making fruitless enquiries at a roadside pub., pro-
ceeded to refresh himself in the bar, and got quite
chummy with two casuals who “shouted” so
liberally that the three left the pub. together
pretty mellow. Then the two strangers got talk-
ing marksmanship, and they all had a try with
the constable’s revolver; but as soon as it was
empty the men annexed not only it but the con-
stable’s watch and Her Majesty’s bracelets. Then
they lashed the unlucky trap to a telegraph-post
and left him to his reflections.

“ Scotty the Wrinkler” :
“Munnner” and I are at one re the nigger-loving
white woman, and the fervent Hindoo or any
other darkey. I fancy General Wheeler was of the
same opinion just before and during the mutiny, likewise
the garrisons of Cawnpore and Lucknow. And I once
saw a paymaster’s daughter being mugged by a chokera.
Then, again, I myself was gone on a colonel’s sister-in-
law, who married into the Bengal cavalry, and had a
yellow baby three months afterwards. What I witnessed
in the Hotel de.l’Europe, in Suez, is known only to me and
another colonel s wife, on her way home, unless the Arab
courierisaliveorsplit beforedeath. At Woolwich, the West
Indians sent home for instruction always had nice white
girls on their arms. Even in Glasgow (although, I confess,
the Scotch lassie is not much of a black-worshipper), the
missionaries of coffee tint always went down well,
but then the Scotch are “ unco guid.” And, look at N.S.
Wales: How the squatteresses do pamper their Jap and
Indian cooks, butchers, and laundrymen ! I think that,
in this country, a woman who allows a blackfellow to wash
her linen, and then joke in the hut about it, is just fit to
live with that black; and, judging by some episodes on
the Murrumbidgee and Lachlan, these lydies are also of
my opinion. At one station, a Chinaman used to hug the
manager’s daughters. Near this place, Narandera, I
have heard jokes passed between Syrian and other tarry
hawkers and white women that would mean look-up for a
white pedlar. I should like to see Ranji’s letters from Aus-
tralienneB.”
ART IN AUSTRALIA.
EMINENT ARTIST (to intending pupil): “You think

“Yes; but, then, YOU learned from good masters! ”
The neutrality proclamation issued by
Australian Govts, in re the United States and
Spain recalls to many the similar proclamation
issued in Ireland at the opening of the Franco-
German war, and the stratagems devised to nul-
lify effect. The Paddies were 10 to lin favor
of France, and, as there was no fighting to be done
just then in Ireland, the most bellicose of the
nation determined to skip the Channel and “offer
their swords ” to Gaul. Under the neutrality
laws, ambulance parties might go from British
territory to either country, and every town and
village in Ireland accordingly started such a corps.
They drilled, collected firearms and ammunition,
and whispered slaughter, but when a spy dropped
on them they pretended to be wholly occupied
studying the way to bandage a sprained ankle or
administer tonics out of pocket-flasks. The
British Govt, shrewdly suspected that when a
corps reached France it would leave ambulance
work to others and get quickly into the thick of
the fight, but, under the neutrality laws, it was
hard to interfere. Scores of Englishmen, how-
ever, went under similar cover to Prussia.

Yet another Sydney woman burnt to
death through her flannelette wrapper catching
light. In anything like close contact with fire,
flannelette is as dangerous as calico soaked in
kerosene.
* * * *
The Sydney police, of course, are paid
privately not to see the scores of open betting-
shops, and all tho Sunday-trading. If the police
aren’t paid, who is paid ? What a farce to have
laws that can’t be enforced by public opinion !
“ ’Twould seem,” writes F.R. to The Bul-
letin, “ that fama and fames make up a chemical
compound. The other day Gordon Coutts’ pic-
tures —some of them really adm.rable—were auc-
tioned in Sydney for a song (and a street-singer’s
song at that), and clever Lister-Lister’s sale did
little more than prove that a picture is always
worth the canvas it is painted on. And nobody
knew that the auctions were to be held. This
sets one thinking of the Painter v. Singer contro-
versy. Singers only appear to push themselves,
because their art necessitates their being literally
before the public. A painter lets his picture
strike its own chords, and will not aid it by one
single advertising tootle from his own trumpet.
This is one of the Seven Deadly Virtues. His
predecessors have placed him on a high pedestal,
and the only way to get down (that he can see) is
to fall. Doubtful though if advertising would
get him better prices, as few men think they get
50 guineas’ worth of pure enjoyment out of any
picture, whereas by the simple leverage of a shil-
ling or two they can open the floodgates of Town
Hall melody. The reason is easy to see. The
average Australian loves Art certainly, but
Reality more. Now, music alone combines the
two. A voice is real. A picture can only be
realistic. This explains why the A. A. prefers to
take his ‘ Ever-restless Sea’ from the Coogee
cliffs, and not at the Sydney Domain Daubery.”

Is it a fact—as stated by Bathurst (N.S. W.)
Advocate —that on last Easter Monday a number
of Christian ladies gave a picnic at Lane Cove
River to a lot of “converted” Chinamen, and
played rounders and kiss-in-the-ring with them ?
It does not sound impossible. There is hardly any
length to which lovely woman will not go to pluck
brands from the burning.

One-in-the-eye for the N.S. W. Cold-Water
Crowd in response to its blundering assumption
that, whereas Gov. Hampden had refused his
patronage to them, he had accorded it to the
accursed Bung-Brotherhood :
In reply, I have to say that you are in error in stating
that H.E. is a patron of the Licensed Victuallers’ Associa-
tion, his patronage being limited to the picnic of that
association. In his Excellency’s opinion, the Licensed
Victuallers are a respectable body of tradesmen, and he
sees good reason why, as the representative of the Crown,
he should support the provision of a day’s pleasure to a
hard worked body of people, and the augmentation of a
fund for relief of distressed members of the trade.
Lord Hampden, it is added, discriminates between
patronage of an association and patronage of an
entertainment therewith connected. Which—as
the wag put-in when the chairman commenced
his speech with “ Sir Gorgias and gentlemen
is “ a very proper distinction ’’!

No possible ingenuity can twist the Bible into a teetotal
book. Its very warnings against excess prove that, as
also do its expressions of tolerance for the teetotallers,
the Rechabites, and the Nazarites. If Christ turned
wafer into wine, it was assuredly intoxicating. No
rational being can suppose that the recognised toast-
master of a city like Cana of Galilee, when teetotalism
was all but, or quite, unknown, would crack up some such
rotgut slop as the elderberry wine, huckleberry wine, and
the like, which, after 2DOO 3 - ears’experimenting, is all that
teetotallism has achieved. At the same time, Card.
Moran and Archbishop Carr are to be honored for their
total abstinence, as a measure unfortunately, and only
temporarily, needed in their position, because, through
deficient social conditions, the one vice of drunkenness
happens to be the most pronounced in their denomina-
tion. I believe Bishop Goe takes his glass of stout, and
Bishop Moorhouse said ten years ago that a little whisky

was a necessity of life to him, for enabling him to stand

Apropos of Darlinghurst prison. A stipen-
diary magistrate outside and in open court cannot
inflict corporal punishment without having a
colleague on the bench, but in prison the visiting
justice—the city stipendiaries take it in turn now—
can (solus) order the “ cat ” for breaches of dis-
cipline.

* • * *
“ I hate rich Victorians ; they are such
snobs—they are worse than English people,’’ once
wrote Francis Adams. Melbourne snobbery is
shortly to be responsible for the appearance of
“ The Australasian Society Blue Book,” the
object of which “will be to keep a complete re-
cord of Victorian society ” —haw ! “We use the
word ‘Australasian,’” magniloquently adds the
“ editress ” —lovely word ! “ because it is our in-
tention shortly to embrace the whole society of
the Southern Hemisphere”-—including, it is to be
presumed, the haut ton of the Hottentots and the
toffdom of Tierra del Fuego. The Bulletin also
surmises that this novel application of the term
“ blue-book ” is intended to imply special refer-
ence to “ blue blood,” and the absence from the
haughty pages of patronymics unpleasantly re-
miniscent of the till, the tap-room, and the
butcher’s-block. According to the circular, the
cost of getting your name into the thing is one
guinea.
* * * *
At Melbourne City Court the other day
lawyer Gaunson, appearing for the lady-principal
of a Lonsdale-street bagnio, wanted to know
whether it was better “ that the Magdalenic
class should be housed in the city, or in the
suburbs ? ” When the prosecuting policeman sug-
gested “the suburbs,” Gaunson drew a word-
picture of the temptation that befalls any man
journeying home, after a banquet in town, in the
same railway-carriage with a tawny-haired siren.
There’s a good deal to be said on both sides of this
improper question, but probably the lawyer was
right. Matters should be so arranged that a man
who reaches his suburban residence at an ungodly
hour may truly attribute the cause to business in
the city.
* • • *
A Northern suburb has a dog-inspector
who gathers in the mangy pup and the wander-
ing mongrel and sells or destroys the unclaimed
animals. He has also a shop in another suburb,
run by his wife, where are sold the juicy meat-pie
and succulent sausage. There is not necessarily
“ anything ” in this but a humorous coincidence.

Holdar M. : “A passion to attach livery
to the hireling whose business exposes him to the
general gaze develops fast in Sydney. The paper’s
'name on a blue ribbon round a tram-guard cap
now adorns many news-runners. The turnstile-
man at the ferry begins to loom large in blue and
gold, while the portly person who officiates out-
side the theatre and millinery-shop becomes more
and more elegantly equipped. The suburban
slavey, too, now seems to take more kindly to the
tell-tale trappings, and I await the capture of the
’bus-driver. Is this uniform mania a dignity
development, or what ?

395 George ' Bt -, Sydney - CALLAGHAN & SON,
J. J. Strickland, of Breanhorne (N.S.W.),
an unusually intelligent J.P., proves himself pos-
sessed of a fine stock of commonsense patriotism.
He Las voluntarily travelled over a large portion
of Grenfell district to explain to dull electors the
value of electors’-rights in forthcoming tussles,
and to witness their signatures where necessary.
Strickland seems to know the average bushy well.
Probate of the will of George Murray,
printer and paper manufacturer, was granted in
Sydney this week, Murray died about a month
ago, and there was absolutely no newspaper refer-
ence to his demise. He was doubly connected by
marriage with Henry Parkes, having married as
his second wife a daughter of the ex-Premier,
whilst the wife of Varney Parkes, the ex-Pr«nier’s
son, is Murray’s daughter.
Wild-eyed and hairy anarchists were much
in evidence at Melb. May-Day Celebration.
Fleming, with a more or less clean red handker-
chief round his waist, a red rosette in his button-
hole, and a broom-handle with a banner of old
red flannel shirt clutched in his right hand, assured
the assembled listeners that “ unless the cry of
the masses were heeded bleeding heads would
again be impaled upon pikes as they were in the
French Revolution.” Dr. Maloney was up-
holstered in flaming red tie with rosette and
cockade of same hue, but Hancock contented
himself merely with a red flower in his button-
hole. The oratory was as lurid as the trappings,
but the 15,000 or so people who by turn listened
and meandered about didn’t take much stock in
the gore. Tillett, speaking with his usual good
sense, was probably the only man present who
said anything that could help the cause of labor.
Probably Maloney’s French education was
mainly responsible for the dramatic incident
which decorated Deakin’s Federation meeting last
Friday. Also, an incidental woman, who climbed
on the platform and left a tumbler half-full of
water on the table in front of Maloney, was partly
responsible in an indirect fashion. It was not
until Deakin and Maloney had completed the
exchange of compliments that the former, turning
towards the table, saw the tumbler. There was
no hesitation; reaching out for the glass, he
emptied its contents with neat precision in
Deakiti’s face, accompanied by a hissed challenge
to meet with pistols. The after events were also
colored by the little doctor’s affectation of French
manners. He went quietly to a room with the
sergeant of police and a few of his friends, where
he instantly flourished his cigarette-case, and
after having handed it round calmly lit a cig.
himself and awaited the result with smoke pouring
out of his nostrils. The daily paper reports of
the occurrence make Deakin act with great
dignity, which is perhaps accounted for by the
fact that he went straight from the hall to the
Age office and thence to the Argus. This may
also account for the fact that both papers state
most of the water missed its mark, which it didn’t.

“I thought I had a Stamp.”
Many inquiries having been made at this office for
the above humorous picture by “Hop” which
appeared in The Bulletin as a coloured supple-
ment some years ago (and which has been long out
of print) Mr. Hopkins has, by special permission of
The Bulletin, executed an etching of the work,
and a limited number of signed artist’s proofs
may now be had only on application to this
office. The proofs are printed on Whatman’s
“Creswick ” drawing paper, 14 x 17£ inches ; size
of plate, x 12. As every detail has been carried
out by the artist himself (including the printing)
these proofs have all the value of an original
drawing. This will be an exceptional opportunity
to possess an example of “ Hop’s ’’ work, which is
rarely to be seen outside the pages of this paper.
