A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 3, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
Your scribe is a little melancholy this week,
and if her gloom overshadows this budget, learn
the raison d'etre and be lenient. Well, there was
a clergymen’s picnic—given by the Broughton
(clerical) Club —during the week, and I wasn’t
there, because I wasn’t asked. And there’s the
case in a nutshell—or a steam-launch, which was
theactual craft of these good picnickers. The Arch-
bishop was the peg the picnic hung upon, and the
Parramatta River was the scene of the revels, and
the Archbishop, who is just back from Europe,
said he had derived much pleasure from the enter-
tainment, and the river smiled and said nothing
at all.
And yet there is such a thing as under-doing a
parsons’ party. Once, not so very long ago, there
was a Sydney lady whose heart delighted itself in
rendering homage to young curates, and the rest
of the clergy, married and unmarried. The hom-
age generally took the form of a tea-party, and
it was noticed by the lady’s cat, which she
always had with her, that no one parson
ever came to any two tea-parties. One night
the cat learned the secret. There was a party,
and a pint of prawns stood at one end of the
table, and a pudding of astounding richness
at the other. In between, there was some butter
and some bread, and very likely a red-glass
flower-stand with pelargoniums and sweet-williams
falling about as stiffly as soldiers. And all round
the festive board sat hungry canons, and hungrier
curates. And the prawns were given in charge of
an archdeacon, who had helped many a prawn to
grace, and the lady herself took charge of the
pudding. After the blessing, which was sung
standing, the meal began, and there and then the
archdeacon disgraced himself for ever and ever.
He helped the whole dish of prawns out in two
distributions to the two old canons, who had said
“ if you please,” to his “ Prawns? ” And for all
the rest there was pudding—only pudding—and
here is where the cat got its fullest significance of
the perils of the clergy. The lady’s husband, who
was just as Scotch as she was, remarked, “ What
a reech puddin’ ye’re geein’ them to-nicht.” And
the cat turned and fled that house for ever, when
it heard the lady’s placid auswer—whispered, but
clear enough to reach the curate, who was tilling
himself on a second helping—“ The eggs was
addled, my dear."
If the end of the century has not seen all the
wonders there are to be seen, it has had a view of
some rare and strange ones. It has watched a
lady go round the world with all her travelling
equipage nursed in one little bag on her right arm,
and it has seen portmanteau after portmanteau
pass away into the silences, and bandbox struc-
tures fall into evil odour beside the sailor-hat that
never needs a bandbox. And now, even a raw
newchum like Sydney has beheld a maiden lady
gather together a party for a private expedition
into the very jaws of the South Sea Islanders,
who, by the way, have never caught the civilised
and unaccountable prejudice against unmarried
ladies.
This latest thing in explorers is Miss Hastie, a
Scotchwoman with a pile to play with as she
chooses. Her choice runs to seaweed and science,
and expeditions into the Unknown, where, in-
deed, most women like to wander, even if they
have to charter a spook instead of a vessel. But
Miss Hastie’s unknown is a tangible place, in-
habited by men with big teeth, who would never
join with Shakespeare in decrying too too solid
flesh. She has chartered and fitted-up the
schooner Sydney Belle at her own expense, and
the boat left Sydney last Tuesday for Wellington,
where the rest of the party gets in. Along with the
others goes a lady botanist, Miss Prince, and three
more or less learned females, and the trip ought
to be an uncommonly jolly one, if learning does
not overcloud the sport. The majority of the
party hail from Maoriland.
Maoriland is also responsible for Mr. Alfred Hill,
Sydney Liedertafel’s new conductor. A meek’
little young man, with a pale and boyish face, and
locks where the hair ought to be, he put an extra-
ordinary amount of energy into his waves and
passes at the first Liedertafel concert of the
season. The dreamy members woke into new life
under his baton, and, now and then, shook off for
a moment the heavy suburban atmosphere, under
which they usually pass their time, or have it
passed to them. The audience was less successful.
Suburban it came, and suburban went away. Miss
Hetty Holroyd, a new soprano, roused everyone
who was feeling a little sleepy with her brilliant
rendering of “ Ah Non Credea ” and Strelezki’s
“Dreams.” Herorgan is dramatic, penetrating, and
exquisitely clear, and much developed sinceher first
appearance in the “Sign of the Cross.” She was
the lady, you remember, who twanged the lyre to
Berenice. On the whole, although the part-sing-
ing was a little violent with its diminuendos, the
Liedertafel seems in a fair way for a revival.
That much-admired personage, “The Idler,”
came back to the Criterion on Saturday, and every-
one who was anyone at all was there to meet him.
Diamonds sparkled in the opera-boxes and swag-
ger frocks were the order of the night. Especially
on the other side of the footlights. Miss Lamb
was a well-groomed Lady Harding, but she had
one house-dress that amazed me with the tying
of its bows. The mainstay of the frock was soft
pink flowery muslin, and up the front and down
the back pink satin ribbon was knotted in the
weirdest —well, I remember once trimming a dress
in a nightmare, and I tied bows just so. The
“ just so ” is indescribable. I only know that the
ribbon has the appearance of being in one long
piece that is never cut anywhere, and every bow
seems to be tied on the far end of another bow.
In the fourth act Miss Rickards paralysed
Saturday’s audience with a red hat of the loudest
order of reds, and a blue-green frock that reflected
a sea and sky by Holman Hunt. The bodice was
barred all over with a lattice-work of narrow black
velvet, criss-crossed until it looked like a bird-
cage. And as Noni herself is not unlike a canary
the raiment was out of place among the garbing of
the featherless bipeds. Tho house uttered an
anguished “ Oh ! ” when this vision burst upon it,
and nobody heard anything for quite five minutes
after. Monday night put things right again.
The red hat disappeared, and the black one toned
the frock down half a scale, and it was then one
saw that the dress itself was very good in its way,
and would have been excellent attire after the
lady had become Mrs. Simeon Strong. Miss
Lamb wears her dresses very long in this play,
but walks gracefully in spite of the frills under her
feet, and she and Miss Stewart between them
strike several harmonious tones of colour. The
latter lady is just the sort of chaperon you would
have liked for your first season. No higher praise
can be given to any old lady.
A few days ago I had a talk with Miss Lamb,
and she truly is a rarely interesting woman—
clever, unconventional, and, strange quality for
an actress, shy. In her deep voice she discoursed
to me about this same reserve of hers, and wished
’twere otherwise with her. And thereupon I
mentally tabulated a theory that has been vaguely
floating about in search of a landing-place.
Pushed briskly to a point, this theory is that th«
two English actresses, who are starring respective
companies in Sydney to-day, fall short of
greatness for two utterly opposite reasons.
Maud Jeffries, with her fine presence, beauti-
ful face, and her attractiveness and senti-
ment, ruins herself by the looseness of her
method. Her tears rush out in oceans with one
touch of the tear-tap. Her voice quivers, trem-
bles, breaks at the slightest suggestion of emotion.
Floppety-flop all over the shop go her arms and
her head when dejection is called on. Then how
different with Miss Lamb. You want her to feel,
to weep. You admire her simple entrance and
exit, her easy speech, her clever carelessness. You
see that she can feel and yet she cannot make you
feel her feel. When the touch of emotion is
wanted something seems to bind her down. And
if Miss Jeffries’ over-quick capacity for the floridly
emotional could be kneaded up with Miss Lamb’s
overstrained restraint, what a big bright star
would flash from out the leaven ! As for
the name' of the star, I am inclined to think it
would have to be brainy Beatrice Lamb.
The discussion of problem plays is as out of date
as crinolines, but the “ Tree of Knowledge,” that
has just died down at the Criterion, has left a
provocative odour behind. Am I alone in my
ignorance or does anyone else want to know what
the problem molecule is made of ? A brief study
of the question reveals darkness and more dark-
ness and problems all along the way. Should the
problem of a problem-play concern itself entirely
with the future of a Woman with a Past, taking no
account of the past aspect of many men’s futures?
Or does the unsettled question of Hamlet’s mad-
ness send Mrs. Tanqueray and the Prince of
Denmark to sea in the same frail boat ? At the
end of all these wonderings one comes to a little
rock of belief that the dramatists have merely
been trying to make womankind appear more
“curiouser” than the real thing warrants. And,
with that rock to stand on, it is not surprising
that the problem play has failed this century.
Neither you nor I own cab-horses, so we cannot
be accused of puffing up our own when we com-
pare the stateliness of a cab-horse turning corners
and crossing streets, with the impudent freedom
of the cycling animal. The law keeps its eye on
the pace of the former, but the other beast travels
at its own free will, and last week this free will
upset two ladies at one spurt. One of the victims
was knocked up against a ’bus, and had her feet
badly-injured. The worst of it is that the
man on the bicycle can run away as easily as he
runs over, and before the victim has time to cry
out, the cycle is in another part of the town. By
the way, the cycling fever is cooling down a little
among women, and authorities reckon it will be
as dead as roller-skating in two years’ time.
This month is brisk with balls and benefits.
The long-talked-of Straus concert comes off
shortly, and ought to be the very biggest
thing of its kind. For years the name of Straus
has been on every list of musicians who were
helping other people’s charities. Among other
functions, little Miss Usher’s benefit at the Town
Hall next Saturday, and the Neutral Bay tennis
dance at the Paddington Town Hall next
Wednesday, are both likely to attract.
The French have lately annexed the word
“ snob,” and gi ven to it an entirely different sig-
nification from its English one. A French snob
is an ultra-fashionable or smart person, while the
corruption “ snobisme ” is used to denote the most
exclusive creme of Parisian society ! Last year
the boulevard word for chic was deuil (mourning),
on account of the number of high-toned families
who were placed in black through the terrible fire
at the Paris charity bazaar. To say a person was
“ tout fait deuil ” was to pay the highest com-
pliment to his or her “ form.”
Is there not something baffling to a thoughtful
person in the fact that, after more than 20 years
of higher education, the New Woman movement,
problem novels, and all the other shrieks with
which woman has proclaimed her intellectual,
moral, and spiritual equality with man, woman’s
taste in reading-matter has remained practically
in statu quo? Take, for example, the innumer-
able magazines of to-day, bearing such significant
titles as Woman’s Life, Home Chat, &c., and to
what subjects does one find them devoted ?
The sameness of their contents month after
month appalls one. There are long, grovelsome
disquisitions on the manners, customs, and appear-
ance of Europe’s upper ten thousand, and advice
on keeping nails, hair, house, and young man, in
order. Particularly the young man. He got a
whole page to himself, and the page is headed
“Lovers’Difficulties,” and if the paper doesn’t
wipe-out the difficulties, it is certainly calculated
to put an end to the love and the lover. The
most popular of these feminine productions boasts
that nearly all its contributors own a title, or
move in high society, and the noble disregard of
the rules of grammar and composition that these
contributors evince points to the truth of the
editorial statement.
One of these woman’s-magazine-writers, dis-
coursing on the [late Battenbnrg’s only daughter,
Miss Ena, remarks that when she is older she
will, probably, be her royal grandmother’s most
devoted companion. The youngster is not yet ten
years of age. How many years do they expect
that old lady to live ?
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ There were no less than three lawyers in a
recent quarantine batch, and didn’t the men of
law talk themselves red in the face re the unlaw-
fulness of their detention ! At last fellow-martyrs,
tired of ‘ torts ’ and similar legal topics, treated
the talkers to a midnight visitation by black-clad
mourners carrying corpse-candles. Habeas corpus
humor! One quarantine victim was an expec-
tant bridegroom. On the day his wedding-bells
were to have rung he was in the hands of the
vaccinators.
“ Lots of girls regret the uncertainty as to Wil-
son Barrett s return to Melbourne. He was de-
lightful to meet at afternoon-tea, except that he
generally swore you on the bread and butter to see
his Hamlet. Even that non-theatre-going step-
sister of his, mother of a suburban ex-mayor, ven-
tured into a box during the regime of the melan-
choly Dane. Saints who habitually disapprove of
the stage, but occasionally make an exception in
favor of the ‘ Sign of the Cross ’ or sich, seem to
feel safer, somehow, fenced off in a private fold
with their own unpolluted family party. ‘We
came in a cab,’ they tell you, ‘and went straight
to our box ’ ; and there they sit with their eyes
timidly averted from the rest of the audience. All
the same, I believe they’re rather disappointed
that the acting includes no horrid scene among the
chronic habitues, something after the style of
those midnight orgies in the vicious dens of Holtian
melodrama. After protesting all your life that it’s
not ‘ the histrionic art ’ you object to, but the
‘ contaminating association of play-houses,' it is a
bit unsatisfying not to sniff so much as burnt
feathers when you hang over the Eiery Furnace.
The latest Madden photo, gleams from every
bookstall and dazzles Melb. Beauty-pictures of
the Madden daughters are common enough, but
this, the most beautiful of all, is the Madden papa
in his uniform as ‘ Commander-in-Chief-of-the-
Vietorian-land-forees ’ in a full military rig (the
turn-out cost, I believe, .£3OO !). The learned one
stands in a commanding attitude at the foot of
Govt. House steps, one hand on his sword, the
other tucked jauntily on his hip. He looks so
very like a comic-opera soldier —no man of his
‘ cut ’ should ever wear fancy-dress—that it’s a
wonder Vic.’s Chief Justice, who tried the Mer-
cantile bankers, isn’t frightened of being known
as General Boom !
“Touring Bananalanders describe their new
Minister of Railways (Murray, M.L.A.) as a
bearded, loose-limbed Scot, who, ere he attained
his present political eminence, was chiefly famous
in his ain district as an agile stepper of reels and
siccan. According to Central Queenslanders,
northern ball-goers hated the sight of Hieland
Jock in his dancing days. Local beaux and
belles would be waltzing languidly and flirting
furiously to the tumty - turn of ‘ a Highland
schottische, when suddenly the mooners would jab
elbows with this conscientious Caledonian whose
swishing beard, windmill arms and double-jointed
legs as likely as not cleft you and your partner
apart, while the enthusiast snapped all his fingers
in the air at once and skirled across country with
an eagle’s scream, repeated from time to time in
unison with certain beats of the music. The
Minister’s first wife was a daughter of Parson
Hartley, that dead-and-gone pioneer of Methodism
in Central Queensland, remembered thereabouts
as the doughtiest devil-thumper of early times.
Like most preachers, his departed reverence sank
early into anecdotage, and hearers gradually har-
dened to such thrilling chronicles as that of the
oft-quoted young lady who would go to a ball, and,
having got there, ‘ screamed, swooned and died ’ in
the midst of her sins.
“ Austral Salonites having taken the cause of
the Old Colonists to heart, undertake to express
their sympathy through the channel of a stall at
the coming Carnival. Mr. Chief-Justice Madden
is to preside at a business-meeting, at the Salon,
on May 2nd, to be attended by members and
adherents en masse, each and all to plank down
her preliminary half-crown, in token of loyal co-
operation.
“The Austral Salon is a warm-hearted, also a
level-headed, coterie when all’s said and done. It
always has a practical scheme of help ready for
any charity carnival or wind-raising hullaballoo.
Also, last meeting a young member arose and put
forward the woes of a distressed musician’s family
so forcibly that the hat went round and £5 were
collected.
“The crowded state of Melb. Town Hall on
Saturday night showed that the Newbury-Spada
combination has struck a bright and paying idea
in inaugurating shilling “ pops.” Tenor Newbury
sang many of his old and favorite songs, but
chiefly fetched his audience with that popular
chestnut, ‘The Holy City.’ Mme. Spada, in pale
blue, with sleeves that had slipped to her elbows,
was most applauded for her parts in duets, espe-
cially in the ‘ Trovatore ’ scene, in which the tenor
conscientiously concealed himself behind a red
screen ‘ off.’ Encores were so frequent that I only
hope accompanist Scherek was on piecework, and
not on time. More by token, no one knew better
than Herr Scherek how much of the applause is
often due to his careful ‘ nursing ’ of the per-
former’s voice and how much to the singer.
“ Marshall Hall thought up a morbid programme
for his Saturday orchestral concert in Melb. Town
Hall. Beethoven’s | Eroioa ’ funeral-march con-
veys the most realistic and convincing impression
of death—the grim, unadorned fact that ‘ puts
the comether’ on life yet given to the
world in music. Yet fieethoven had too
keen a sense of the ridiculous to write up to
the sordid grotesquerie of the hearse and the black
box and the nodding feathers. Ho wouldn’t be
guilty of an anti climax, although Nature sets him
the example. To tho great climax, Death, she
claps on the absurd littleness of the undertaker.
The programme’s second morbidity, death musio
for ‘ Alcestis’ (the Greek play about to be struggled
with in public by Trinity students), didn’t come off,
owing to indisposition of the vocalist, Mme.
Wiedermann ; but the audience evidently thought
that the succeeding number, ‘The Ship of the
Fiend,’ never performed before, was a little thing
of tho conductor’s own, and clapped him with
enthusiasm. The last verse of the cheerful ballad
which Hamish M‘Cunn’s descriptive music inten-
sifies runs—
He struck the mainmast wi’ his hand,
The foremast wi’ his knee ;
He split the gallant ship in twain,
And sank her in the sea.
This is the kind of pleasant Saturday afternoon
that makes an audience sadly disperse musing on
its overdue gas-bill and shabby gloves, and won-
dering where on earth it is going to raise the price
of its winter outfit.

“ There is a capital portrait of M.L.A. Theo-
dore Fink (Vic.) by Phil May in London Punch
for March 12. It is in a sketch, the letterpress to
which represents ‘Jink ’ (i.e., Fink) saying to one
Mac Fuddle: ‘ My dear Mac Fuddle, it’s the very
thing! a charming house, lovely spot; cheap,
too; but one great drawback, you can't get any
water there.’ Mac Fuddle: ‘Oh, that doesn’t
matter.’ A few months back Phil May had in
Punch a caricature of a ball-room tenor singing a
song. The front page of his music was turned to
the reader and represented the song as being by
‘ Theodore Fink.’ Phil May had a warm fond-
ness for the chirruppy Melbourne solicitor, and
writes him delightful letters illustrated, often in
color, in the corners and margins with humorous
pictures—letters to prize.

one could go and hear Albani without the dread-

of course not! ’ Old lady : ‘ No, I thought not.
It’s so naice, I think. And if there had been
anything, I should have been sure to see it in the
papers. ’ The old lady’s tone betrayed a touch of
disappointment, nevertheless.

xomkinson, M.L.C. (S.A.), banker and stalwart

“A- vice-regal representative is allowed
little latitud o as a speaker,” says Acting-Gov.
Madden. Fortunately, though the latitude is
small, there is no limit to the platitudes.
Gov. Buxton, when seeking a seat in the
Commons, was rejected five times running by
Bnglish constituencies. He lately expressed his
intention of making one try more when he finally
cuts himself adrift from Australia.
Sydney Daily Telegraph staff produced
two authors in one week. Mr. Tom Courtney comes
to the world with a new shorthand system of his
own, and Mr. R. L. Nash, as becomes a financial
critic, presents a stockbrokers’ vade mecum.
The late “Charley” Collins is the third
member of the present N.S.W. Leg. Assembly
removed by death, the other two being Angus
Cameron and D. C. J. Donnelly. In the same
space of time seven members of the Leg. Council
went over.”
" THE BULLETIN'S ” PORK SUPPERS.
Another bad night.
It seems idle in N.S.W., so far as direct
results are concerned, to show up the tyrannies
or follies of a Judge. Once let a man get a seat
on the bench, superior or minor, and ho can
wallow in insolence, cruelty and unreason, till the
teeth drop out of his head.
Printed in a M.L. paper that the manager
of one of the Hagey institutes, more or less re-
cently established in Australasia for the cure of
drunkenness, was charged before the local magis-
trates with being helplessly drunk, and was re-
manded for four days for medical treatment.
Obituary, Pastor Hastie, of Buninyong.
Most graphic used to be his description of Bal-
larat, before the first gold-digger arrived. A
shepherd said to Mr. Hastie, somewhere in what
is now Sturt-street: “ Sir, the stillness here is so
awful that I feel as if it would drive me mad.”
The deceased mining-speculator Crotty’s
bequest of <£loo,ooo to Archbishop Carr revives
veteran architect Warden’s hope of seeing all the
three spires of his beloved St. Patrick’s R.C.
Melb. Cathedral on. <£30,000 will do it. So that
Melb. won’t have to wait 400 years like Cologne.
Bishop Field Flowers Goe has announced
that young Australia does not laugh. The poor
bishop is understood to have arrived at this con-
clusion because an audience of young Australians
sat perfectly impassive during the delivery of one
of his lectures which he conceived to be chock-full
of rollicking humor.
The Queen of Spain presented a gold altar
flate to St. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney, in the old
'olding days. Senor San Just, who was then
Spanish consul in Sydney, handed over the
treasure and was in return decorated with the
badge of the St. Gregory Knighthood, being thus
the first in Australia to receive that distinction.
Jennings came next.
Rumored that Gov. Gormanston, Tas.,
will apply for leave to extend his term, which
expires in August. There is one thing about
Jenico Joe which greatly endears him to the hearts
of the majority of the Van Diemons. They never
see him and seldom hear of him, and, when the
latter, it is a pleasant intimation that lie is either
fishing in the lake or bogged on its shores.
If the local selection of Dr. Higgins,
Auxiliary Bishop of Sydney, to succeed Dr. Cani
as Bishop of Rockhampton (Q.) be confirmed by
the Pope, Dr. O’Haran (Card. Moran’s nephew)
or Monsignor O’Brien will have the way open to a
mitre in Sydney.
Australasian Pastoralists’ Review advo-
cates a conspiracy of silence against Ben Tillett.
Argument will generally burst-up everything but
the truth, and to resort to the suppress is practi-
cally to admit that the other fellow, being in the
right, is unanswerable.
Rev. J. Gr. Gell, rector of Buxted, in Sus-
sex, who has just died in England, was, years ago,
before he became a parson, a Colonial Office
official. As such he was sent out to Tasmania,
where he married a daughter of Sir John Frank-
lin. Gell was the original of that splendid charac-
ter, Old Brooke, in “ Tom Brown’s School Days,”
and seems to have been a grand type of man.
A Btoc< of Dyson’s “Rhymes from the
Mines ” was destroyed in the fire at Murray and
Co.’s, last October, together with the stereos.
This necessitates the re-setting of the whole of the
volume, and the work is now being pushed on.
The next issue of the “ Rhymes ” will be printed
from new type, and, from a mechanical point of
view, will be away ahead of the first publication.
There are some queer names among the
clergy. One Melb. suburban shepherd rejoices in
the name of Drought; another sin-shifter in a
fashionable pulpit struggles along under the awful
burden of Holyoak Rust as a cognomen. Field
Flowers Goe is the best-known example of a
weirdly-named gospel-spreader, but Rev. Godbe-
here really seems as if he had been labelled
a-purpose.
Judge Dobson, of Tasmania, some time
ago was respectfully requested by Hobart health
authorities to remove from his premises the
“nuisance” which flowed into an open rivulet
running through a populous suburb. Knowing a
little of the law and the profits, Lambert Dobson
“minimised” the trouble by digging a cess-pit
and manuring his garden therefrom. His Honor
died of typhoid fever.
Towards the middle of this month Tom
Durkin is to be given a big complimentary shivoo
by the Melb. artists. The festival is to take the
form of a musical smoke-night, with the usual
liquid accompaniments. Half-a-erown is the tariff,
and a crowded house is expected by the organisers.
As Durkin leaves for England early in June, the
corroboree on Eastern Hill will partake of the
nature of a farewell demonstration.

P.J.M. : “lie your reference to the ’9B
heroes, does anybody know where ‘ General ’ Holt
is buried? C. S. Parnell’s shooting-lodge was
right in the centre of the country held by Holt
during the rebellion, and P. used to point out the
scene of the various engagements and dilate on
Holt’s judgment in choosing them. The fact that
the Irish mountains are all coastal was, in Par-
nell’s opinion, fatal to the chances of a successful
rising.”
Died the other day at Auckland (M.L.)
Francis Dart Fenton, lawyer, musician, linguist,
and author. Fenton, during nearly fifty years’
residence in M.L., played many parts, and most
of them creditably. He was a magistrate, dis-
trict judge, chief justice of the Native Land
Court, founder of Auckland Choral Society,
composer of an opera, a virtuoso of the violin'
wrote several standard works on Maori history,
etc., and as a Maori scholar had few compeers’.
He was greatly handicapped by deafness and a
somewhat irascible temper, but he enjoyed the es-
teem and warm friendship of such nnn as Hori
Grey and Judge Maning (“ Pakeha-Maori ”), and
his faults were chiefly on the surface. Peace to
his ashes !
Capt. Miller, the deceased father of Vic.
“ Money Miller,” a Waterloo man, always
walked as though he still wore the leather stock,
and carried his walking stick on his shoulder like
a musket. Ho had a keen eye for a pretty girl,
and would plant himself every afternoon at
“ Walch’s Corner ” in Hobart to see the girls go
by. “Ah,” he would say with an unctuous
smack of his withered lips, and a wink of his
wicked old-campaigner’s eye, “The Tasmanian
girls have no ankles, sir—no ankles 1 Why, sir,
when I served in Spain—hm !ha !—but it won’t
do to tell you youngsters,” and then old Captain
Miller would cackle himself blue in the face. He
was close in money matters, and passed that on to
his son. Whether he passed on the other weak-
ness also isn’t definitely known. At all events no
one ever found Money Miller winking publicly at.
any nice girl.
Tillett is tough, but his lung-weakness is
wearing him down (writes a Melb. pressman to
The Bulletin). He is plucky and is making a
grand fight with disease. It is pitiful to see him
after one of his lectures, when the frenzy into
which he whips himself has subsided. He is
bloodless, hollow-voiced, slow-eyed and nerveless.
This is the exhaustion of the physical man. Men
tallyhe is still active, and mental activity has begun
to deprive him of his natural rest. After a lecture-
night he rarely shows up next day till afternoon,
not having turned out till 12 o’clock. Even then
he looks haggard. A significant sign, too—his
voice grows weaker and somewhat cracked. During
the first part of his speech at the Eight-Hour Gala
luncheon he was told to “ speak up.” Pathetic !
the man with the voice that could once make itself
heard over a mass of a hundred thousand
“ dockers ” told to “speak up ” ! Tillett, by the
way, has made a big blunder in joining the
wholly-brainless, ragtail little crowd of Vic. anti-
Federalists from the Trades Hall.

“ WHATS IN A NAME?”—A SYDNEY
’BUS INCIDENT.
Melbourne ’Erald S. V. Winter, being on
his trave's, sends paragraphs to his paper about
the wonders he sees. Writing is, apparently, not
S. V.’s strong point. For instance, discoursing on
the Aquarium in Naples, the Literary Thunder-
blast writes—
The principal attraction at the Naples Aquarium ap-
peared to be a small octopus, whose gyrations in his tank
was decidedly gruesome.
Rev. Dr. White, of Singleton, who went
as minister to that hamlet in 1847 and has held
the fort there since, enjoys the distinction of
being the only man living for whom a Cabinet of
politicians begged a University title. The Cowper
Ministry wrote to Glasgow University soliciting
some title, even a little one, for the book-loving
parson, but the University couldn’t see it. This
riled Mr. White’s friends, and when the Sydney
University opened its doors the rev. gentleman’s
admirers assaulted that institution so vigorously
that it yielded him in rapid sequence a 8.A.,
an M.A., an LL.B. and an LL.D. Dr. White,
who is still hale and hearty at 77, was for some
years classical master in Dr. Lang’s old college in
Jamieson-street, Sydney.

That grand old Victorian warrior, Francis
Longmore, has been gathered to his fathers, at
the age of 72. Longmore was in Victorian poli-
tics almost from the time when Victoria first
began to have any politics, and his first appear-
ance in Parliament dated as far back as ’G4. He
and Berry were colleagues in Duffy's Cabinet of
1871-72, and he was Commissioner of something
in Berry’s 74-days’ Ministry of ’75. Also, he
held office in Berry’s second Ministry of 1577-80.
Then he lost his seat in 1883, and dis-
appeared out of politics for 11 years. He came
back to Parliament in 1894 and dropped out again
for the last time in 1597. Longmore, in his best
days was a fiery orator, a strong democrat, and an
unlucky politician, inasmuch as the great art of
sitting on a rail was not in him. When the
Coalition days came there was no room for an irre-
concileable like the old man of Dandenong, and he
had to go. In his later political days he was as brave
and as irreconcileable as ever, but physically he
was only the cinder of the bygone volcano. Also,
he was very poor, which is the latter end of most
politicians—and the sure and certain end of every
politician who is honest. Longmore’s hatred of
landlordism had a solid basis —while a boy he was
thrown out on to the roadside in the North of
Ireland with his evicted father and mother.
■' Jimmy ” Martin, ex-member of N.S.W.
Leff. Assembly, who died suddenly at Sydney on
Saturday, was, while a bit of a politician and
canny underground engineer, chiefly notable for
dog-like faith in the supreme powers of the late
Parkes. He was a Hughey Taylor No. 2. In
selecting a trustee to administer the Parliamentary
grant to Parkes’s wife and children the Govern-
ment hesitated a long time between Jimmy and
Hughey, and, in the end, picked the former
because of his comparative youth. And now
Jimmy and Hughey are both dead.
Victor Daley’s poems (nine-tenths of them
originally printed in The Bulletin) are at last
well advanced towards issue by Angus and
Robertson. The modest Daley’s newspaper con-
tribution of verse has extended over many years,
and as his bent has been almost purely literary,
he is, as compared with the writers of “ popular ”
verse, almost unknown. Even the Gyles Turners
and Alexander Sutherlands have not made men-
tion of him. But it will be found that Daley is
far and away Australia’s deftest living songsmith.
His book will be full of charming poetry and most
delicate workmanship, and The Bulletin will be
proud that it has come mainly from its pages.
When Tom joe Byrnes, new Q’sland Premier,
was in England, he lunched with Dilke at the
House of Commons, and Dilke having chatted
about Australia fora time ended with a vigorous
laudation of old Parkes. Byrnes looked on the
old man of Faulconbridgeas a humbug of the worst
kind, criminal in his raising of the Kiama ghost,
Kilitical spieler in everything. He reminded
ilke that Dilke had cruelly said of Parkes that
“ Parkes’s one virtue was his remarkaNe buoyancy,
for neither his poetry nor his debts could sink
him.” Dilke admitted the political spieler and all
the rest of it, but still defended’Enery. ‘‘Why,”
said Dilke—“his vices were on such a European
scale.”
It turns out that the young man, Vaiange,
whom Chief Justice Madden’s daughter Sylvia is
to marry, is a grandson of Melbourne’s first
Mayor, old Condell. This is the worthy person
who, on the occasion of the first Mayoral pro-
cession in ’42. arrayed himself in a Masonic robe
of crimson sdk—there being no proper Mayoral
robe then—and paraded the streets in it, looking,
as chronicler “ Garryowen ’’ remarks, “ as if clad
in the mormng dress of some obese dowager.’’ A
bullock took alarm at the red garment, and chased
one of the Corporation officers who was carrying
a banner into a deep drain full of slush, where he
was covered up to the neck.
On the mcrningof mining-swind- ;r Geane’s
releaso from prison a number of ooorlv-clad
elderly women waited outside Melb. gaol, and
every now and again called over to the-detective
office to enquire whether the pious fraud had yet

been transferred some months previously. As a
lawyer, Geake has bested the Vic. authorities
badly, for they made the error of having him ex-
tradited on one charge only, instead of on the
score or so they might have made against him.
Which is why Geake, in a trifle under two years,
is again a free man. Neither is it likely that any
of his many victims will take the law into their
own hands. The only instance of personal
reprisal which resulted from the whole land-and-
bank-booni transactions was when a blacksmith
stoushed ex-Premier Munro in Collins-street.

lie ’9S celebrities sent hitherward. Hayes
(Sir John), of Vaucluse, was not of them. He
was out here before the rebellion - sent out for
attempting the abduction of an heiress in Ireland.
Had a mortal hatred towards Curran, who prose-
cuted him. Rigged up a figure of Curran in a
shooting-gallery, and daily practised on it with
his duelling pistols. “Now then, for the little
blackguard's heart,” he would say. “Ha ! that’s
home ! ” He was mad on this point; for the rest,
a quiet, gentlemanly, and well-mformed man.

Aboriginalities.
Archer: “There is a bird in Tas., the
black jay, which says ‘ Want! ’ (three seconds’
pause) ‘ Anti - anti - federal ! ’ The ‘Want’ is
solemnly guttural, and the pause invariably the
same length ; the ‘ anti-anti-federal ’ rising into a
cackling shriek. It is so ridiculously plain that I
have stood for half-an-hour listening to the legend
with joy. Any Tas. bush man will corroborate
this.” But Jack Want is a white jay.
* * * •
H.F. : “The bush is not monotonous to
the native thereof. Every tree has a story.
Sleepers, girders, slabs, posts and rails —the native
sees them under their bark covering. And, look-
ing at this bark, lie knows by the scratches what
animals live in the tree : native cab, bear, silver
fur, rum per, or ring-tailed ’possum. He tracks
bees’-nests by spiders’-webs ; the dead bees hang-
ing thickly as the nest is neared. Nearly all his
sport is at nightfall ; then, sheltered by a stump
near a grassy clearing, he waits for hares to come
and play, or imitates the curlew’s cry and lures
that wary bird within gun-shot. He fishes by
moonlight, dropping moths in the waterholc for
the sluggish perch. In winter there are ’possums
to snare; and in summer, wattle-bark to strip.
The so-called monotonous bush is to him home,
friend and companion ; he has personal recognition
of every tree for miles around. He talks little,
except in that silent language heard in old cathe-
drals ; on high mountains and ocean beaches ; in
the long aisles of eucalyptus—the tongue that
binds the sympathetic soul to perpetual brother-
hood. Though the bushman never speaks of this
sylvan freemasonry, he feels it in the inmost part
of him, and returns with joy after visiting the
outer world to the sweet scents of the gum-
leaves aud the rich silence of his solitude.”

The difference between the Kanaka and
Jap. is strikingly illustrated in the Torres Straits
pearl-fisheries. While the Jap. views death
as an everyday circumstance of very little ac-
count-equivalent to the breaking of a saucer,
for example—the Kanaka makes a big howl
and performs slow and solemn rites over his
dead. He doesn’t like to touch the body, ex-
cept in certain set fashions, and he is very
fastidious about being the first to put on a diving-
dress in which a man has died, or in which one
has been hauled up only to die. Not so the
Jap. He syndicates in all his boats, and when
one dies t’other comes on. He’s greedy enough
for deep-water shell and reckless enough to go for
it. His mates pull up a paralysed or suffocated
diver. No matter. The weather is fine, the tide
good, the sea quiet, plenty shell down below
under the helmet goes the head of the
brown man,” and, with a “good-bye; s’pose I
die, plenty more Japanese in Japan,” down and
down and down and down he goes.

Scotus : “ The Narandera Bushman’s
Home is a doss-house, where stretcher, fuel, light,
and cooking-utensils are found for twenty-four
hours for the very reasonable sum of 6d. This
caravanserai is called Jubilee House, in twelve-inch
letters over the portal. The name has driven
many customers to the sandhill and river-bank.”

Dear Bulletin. —Never before heard of a
wild magpie imitating sounds, and I think that
stallion-imitating bird very doubtful. So far as I
know, Australia has two mocking-birds—the
bower-bird and the lyre-bird. The former is
mostly met with on flat, scrubby country in
Western N.S.W. and N.W. Queensland ; the lyre-
bird only in mountainous fern country. The two
are the shyest birds we have, and rarely, if ever,
survive captivity. Marvellous tales are told of
the bower-bird’s powers of mimicry ; but, in my
opinion, the lyre-bird surpasses him. In Black-
warry and Currajong (South Gippsland) ranges,
L’ve often heard the latter faithfully imitate the
sounds of the fencer’s axe, the thud of the paling-
splitter’s mallet, the grating of a file on a cross-
cut, the squeaking of the pit-saw, the howl of the
dingo, the cry of the black cockatoo, the chatter-
ing of lories or rosellas, the ding-dong of the
bell-bird, and the cracking of the stockwhip-bird.
The laughing-jackass beats him, though. I’ve
heard a lyre-bird attempt the jackass’s Peacock-
smile and fail.—6 xB.
A nevv-chum English-
man recently went on to a
Yic. station, as a kind of
assistant-manager. There
was no doctor within 30
miles, and one of the func-
tions of the new-chum was
to act as surgeon and phy-
sician to the station-hands,
in cases of emergency.
This he did with the aid
of a small medicine-chest
and a cheap volume of
the “ Family Doctor ” or-
der. One night, soon after
the new-chum’s arrival at
the station, a knock came
to his bedroom door, and
he was told that one of
the hands had poisoned
himself. He hurried into
his clothes and hastened
to the scene, where he
found a young fellow
writhing on the ground,
palpably suffering from
poisoning. Ho reached
down the medical hand-
book, and read therein
that, in cases of suspected
poisoning, the best thing
to do was to administer
an emetic of warm water
and mustard. A stiff dose
of the prescription was
quickly mixed, and poured
down the throat of the
poisoned man. “But,”
said the new-chum, as he
related the story after-
wards, “ we couldn’t get
the blessed stuff to stay
down. So we mixed an-
other dose, but that came
up too. Then we mixed
a third lot, but the fellow
brought that up also. So
I said, ‘ This infernal
emetic is no good ; it won’t
stay down. We must try
something else.’ But bless
you, before we could try
anything else, the bloom-
ing fool came round all
right.”

THE POWER BEHIND THE THROWN.
Are there any authentic instances of a
man being saved from death by an apparently
deliberate action on the part of a horse ? When
writer was a small boy one of his chums was the
rather chuckle-headed son of the local pound-
keeper. This galoot was the proud possessor of a
piebald pony that followed him about like a dog,
would frisk with him with the playfulness of a kid,
and had an intelligent affection for him that was
obvious to all. The rest is the boy’s story, and it
was firmly believed in the township. One day he
returned to his home, dripping-wet after a long
ride, and said that he had fallen into the river
and been dragged out by the pony when on the
point of drowning. The horse, he said, deliber-
ately plunged into the water, swain to him, seized
him by the arm, and dragged him back to shore,
landing him well up on the bank. Writer has
often seen the pony follow his master into the
water of a quarry-hole when the boy was bathing.
And, by the way, that pound-keeper’s son was not
a bit less brutal than boys usually are with a horse
after the novelty of ownership has worn off.
• * * •
A Waifcaki North (M.L.) farmer recently
rounded up a lot of cattle trespassing on his land,
and drove them into a pound, whereon he was
promptly sued on the ground that “nearest
pound ” meant nearest by road, and not nearest as
the crow flies. £25 damages. Later on lie rounded
up another lot of trespassers, valiantly drove them
to the pound whither the court had ruled he should
have taken the first lot, and went home, shaking
hands with himself. Sued again for damages f< r
wrongful impounding, he produced the court’s
ruling, only to be politely informed than mean-
while a Judge had ruled that “ nearest pound ”
meant nearest as the crow flies, even if it meant a
detour of 500 miles by road to get to a pound five
miles away.

S.S. : “ Met a man from East Gippsland
the other day, who told me that the country was
so steep in the part he had just come from—
Gibbo Ranges—that the residents have to take
their horses 10 miles to have a roll. One peak
there is so high that the sun cannot go over it,
and always goes round. Natives bred in this
locality cannot walk on level ground. Further-
more, it rains so hard up there that people often
get drowned through standing out . in it.
The greatest difficulty the settlers experience
is in building fences that will keep their cattle
from falling off the selections. The snow is
troublesome too at times. Last winter a man
named Ephraim Casley fell into a drift and was
not recovered for a fortnight. He would have
been smothered had he not worn a respirator, says
the man from East Gippsland.”

O.D. (Monaro) : “ It may not be generally
known that that purely Australian quadruped, or
sextuned, ‘ the bull-dog ant,’ can use his business
end for days after decapitation, and can kill a
blowfly in one act four days after being cut in
two.”
* * * *
Nareen : “ Despite improved stock-routes
and special rates for railing starving stock, the
drought-stricken squatter of to-day is a resource-
less creature compared with the pioneer grazier.
Instance old M'Thustle, of Uiawulkey : Fought a
drought for two years, in the early ’7o’s, and never
admitted, till he had cut his last patch of scrub,
that things were beginning to look serious. Then
went off to.get men for skinning and boiling-down,
but having, at the nearest town, purchased the
effects of a recently-deceased eye-specialist, he got
a sudden inspiration, which sent him back to the
station as fast as horses could travel. Every jum-
buck was then supplied with a pair of green
goggles, with the result that it discovered fresh
fields and pastures new in what was previously a
parching wilderness. Mac. thus transformed his
staggerers into fair stores within six weeks, and,
when the drought broke, some months later, his
drafts for Flemington nicked the market at famine
prices. Mac.’s optical experiment, moreover, had
another good effect, for, whenever a crow made
an attempt on the eyes of a bogged sheep, that
bird retired sorely wounded in mind and beak ;
and hence it is that, although MThustle and his
methods have long since passed away, the sheep
on that run are immune from crow-bite even to
this day.”

Nicky Noubray : “Re “Tomahawk’s”
‘ Aboriginalities ’ par. (26/3/98) about jackass and
eel. On the Upper Hunter River, some time ago,
I saw a jackass take out of the river a live 2ft. eel,
fly with it into a tree, kill it by dropping it to the
ground, and then fly off with it into the bush.
The water from which the eel was taken was fully
3ft. deep, but I presume the victim had been
swimming near the surface. As I had never heard
of jackasses killing fish of any kind, I was a very
interested spectator.”
Via : u Re the kanaka murdered and muti-
lated by blacks at Cardwell, N.Q. Cannibalism
was scarcely the motive, as a piece was cut out of
the thigh only. Tt is from there that the bone,
so potent in the ‘ bone-pointing ’ rites, is obtained.
This mysterious and deadly rite is common to all
Q. aboriginals. A bone pointed at a nigger by an
avenger is as sure a mark of death as if the knife
was at his throat.”

* * * *
When the homeward-bound Brasseys were
doing their recent visit to Sandstralia, they were
taken out into the bush to see the big Denmark
saw-mills, near Albany, and the usual thing hap-
pened. The great episode was to be the hauling
of a colossal 50-foot “ karri ” log down a steep
piece of road by the crack team and driver of the
district. The driver had been specially instructed
not to swear in the hearing of the ancient mariner
and his family, so the team and the log and Bill
came rolling steadily down the incline in a
strange atmosphere of Sabbath calm. Then the
front wheels struck a soft spot and began to sink.
A squall of sudden blasphemy and a prompt on-
slaught with the whip would have got them over
the bad place in no time, but Bill couldn’t swear lest
Brassey should hear, and he couldn't tear pieces
of hide off the team lest they should blow into the
ladies’ back-hair, so the disgraced team was bogged
and came to a stop, while the humiliated Bill
longed to go behind a jarrah-tree and there weep
tears of beer and tobacco and bush-rum and rage
and blanky. The party went away under the
impression that Brassey could have driven that
team much better himself. When it was out of
sight Bill let himself loose, and leaped eight feet
in the air, with a blood-curdling execration that
made the welkin ring, and the team got the big
log shifted in 40secs. But the show was spoiled.
* • « •
Dear Bulletin, —Mistake to think that a
snake (or, anyhow, a Kanakaland one) with a
broken back is done for. Some scrub-cutters here
left a big 16ft. carpet snake with its back broken,
but next day on going to work they saw what
they at first took to be a large rope stretched from
tree to tree. It was the snake. He had taken a
half-hitch with tail and head round two saplings
about 12ft. apart. He was left hanging and
stayed there for a month until his backbone knit,
when he departed sound and whole.— No Tozer.
« * * *
Bananaland Govt, having taken the Nor-
thern nigger under its special protection, you can’t
get King Billy to chop a bit of wood or carry a
few buckets of water unless you sign and stamp a
special aboriginal agreement form. The result of
tiiis in certain quarters has been that white resi-
dents won’t engage aboriginals at all, and the
poor nigs are on the verge of starvation. In other
localities the whites hire the blacks just the same,
and tell the officials to go-ahead. And, appar-
ently, the authorities daren’t prosecute.

“Along the mail-road from Albany to
Perth, made by convicts in the Penal days,” writes
a Bulletin correspondent, “ are scores of old-time
settlers to whom the influx of t’othersiders to the
goldfields has brought no change. They live as
they did 20 years ago, almost unconscious of the
progress of the rest of the world. Nine out of
every ten of the young folk have never been 30
miles from home, and are just about as'un-
sophisticated as they make ’em. In some of the
settlements the kangaroo-butcher ranks next in
importance-to the beer-vendor, and hawks his
supply of marsupial with as much conceit as if he
were the firm of Bennett and Woolcock under one
hat. Kangaroo-steak was served for breakfast
during a visit the writer recently made to a
Southern hotel.”

F.O. : “Near Ballarat, the other day,
met a man driving a starved, dilapidated hope
attached to an antiquated spring-cart containing
some blankets, a tent, an old mattrass, and some
cooking requisites. Upon these things sat the
driver, while behind walked a haggard-looking
woman and a thin, wild-eyed girl of about 12.
The man pulled up and hailed me as I approached.
‘ Got a match on yer, boss? ’ I handed him my
box. ‘Going far,’ I asked. ‘P’raps. Were
traveilin’.’ ‘No room for the women-folk up
there ? ’ I asked. ‘ They look as if they’ve had
quite enough walking exercise.’ ‘Well, yer see,
boss,’ he answered, after lighting a stumpy clay
and handing back the box, ‘ if the missus an the
gel wus to git up I’d have ter stump-it meself.
This old crock couldn’t never dror the lot of us
Git up, hoss !’”

S.M. Herald struck a great and charac-
teristic idea the other day, when news was
received that America and Spain had begun war.
One imagines the scene in the sanctum. Editor :
“ This is a big; thing : we ought to have something
special about it.” Sub.: “ Yes, we ought to dish
the Telegraph somehow. But how ?’’ Editor
(suddenly illuminated): “Ha ! I have it ! Send
a first-class man round to .” Sub. (eagerly):
“Military and naval heads?” Editor (indignantly):
“Great Abraham ! no ! To the leading parsons,
of course.” And the Herald did—it collected
the ignorant commonplaces of reverends of all de-
nominations, and joyously printed them—a whole
half-oolumn’s worth ! Dear old Granny !

A. Shadier, whose name has been synony-
mous with the best and most wholesome bread in
Sydney and suburbs for nearly fifty years, has
still further earned the blessings of his fellow
citizens by opening a handsome and commodious
refreshment room at Manly. The new place is a
Charming resort for ladies, with its delicious
bread, confectionery, tea, &c. Shadler’s business
comprises three factories—one in Sydney, a second
in North Sydney, and a third in Ashfield ; and it
employs very close on 100 bakers in addition to a
numerous staff of men, horses and carts. At least
40,000 of Sydney’s population oat Shadler’s bread
every day, with the result that good digestion
follows appetite and health follows both.
The firm by its perseverance, punctuality,
cleanliness, and other virtues, continually
progresses ; and owns the property and
buildings of its manufacturing establishments
in Sydney and suburbs. This is an age when the
baker of bread must be a master in his business
to please his customers, and Shadlers are able to
provide suitable and nutritious bread for the
robust man, the delicate woman, and the tender
child. Dyspeptics and indigestion-sufferers should
obtain Shadlers’ special bread ; benefit follows at
once. Mr. A. Shadier, the founder of this big
firm, retired from business some two years ago,
and his son, Mi’. A. C. Shadier, the present pro-
prietor, with his well-known activity and thorough
and constant attention to business, promises a still
further 50 years of prosperity.*

Grouzelle is a word destined to become
celebrated in every household in N.S.W.
Grouzelle signifies all that is best and newest in
the photographic art of the world. Grouzelle’s
new studios in George-street, Sydney, constitute
a revolution in competitive photography. From
the street you see a handsome staircase beauti-
fully carpeted, leading to a landing hung with the
rarest specimens of photographic skill, and re-
lieved here and there by evergreens and charming
decorations. A few steps and the great reception-
room comes into view with its unique drawing-
room furniture, its artistic effects, its superb
photographic views : while the soft light coming
through the colored windows adds beauty to the
palms, the leaves of which fall gracefully towards
the rich carpeting. A small flight of stairs leads
to the dressing-room and the studio. These
dressing-rooms have every qualification that the
most exacting could ask, and a lady-attend-
anb is employed to give the gentler sex
any assistance that may be desired ; and
throughout these dressing-rooms there is the same
conscientious and praiseworthy attention to de-
tail, the same comfort, the same adaptation
to the needs of the clients, and the same general
harmony of surrounding to soothe and chaim the
mind of the visitor. If a lady cannot make her-
self look well in these dressing-rooms, she must for
ever despair of being able to do so. The studio is
a revelation: the lighting and contrivances for
posing being equalled only by the instantaneous
and perfect working of the camera—a splendid
model imported with all its accessories from Paris.
And the operator at the camera is, compared with
others, an artist, and one who secured a unique
reputation in Melbourne before he came to
Sydney.*

“ Softly ” :—As to the “ cow-
ardice ”of Australian blacks. Years
ago, when Webster (I think) was in
charge of Mossgiel station, the blacks
and their dogs were annoying the
station people, and refused to go
when repeatedly ordered off. Web-
ster as a last resource took his gun
and proceeded to the camp (in hopes,
I suppose, to scare them), and or-
dered them to clear out or he would
shoot their dogs. Scotty, a kind of
leader, said, “No; we won’t go.”
“If you don’t,” said Webster in a
rage, “ I will shoot you ! ” “ All
right,” replied Scotty, coolly ; “ you
shoot; that one only kill one feller ;
then you look out.” Webster re-
turned without any scalps. More
recently, at Willandra station,
“ Whyco Tommy’s” dog had been
worrying sheep, and one of the
overseers was proceeding to the camp
to shoot it. Tommy met him and
inquired where he was going, in not
too civ ’ a tone. “To the camp to
shoot jour dog.” Tommy had a
waddy in his hand, and drew a line
on the ground and stood at the end
of it. “ Now, you walk over that
mark ! ” said he. The overseer did
not cross the line. Of course, I don’t
mean to say that there are no cow-
ards among the blacks—only that
the proportion is not greater than
amongst the whites. As to blacks’
“ thievish propensities,” I was at
Moolah about 18 years ago ; and
while at work my tent was unpro-
tected. There were over 100 blacks
there, all told, and I worked there
nearly two years and had nothing
stolen except a rug. That I found in the tent of
an alleged white man to whom I had been of con-
siderable service.

and in no case will MS. be returned unless stamps
(of any province) accompany.

You say only very rich men could be elected to the

“ The Swiss call themselves free, but the
Sun gendered a knot of petty tyrants out of the
carcase of the Oppressor.”— Goethe.
C. Mitchell’s “ Newspaper Press Direc-
tory ” for 1898, just published, impresses The
Bulletin as the best book of the kind extant.
The Australasian section is particularly full and
valuable.
The English papers take from Kalgoorlie
Herald an account of the alleged horrible cruelty
with which a Maitland River black-boy was
branded all over the back with a hot iron, and
then pickled with salt fired into him from a gun.
What an all-round look-in would be the produc-
tion and exhibition of the victim ! Sympathy and
notoriety for our badly-damaged black brother;
shekels galore for his exploiters.
“ THE TEMPERED WIND."
“ That woman seems to have lots of cheek."
“ Yes: and she needs it. Don’t you sec how little
clothes she’s got?”
Other old Sydney taverns : Currency
Lass, Sir Maurice O’Connell, Governor Bourke,
Three Tuns, Dog and Duck, Native Youth, Emu
Inn, Cocked Hat, Commodore, Man o’-War, and
Country Lass. At Parramatta the Bed Cow was
celebrated from the thirties up to the sixties as
the honeymoon resort of well-to-do Sydney couples.
A tavern on the “ Rocks ” kept by Bullivant, one
time public flagellator, was probably the first
licensed house in Australia.
The next municipal difference of opinion
between Ballarats East and West is pretty certain
to produce bloodshed. Mayor Shoppee, the
Western potentate, in his manifesto re the late
Easter encampment, says: “ It has done much for
•our city, besides imbuing our youth with a proper
military and warlike enthusiasm, which will yet
add to our defence against a foreign foe, and upon
which I may justly congratulate the commandant
mid his officers and the citizens of Ballarat.” A
warlike enthusiasm for defence must presently de-
velop a furious spirit of attack. Shoppee, as every-
body in Ballarat —West or East—can testify, is
not the sort of man who, having put his hand to
the sword, will wait for an enemy to turn up.
He’s a belligerent at heart, and when the citizens
aforesaid feel ready to get bathed in blood they
will only need to ring for Shoppee. Meantime, he
is teaching the young idea how to shoot.
Tassy : “Knew a woman who cured her
husband—a bad case—of drinking. Instead of
keeping him out in the cold, and bombarding him
with boots and hairbrushes, going home to mother,
and all the rest of it, this angel kept his slippers
warm, with a night-cap if he was home in reason-
able time; went to bed if he wasn’t; and next
day fed him up with broths, occasional pick-me-
ups, and so on, and coddled him until he was
steady enough to go to work. He told me, after a
big drunk, that he felt so mean at his wife’s treat-
ment that he had sworn never to get drunk again ;
and as long as I knew him he didn’t.”

The youth of Ballarat.
They were indolent and fat,
And n«t at all inclined to martial prattle,
Till some soldiers of the Queen
Came and camped upon the scene
And made the city famous for its Battle.
They haven’t eaten buns
Since the smell of roaring guns
Distracted their attention from the larder ;
Plum pudden lost its charms
When the trumpet call to arms
Had filled ’em up with military ardour.
They onetime would chyike
Valiant Shoppee on his bike,
Those grinning, irrepressible rapscallions.
They grin at him no more
Since the sturdy Mayor-o’-war
Went biking in command of his battalions.
Their craving, day and night,
Is for foreign foes to fight,
Each boy is a brigade of Greater Britons ;
And every lady cat,
In the town of Ballarat
Has been slaughtered whilst provisioning her kittens.
Should the local thirst for gore
Seek relief in civil war
And a list of slain be sent along as “ copy,”
’Twill be headed, let us pray,
By the hero of the day,
Then the “ Dead March ” we will play for good old
Shoppee.

AUSTRALIA’S GLORIOUS FOURTH.

Public Notice 3.
Public Service Board,
60, Young-street,
Sydney, 21st April, 1898.
A COMPETITIVE Examination of Candidates foi
Junior Clerkships in the Public Service of the
Colony will be held in Sydney, and at various Country
centres, which will be fixed as far as possible to suit
the convenience of candidates, on the 27th June, and
four following days. The estimated number of appoint-
ments to be made is 50. Candidates must not be less than
16 nor more than 21 years of age on the first day of exam-
ination, of good moral character, and in sound health.
Persons already employed in the General Divisions of the
Public Service will be admitted to the examination, pro-
vided their age does not exceed thirty years. The sub-
jects in which Candidates will be examined are : —Hand-
writing, English (Dictation, Composition, Precis writing,
Parsing, Analysis, and derivation of words), Arithmetic
(full course), Geometry (Euclid, first Book), Geography
(outlines of physical and political geography, and the
geography of the Australasian Colonies in moderate
detail), English History from the Middle of the Eighteen
Century to the present time, especially with reference to
the Colonies and Dependencies. Candidates will also be
allowed to take any two, but not more than two, of the
following subjects :—Mathematics, English, a prescribed
Author, viz. Tennyson’s “ Gareth and Lynette” (Mac-
Millan), Latin, French, German, Elementary Chemisty,
and Elementary Geology. Application to attend the
examination must be made on a form for the purpose,
which can be obtained at (he office of the Board, and
must reach the undersigned not later than the Ist June.
Candidates will be required to pay an examination fee of
10s., which must be lodged with a Cierk of Petty Sessions
or the Treasury, and the receipt therefor forwarded to
the Secretary of the Board with the form of application.
A list of registered candidates with centres of examination
will he published in the Government Gazette, after the
time for the receipt of applications has expired, and can-
didates will also be advised direct of the place at which
they will be examined.
The Regulations and copies of the papers set at the
last examination can he obtained at the Government
Printing Office, and the principal booksellers.
Bv order of the Board,
J. W. HOLLIMAN. Secretary.
9 a 70.
pUBLIC APOLOGY.
Perth, April 7th, 1898.
F. C. B. VOSPER, Esq., M.L.A., Perth.
Sir, —Referring to certain remarks made by me at the
fortnightly meeting of the Perth City Council, held on the
25th of March last, I think it only due to you to express
my regret that in the heat of the moment I used epithets
referring to yourself, which, in my calmer moments, I cer-
tainly should not have done. No doubt some of the
words hastily used by me bear on their face the construc-
tion that I considered you were the originator of, or a
party to, the late disgraceful riot at Kalgoorlie ; and,
therefore, to make matters clear, I desire to state that in
my opinion the riot was not caused by you personally or
at your instigation ; and I therefore withdraw the accusa-
tions I hastily made against you, and again beg to express
my regret that, in the heat of the moment, I should have
been betrayed into the expressions above referred to.
Yours faithfully 7,
T. QUINLAN.
