POLITICAL
POINTS
The new Q. Ministry—Byrnes, Philp and
eo. ! 1
Over 80 per cent, of N.S.W. country
newspapers favor the Convention Bill.
Premier .Reid’s attitude to Federation—
Willing to wound, and yet afraid to strike,
Just hint a fault, and hesitate dislike.
— Pope.
Justice Hood’s decision in the Bulimba
Sunday-work appeal ease excludes, for the first
time by Supreme Court authority, the seaman
mass from the general body legislated for as
laborers.”
Incautious remark by Sydney D.T. :
Mr. Want and Mr. Millen, M.P., will next Tuesday'
attack Bathurst, which is popularly supposed in Sydney
to be a hotbed of Federation.
“ A straw shows ! ”
Special privileges die hard. Apropos,
Tattersall * influence in Tasmania is un-
derstood to be dead against Federation. Tas.
would lose perhaps £20,000 per annum by the
Federal abolition of the consultation person if he
is abolished.
Governor Buxton (S.A.) is going to Hing-
land to attend to “ urgent private affairs.” One
reason why we should produce our own teetotal
Governors is that they wouldn t have to rush away
to the otlmr end of the earth to look after their
brewery dividends.
The know-alls say that Premier Tomjoe
Byrnes offered a portfolio to Andy Dawson,
Labor M.L.A. for Charters Towers. Both Byrnes
and Dawson are under 40 ;of the soil; and the
smartest men in their respective parties. But
Dawson “ didn’t see his way.”
Federation has put all parties in Victoria
at sixes and sevens. Trenwith against the Trades
Hall, the latter playing speaks with the Arm ; the
Aue against its quondam love, Protectionist
Maimer ; Freetrader Max Hirsch on the same side
as Iren with - but the big men (bar one) are all on
one side.
A Melbourne paper has a yarn about a
boat-load of excursionists bursting into enthusias-
tic cheers at the sight of a star-spangled flag hung
out on an anchor' d vessel. The people thought it
was the American flag, but a sorrowful Australian
moral lies in the fact that it was really the Aus-
tralian trig.
Premier Braddon, who ought to know
something about the matter, telegraphs to the
effect that Coghlan’s alarming theori s about the
insolvency of Tasmania under the Federal Bill
* r, “ hopeless bunkum. Braddon, as a fine old
British gentleman, doesn’t say “ bunkum,” but he
plainly means it.
To-morrow (Friday) is the last day on
which the N.S.AV. citizen who is not yet on the
rolls can get his elector’s right with a view to
voting at the Federal Referendum. The voter who
doesn’t obtain his document by to-morrow may
allow his attention to wander from the contro-
versy thereafter, as he is out of the running.
The Federation fight between the Sydney
dailies is “ nuts ” to those of their staffs who have
been accustomed to Melb. press-work. Henry
Gullett, the Herald’s acting editor, long used to
the Argus-Age feud, is throwing himself into the
fray with a vim.that must make the Tory readers
of Granny rub their eyes with astonishment.
Premier Kingston (S.A.) objects to de-
partmental heads fixing up postal and other con-
ferences at which Ministers appear but as lay
figures to record the decisions of their subordin-
ates, and he affirms that directly one conference
is over the permanent officials begin to hunt round
for subjects to be discussed at another interpro-
vmciaJ meeting.
Next session of Q. Parliament will see the
proposal of several land-grant railways, notably
the Normanton-Cloncurry line—a very big thing
indeed. As there will be considerable pickings in
this, it will probably go through. The easy
passage of the ChillagOH line will be bandied about
and another slice of N.Q. will fall into the hands
of the all-powerful syndicate.
S.A. Labor-party has at last taken the
plunge, and organised an anti-Federation league.
Such famous politicians as Ebenezer Ward and
loud-voiced ex-Mayor Cohen threaten to assist.
Cohen at one time represented N. Adelaide in
S.A. Assembly, and is one of the numerous past
political failures who have temporarily revived
through the Federation boom.
Westralia’s population still mounts—a
net gain of COG 3 for the first quarter of the year.
But the female increase is still small—lB24 as
against 4581 males—though the excess of births
over deaths is for males 74 as against females 208.
The nett increase is, however, very different from
those for the corresponding quarters of ’OG and
’97 —15,870 and 14,074 respectively.
Some Q. Ministerialists who openly ex-
Sress no confidence in Premier Byrnes : Petrie,
I‘Master, Leahy, Fraser, M‘Doiiald-Paterson’
Curtis, Hamilton, Grimes, Thorn, Bell, Batters-
by and Budges. Enough dissentients to burst up
the Government if they had the courage to vote
asthe.v think. But most Bananaland politicians
are either bank-tied or easily bullied.
John Leahy, M.L.A., is the saddest man
in Leperland. He has been mentioned—with
monotonous regularity—for every Ministerial
vacancy in the past three years, and now, in the
very summer of his hopes, his expectations are
shattered by Tomjoe. Premier Byrnes, in trying
to fight down the ancient and carefully-dissemi-
nated impression that he is a R.C. first and a
Queenslander afterwards, will doubtless leave his
co-religionists severely "lone when scattering
perks and patronage.
Federated Australia's
Governor-General will, after
all, be merely a historical
resurrection. The official title
of the Governor of N.S.W.
was for many years “Governor-
General,” and Imperial ordi-
nances elaborately laid down
that he took precedence over
all provincial Governors when-
ever he entered their terri-
tories. Title, claim, prece-
dence and all, however, quietly
died out, and now Hampden in
Maoriland is officially no more
portentous than is Kanfurly in
N.S.W.
In Maoriland, a poli-
tician must be six months out
of Parliament before he can
be appointed to any perman-
ent public billet. This is in-
tended to prevent Ministers
appointing themselves to the
bench and similar pleasant
offices. The rumor goes round
that Robert Stout really left
polities so as to qualify for the
Chief Justice’s billet, when the
present occupant, who is very
old and feeble, drops off.
Judge Prendergast is 70 now ;
he has sat on the bench for
23 years, and Justice has to
roar in his ear to make him
hear anything.
A reform the need of
which becomes every day more
scandalously apparent in N.S.
W.is the prohibition from prac-
tice before stipendiary magis-
trates of any lawyer who is also
a member of Parliament. How-
ever upright an S.M. maybe,
hi-’s only human after all, and,
until he is made officially as in-
dependent of Parliament as
are the J edges, he should not
be exposed to such an obvious
temptation to partiality.
The Telegraph's financial editor, still
.weeping bitterly about the alleged surplus which
is to be such an embarrassment to N.S.W. under
Federation, suggests tl.at it would probably be
got rid of by a big increase of civil-service salaries.
Apparently not a soul except The Bulletin can
absorb the simple idea of enacting that there shall
be no more borrowing, and that the blessed sur-
plus shall be paid into a Public Works Fund in
order to built! the new line to Broken Hill—the
one that can t be built out of borrowed monev be-
cause it won’t pay the needful 3 per cent.—and to
finish the Sydney drains, and other things.
Four of the members of the Bananaland
Cabinet (Philp, Dalrymple, Chataway and Mur-
ray) have several times avowed their faith in
Separation. Did they note the following passage
in Premier Byrnes’ Warwick utterance:—
The Govt, will not in any way depart from the policy of
its predecessors in regard to the partition of the province.
Curious anomaly ! Four erstwhile professed
Separation advocates in an anti-Separation
Cabinet an actual majority for autonomy.
The pillars of Separation posing as the buttresses
of anti-Separation. What asses Northern and
Central Separationists must be to allow themselves
to be gulled by a quartette of frauds so simply
transparent!
The Dudley Flinn and Stephen
Barker party (Vic.) has raised funds
to oppose the Convention Bill. These
resources, at time of writing, consisted
of at least 30s. —mostly contributed
by Ben Tillett.
A HOPELESS CASE .
HOSPITAL NURSE (to groanful patient): “Cheer
up, thirteen: when the doctor comes he'll soon
put you right."
No. 13: “I’m afraid not—I’ve been in a lot of
hospitals afore I came here."
H. N. : “What did the doctor say was the matter
with you? ”
No. 13 (impressively): “They all said as how it
was the very worst case of malingerin' they’d
ever known."
ARTLESS.
“Please, sir, will you help the fallen?"
Queensland has at present half-a-dozen
talked-about ex-Premiers Herbert, Douglas,
Griffith, MTlwraith, Nelson, Morehead—facing
life in different parts of the world. Herbert, the
ancient of the lot, stands open for any little tem-
porary billet which may fall vacant in the repre-
sentation of Australia in England ; Douglas con-
trols a motley empire of browns, yellows and
dubious whites ; Griffith, as Q. Chief Justice, has
managed the cosiest quarters, though Nelson,
considering his constitutional lethargy, has done
very well; Morehead and MTlwraith are sick at
present, but they talk fight, and see what they
take to be victory ahead.
The Maoriland Old-Age Pension Bill is to
be re-introduced in the coming session, and if the
House of Nominees rejects it Seddon proposes to
go to the country and demand power to wipe the
floor with that institution. If Digger Dick would
try the experiment of an elective paid Council,
with no property qualification for members, chosen
by manhood and womanhood suffrage with the
whole province as one electorate, make the two
Houses dissolvable together, and adopt the joint
sitting in case of disagreement, then Maoriland
would have the most logical and democratic con-
stitution on earth, and Digger Dick would get a
huT" monument some day as the builder of it.
The vitality of the Separation ques-
tion in Queensland is largely due to the
masterful manner in which the Govt.,
standing up to its neck in Southern in-
terests, and having at the same time two
Ministerial tentacles in the Northern
hair, sells Central lands in large
areas and spends the proceeds
in general expenditure. If
Central Queensland doesn’t
get some sort of autonomy
soon, there will be very
little CQ. left unsold.
In ’BBM‘llwraith brought
in a Decentralisation Bill
under which each division
was to receive its own ter-
ritorial revenue. The Bill
passed its second
reading, but MTI-
- conveni-
ent luck made
him fall ill in time
to prevent its be-
ing carried fur-
ther. Griffith
championed the
same idea, but he
forgot to act on
hi.s convictions when
■ he got the chance. The
present Minister for
Lands, lawyer Foxt o n,
swears that he doesn’t believe
in large alienations of land, but
he continues making them all the
same. So the process of skinning
Central Queensland goes on. “ Be-
tween the upper and nether millstones ” ?
The nether stone is always the harder.
MacLaurin, M.L.C., orating in the pro-
vincialist interest at Sydney :
Oeorsre Turner, the other day, told the people in
Melbourne it was nearly certain that the seat of the
Federal Government would be in Victoria.
To which the short and simple reply is that
Premier Turner said nothing of the kind and
nothing resembling it. This new bogy is brother
to Want’s statements that N.S.W. coal is shut
out of Victoria, and that the southern delegates
‘ kicked ” the referendum out of the Federation
Bill when there was never any referendum in it;
and John Norton’s fiction about how the other
provinces are either stationary or decreasing in
population ; and Lyne’s ghost about the unchange-
ableness of the Constitution ; and Hughes’s
taradiddle about the Federal Senate being exempt
from dissolution in case of deadlocks till the other
House had been dissolved and re-elected ; and all
the other more or less premeditated inaccuracy of
the Cabbage-Tree Mob.
Melbourne tram employes are in a bad
way. The co. persists in working the men
as long as it dern pleases, despite the fact
that the Act which created its monopoly pro-
vides that the employes are to be worked on the
eight-hour system, and are to be paid specially for
overtime ; with a £5 penalty for every breach of
this particular section. A beautiful object-lesson
concerning the private control of public services.
The 00. has got a cow to milk for a certain time —
and it is going to milk that cow all day everyday.
John Murray, M.L.A., who has" just
joined Q. Ministry, is a bright sample of the con-
scienceless opportunist and tricky politician. For
some time he has emulated the Dicksonian r6le of
caustic but kindly critic of Govt. ; and a portfolio
ha,s been dropped into his mouth just as in
Dickson’s case. Addressing his constituents
lately he expressed a blazing desire for a strong
Opposition, promising to join it when formed.
His chief claims to distinction are chronic
laziness, antiquated ideas and whole-souled scorn
for Glassey and the Laborists. He attends about
six sittings of Parliament during each whole
session, and begins all his speeches with the
declaration that his valuable time is being wasted
listening to superfluous discussion. Rumour
alleges that Murray’s valuable time is mostly
spent in bed.
Premier Reid has found an excuse to
crawl down from his lofty Freetrade attitude with
regard to the sugar industry, and has half-pro-
mised to leave on a duty of £3 per ton. The
wildly irrelevant reason assigned is that Federa-
tion is coming. The Tklegkaph actually encour-
ages Reid in this awful falling away from grace,
and both Reid and his organ agree to drop all
mention of the fact that the .£3 protective duty
will bring in a good deal of revenue to the Free-
trade Treasury, and fetch a good many votes to
the Freetrade cause at next election, and probably
save Reid’s billet. Meanwhile, even if Federa-
tion and all-round Protection are coming, no one
seems to explain why N.S. W. shouldn’t enjoy the
blessing of Freetrade while it can. If the curse of
Protection is coming, why should Reid want t»
hasten it along?
S.S. (from Melb.) : “ The outbreak of the
Cuban war serves to give us an idea of the
strength of Australia’s enthusiasm for her great
cause, Federation. What has become of
Federation since the dailies hjegan to run
to large frantic headlines about America and
Spain ? You turn over the fifth page of your
morning organ of public opinion, and find the
most tremendous event in Australian history
nestling away amongst the advts., with an
apologetic headline, and an ‘ ’opes I don’t intrude ’
atmosphere all over it. I believe that if we get
federation it will be because the Australian
elector does not give a blanky, and if we don’t get
it it will be because the same tired person does not
give a blanky blank. Meanwhile, lam not sure
that David Syme, Jack Want, and the Trades
Hall are not at the bottom of this Cuban war.”
Writes “Gumleaf” :
Federation has sent the Vic. Labor-party to smash. It
has divided the sheep from the goats. The division was
sure to come one day. Brains such as Trenwith’s and
crankiness such as Dr. Maloney's could not continue long
to move along the same line ; and if they had not parted
over Federation they would over some other question
where Trenwith’s intelligence refused to give in to the
other fellow's nonsense. Maloney regards the party ai
absolutely dead, hut the more wily Hancock only admits
that it is a hit “ huniptv.” Both agree, however, that the
cause of the trouble in each case is what they are pleased
to call “ Trenwith's desertion”—which is a nice admis-
sion. There is a fearsome uncertainty as to the attitude
which the secretary of the party, Bromley, will ultimately
take up on the question. It is said that when the figures
are up he will be found on Trenwith’s side. Bromley is
now never to be seen about the Trades Hall, and rarely
attends the meetings of the council. Between him and
the dismal secretary of the Trndes Hall, ex-M.L.A. Bar-
rett, there has sprung up a deadly feud.
Sydney Telegraph, in its issue of 29th
April, announced triumphantly that Federation
was now practically dead in N.S.W., and its
corpse had been repeatedly killed to make it
deader, and “ the referendum will be a pro-
nounced, unmistakeable declaration that the bill
is impossible.” Under these circumstances the
Telegraph would probably like to call Parliament
together immediately and pass an Act abolishing
the infamous 80,000 minimum. The way things
are fixed, if the Federal party raises 79 999 votes
in favor of the Constitution, and there isn’t a
single vote against it, still the side which didn’t
get one single vote wins. If the Provincialist
crowd is the majority, as the Telegraph brags it
is, then it doesn’t need to stack the cards like
this, and annul the votes of one-third of the elec-
tors, and deliberately disfranchise an immense
proportion of the people, and abolish the sacred
principle of majority rule which it is professedly
so anxious to preserve—all in order to choke its
narrow provincial creed down the throats of the
people by a minority vote. A crowd which says
it has a majority, and yet insists on 80,000 start in
the voting, is either a liar or a glutton.

Premier Kingston absolutely refused to let
either the S.A. military or naval commandant
open his lips on the subject of the war. He corn
aiders it indecent for the commanding officers in
the other provinces to be “yap-yapping” on the
subject upon their own responsibility.
When Coghlan’s Federal finance estimates
were first divulged by Premier Reid, B. R. Wise
requested, and afterwards demanded, that Mr.
Coghlan should supply the bases of his calcula-
tions. This Coghlan was willing to do, but Reid
wriggled out of giving the necessary authority.
Had Coghlan . and Wise then met on the basis
figures a national muddle might have been
avoided.
The most solemn of the arguments against
the Commonwealth Bill, addressed to the N.S.W.
public now, were addressed by the supporters of
the old irresponsible Government when the pre-
sent N.S.W. Constitution was proposed. The
people were then exhorted not to jump in the
were assured that, under the pretence
Vln £. th e . m a larger freedom, the framers of
the Constitution were cunningly forging fetters to
enslave them ; and so on.
. Premier Seddon (of Maoriland) declares
m sec terms that the proposed .£2,000,000 loan is
a thin invention of the enemy, and in view of the
*400,000 surplus he will keep on building public
works outof revenue till further notice. Also, Sed-
don doesn't propose to reduce taxation on account of
the surplus, because the surplus, and a great deal
more, is all wanted for railway-building; and to
vxn, UOe ta * ati , on wou ld only mean more loans.
W hen will there be an Australian Premier with
grit and honesty enough to talk like this?
The Yankee coal-ship Saranac, recently
captured by the Spaniards, shipped at Newcastle,
N.S.W. a orew of 1 Maorilander, 4 Yankees, 2
Germans, 1 Austrian, 1 Norwegian, 1 Swede, and
1 Russian.
The one humorous feature of Melbourne
’Erald is the weekly puff for its blood relation, as
follows: “ The country edition of The Weekly
Times was published this morning, and we have a
copy for perusal. Our up-to-date contemporary
may be congratulated on its pages of pictures this
week. ”
The virtue of the counsel, “ keep pegging
away,” was never better exemplified in the field
of politics than by Edward Knox, of the Colonial
Sugar Refining Co. In season and out of season
Knox prodded Premier Reid over the N.S.W.
sugar duties, till now at last Premier Reid makes
a hole in his Freetrade policy and invites Protec-
tionist light to twinkle in.
The Federation movement has done one
good thing at least in Queensland. Bananaland
planters, the most stubbornly Conservative race
in Australia, have come at last to see and, in
many cases, openly to acknowledge, that colored
labor must soon be a thing of the past. The pro-
spect of Queensland being shut out from the
sugar-market of the other Federated, or about-to-
be Federated Australias, has brought conviction
to the hearts of even the hide-bound sugar-kings.
If Premier Byrnes and co. don’t hurry along to
fall in line with the movement for a United Aus-
tralia there'll be a bigger “ raising of cane ” even
than usual round about Mackay and Bundaberg.

Billy M'Coombe has had more accidents upon
the cycle track than any other representative sprinter ;
but he has always come up smiling: for more. When the
wobbler has brought M'Coombe down on the cycle-track,
M'Coombe has merely arisen and placed what was left of
him in a cab and adjourned to the nearest hospital for
reconstruction. And while he has been there many
pretty girls have come and smoothed the hair on his
brow, and placed fresh beef-steak upon his eyes, and
taken his hand and pressed it, and hung flowers from the
canopy of his bed. Lately William plugged through hail
and rain and storm for records on his Dunlop-tyred
Swift; but this time the furious elements said “No 1”•
There are many whiskys on the market —
so many that a man who wants to be sure of a
good one has to think some time before choosing.
But lately an infallible guide has come to aid such
thinkers, for the representative of the British
nation—the British House of Commons—has un-
solicited ordered Sir John Power’s whiskey. When
the representative taste of 40,000,000 enlightened
people chooses Sir John Power’s whiskey, who can
ask for any other brand ? #
The recent two-volume naval-fight at
Manila was a pretty fair go as regarded aggregate
tonnage, the seven American ships totalling, like
the 14 Spanish, close on 18,000 tons. The Yankee
ships, again, were certainly superior; while, if
some of the Dons were “very” small and early,
the powerful assistance of the shore batteries made
Uncle Sam’s row a pretty hard one to hoe.
The burning of two of the Spaniards’ ships,
however, the blowing-up of another, and
the sinking and shattering of sundry more,
make it pretty evident that he kept up his
end all right. The “ intense enthusiasm at
Madrid ” at the close of fight the first seems almost
pathetic in view of the fact that the supposed
“taking refuge ’’ was very probably a prepared
part of the American scheme of operations. The
affair, after all, however, can’t count for much
more than a demonstration, since the American
squadron has neither men to occupy Manila, nor
coal to continue long in Philippine waters.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
So Mr. Sam. Hordern, angry at the turn things
have taken with him on the turf, has decided to sell off
his racing-stock and gear—lock, stock, and barrel. Now,
it’s easy to give a man advice, when it isn’t your funeral.
Sam. Hordern likes racing—who doesn’t ? —and, unlike
999,998 men out of a million, he could afford to race
horses for mere pleasure. He should keep on racing—
for pleasure only. If The Bulletin were Sam. Hordern,
it would not sell off. It would say : “ I am a very rich
man, I love the turf, and I am going to run
my horses for the stakes only. From this day forth,
so that my enemies shall have no handle for calumny, I
shall never bet on or against any horse anything more
valuable than a bottle of wine. Every horse nominated
by me shall start when he is fit, and I shall make it my
personal endeavour to see that he runs to win. I shall
have in my employ no man who is not actuated by the
spirit of Humphrey Oxenham, in his horse-owning capa-
city. It shall be my ambition to make my colors as
popular as \V. R. Wilson’s.” If he has the heart to do it,
he could manage it in three years.
* • * *
The Sydney “Shops” are having quite a bad
time of it just now. Canterbury Park, with the object
of frustrating their little game, cut off the telegraph
connection for last week’s pony meeting; and the Full
Court upheld the magistrate’s decision that the principal
betting rendezvous, a right-of-way off Market-street, is
“ a place within the meaning of the Act.” If the police
really mean biz this should close up the tritons as well as
the minnows ; but the past history of Sydney betting
prosecutions shows that the fat men at the game bear
charmed lives.
» * • •
The principal events of the S.A. J.C. meeting,
which begins to-day (Sat.) at Morphetville, S.A., are pro-
voking great interest. The chief race to-day is the
Goodwood Handicap (6 furlongs) in w’hich Olaf and Paul
Pry figure as first favorites, but in the actual race they
may have to succumb to Staffa and Thunder Queen, and
of this pair. Thunder Queen looks very like the winner.
In Adelaide Cup, Royal Admiral is well treated with 7st.
71bs., and if he can beat Mischief, who is now very well,
he should about win.
* • * *
J.D. : “Mick Callaghan, of Ranges Bridge,
Darling Downs, the breeder of Battalion, tells me he sold
him, as a yearling, to a neighbor, who used him as a hack
for over a year, and, when the owner went shearing, he
left the horse with Mick, who won the double at Too-
woomba, and several other races, with him. Then a
Dalby trainer, convinced of the horse’s qualities, went to
Hollymount wool-shed, where the owner was shear-
ing, and offered £3O for the animal, which was accepted,
and you may guess how Mick, who was preparing the
horse for Brisbane races, felt when he got a wire to de-
liver him to the purchaser at such a price. Old Bat-
tailons, the sire, is now at stud. He is a beautiful chest-
nut, 16£ hands high, built in proportion, and resembling
an Irish hunter more than a blood horse. He is still as
frisky as a 2-year-old, though 16 years old last foaling.
* * * »
According to N.Q. Register, Merloolas, the
Sydney Cup winner, was bought from his orginal owner,
a drover, and his partner, Mr. A. J. Bray, of Hughenden,
by G. Gilbert and R. J. Blacklock, for £4O. He had
been under offer, after the loss of a race, for “ £25 and a
receipted hotel bill,” to the landlord of the Sovereign
Hotel, T. Knox, who declined to trade. The horse was ulti-
mately left with Blacklock to train, wirh a right of pur-
chase. Up to date, he has started 11 times, won six, got
placed four times, and was once unplaced.
* • • •
An Easter race-meeting at Narrewarrin (Vic.)
introduced some novelties which might prove attractive
on metropolitan racecourses. The finish of the steeple-
chase was the last fence—the horses had to be judged in
the air, and the decision was—a dead-heat! The course
included a double crossing of the railway line. A high-
jump was contested by two horses, the property of the
same owner. After clearing the bar a few times, one of
the riders announced that it had been agreed to divide
the prize. In the hurdle race four horses came at the
last jump abreast, and, as there was only one panel stand-
ing, the outside horse was forced to" clear the fallen
hurdles. He won—but was disqualified “ for not clearing
the last hurdle.”
» • * *
The youngsters bought by P. Bolger, at Rand-
wick yearling sales, indicate the keenness of the turf-
competition in W.A. between Perth and the goldfields.
Reg. Pell and A. G. Jenkins, of Coolgardie, are on top at
present, with Le Var, as champion, and Lucky Dog for
next season. Perth replies in the persons of Geo. Towton
(who covers also F. C. Monger and A. Hassall) and Tar-
quin ; L. A. Darldt, the N.VY. squatter who ran as “E.
Firebrace ” ; and, possibly Alex. Forrest, whose half-
brother to Amberite, Dryden, has broken down.
• * * *
At Warragul (Vic.) Patrick’s Day meeting,.
Orient was disqualified after winning the trot, it being
alleged he was identical with a well-known performer
called Paddy. The disqualified owner sought and found
the real Paddy in Melbourne, and now the stewards have
reversed their decision and fined the chief witnesses in
the original hearing £2O, £lO, and £6. How booky is
going to fare after parting-up over the original decision
can be guessed, but some allowance must be made for
Patrick’s Day, and a horse named Paddy.
* * • •
The humors of horse nomenclature. Some
years ago, in Maoriland, there raced a very fair performer
named Hairylegs. A gushing young society damsel, of
18 or thereabouts—her first experience on a racecourse
drew this horse in a half crown sweep, and when she
subsequently saw it canter past the post an easy winner
she attracted much scandalised attention by vociferating,
in an excess of delight accompanied by much gesticula-
tion, “ I've got Hairylegs !—l’ve got Hairylegs ! ” “ Have
you ? ” whispered her brother-in-law (an Auckland bank-
manager), “ well, don’t tell everybody about it.”
* * * *
Two M.L. telegraph-clerks got “the run” be-
cause they- won a £2OOO Tattersall prize. The depart-
mental heads had warned the clerks that anyone found
investing in sweeps would be made an example of. These
were the first two caught, and they left cheerfully.
• * • * '
The Australian Christian World is desper-
ately loose in figures. It deplores the fact that at Onka-
paringa (S.A.) races on Easter Monday £21,000 went
through the totalisator although only 16,00(i people were
present, and groans : “ A crowd that can spare more than
£lOO3 per head for gambling,” &c. A plain worldling
might figure it out at £1 6s. 3d. per head, and as likely as.
not there wasn’t over £6OOO in the gamble altogether.
For the tote pays promptly, and the money is planked on
again, and the same sinful quid gets couuted pretty fre-
quently.
• * • *
Westralia is beginning to invest largely in
blood-stock. W. Forrest, who changed his mind about
buying Essex, and let W. R. Wilson snap up the Sydney
colt, has now paid a high price for Wilson’s Ormuz, with
a view to winning W.A. Derby. Remains to be seen
whether Forrest or Wilson has formed the better judg-
ment re the future of Essex and Ormuz.
» • » *
The V.A.T.C. has added another £2OO to the
Debutant Stakes (due Oct. 8), raising it to £l2OO. The
chance of a lucky start for this half-mile rush of green
performers makes it popular with owners.
Simultaneously with the appearance of English
newspaper grumblings at the hitherto commercial selec-
tion of All England Elevens for Australian tours there
comes a statement that Ranji has definitely accepted the
Melbourne O.C. invite to bring out another team in 1900.
The chances are that the “ Indian Prince ” will be a
played-out identity before then. Anyhow, he is very
unlikely to get the free hand which Stoddart had in fix-
ing things up to suit the Australian importers, who pay
“ professionals only” just about half as much remunera-
tion as the Australian amateurs make by their boodle
expeditions. Ranji’s inane criticisms of batsmen and
bowlers on this side should disqualify him for having any
say in the selection or direction of the next visiting
eleven. As a judge of cricket he is obviously inferior to
the average “ expert,” and the average expert in any
branch of sport is a wooden-headed bigot as a rule.
> * • *
An article in current issue of Review of Re-
views, by one Admiral Ranjitsinhji, who led a band of
English buccaneers through Australia last summer, is
worth reading. Met by General Trott, Australian mili-
tary commander, Ranji and co. got several balls in the
stomach, and capitulated after making profuse explana-
tions. Ranji, now with the American force, is hurling
magazine-articles at the Spaniards with deadly effect.
* * * *
In the cable announcing that 700,000 had volun-
teered for the Yankee army, only three names are men-
tioned—Bob Fitzsimmons, Jonel Sullivan, and Jim Cor-
bett. However, their offers are not likely to be accepted,
as Jonel would need a special canteen to travel round
with ; Pompadour Jim would require a valet to part his
back hair; and Bob would be lost without his manager,
his wife, and the baby.
• * • *
■Australia, which a few years since held the box-
ing world safe at all weights, is now without a single top-
notcher, bar Fitzsimmons. Jackson, Slavin, Goddard,
Creedon, Jem Hall, Griffo, and the rest of them are all
back-numbers, and there are none coming on to take
their places. Fitzsimmons is Australia’s only hope, and,
judging from the cinematographe show of his fight with
Corbett, a meeting with Jeffries, Jackson’s conqueror,
would be none too good a thing for Fitz. The fact of
Jackson being -passi given in, Jeffries beat him in a work-
manlike fashion, and although Australia now hears of
Jeffries for the first time as a likely champion, a section
of the American press two years since picked upon him
as a coming world-beater. Jeffries, a giant, muscled like
Sandow, is as lively on his feet as Peter Jackson, and hits
like the kick of a horse. Fitz. took 14 rounds to polish
off Corbett, and then he had a lot of luck to get the win-
ning blow fairly home. The writer would give Jeffries
about half the time to beat Corbett, but, of course, that
line of argument has been proved defective as regards
fighting-men. However, Fitz. is not likely to take on
Jeffries, unless driven to it by hard times.
• * » •
Latest reports describe Jem Mace as at a low
ebb, both in health and pocket, and but for the assistance
of his son, a Plymouth Brother preacher, the old man’s
decliningyears would be hard indeed. When in Australia,
Jem was-cunning as a fox. His hands were completely
knocked up, and it was more painful for him to strike a
blow than for his opponent to receive it. Capt. Mac-
donald, then a handsome man, of soldierly bearing and
magnificent physique, was a boxing enthusiast, whose
house was a meeting-place for the fancy. Larry Foley
used to set-to with the captain, and, to humor him, would
make a good go of it, allowing his patron to get, if any-
thing, the better of the deal. Mace and Foley were at the
time showing at the circus, and, one evening, it was
arranged that Mace and Capt. Macdonald should set-to,
Mace promising Foley not to out-general his vis-a-v fs.
When the night eame, Mace made a “ holy show ” of Mac-
donald, baulking and tapping him when and where he
liked. When it was over, Foley was irate, and reminded
Mace of his promise in rather strong 1 mguage. Mace
merely laughed at him, and said, “My hoy, by the time
you’re my age, you’ll learn to make up in cunning what
you’ve lost in youth. The crowd saw you set-to with
Macdonald and get none the best of it, and, to-night, they
saw me play with him like a child, and will naturally say,
* What sort of a wonder must Mace be to get all-over a
man that comes out equal with Larry Foley 1 ’ ”

I IThe statement circulated by a last year’s N.S.
Wales football representative that certain players had re-
ceived remuneration for their services came before
N.S.W. Rugby Football Union last week, when it was
decided, after evidence, that there was no truth in the
yarn. The voluble gentleman is to appear before the
committee and show cause, &c.
* » * *
On Saturday the series of what may be termed
“ scratch ’’-matches was continued by Sydney senior foot-
ballers preparatory to the opening of the season on 14th
May. The newly-formed Sydneys outclassed Paddington
by 22 points to 3 ; Pirates beat Wallaroo by 9 points to 3 ;
and Randwick beat Burwood by 16 points to 6.
• • • *
“ Newhaven 11. is doing good work in his
training,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent
(under date of five weeks ago), “ and Blackwell, one of
the young school of trainers, who formerly had D.
Cooper's horses, has great hopes of netting" the City
and Suburban [for which he ran second.] The last
mile and a-quarter of the Derby course is an
easy distance down hill to Tattenham Corner-the
ugly turn ; then straight home finishing up the slope
should not be too much for the Australian cham-
pion with Bst. 81b. He has acclimatised well, is now (31st
March) at 25's, and not counted by the local turfites.
Newman, reserved for the Chester" Cup, is a doubtful
starter for the C. and S., for which Chelandrv is favorite,
and Mrs. Langtry’s Brayhead next at 10’s. Ebor won the
Great Kent Steeple and £3OO stake for bis new owner,
Captain Lambton, in a wretched field. He started second
best at 7 to 4, and the 5 to 4 chance fell, so he waltzed
home by a dozen lengths. Dollery was up, who rode
Cloister to victory in the Grand National record year
with 12st. 71b."

This “cable,” from a Westralian paper, jusfc
about takes the cake : “ Lady Merex has purchased the
picture, ‘ The Survivor,’ painted by Mr. Kelso, the Sydney
artist, and exhibited in the Australian Artists’ Gallery.”
To people who know the astute William, an artist with-
out a doubt in his own line, it is one of the funniest
things that ever appeared in print.
* * * *
The benefit-match to Ned Gregory came too
late in the season to allow of a sum being raised worthy
of the veteran. Some £l2O was the amount realised, in-
cluding 20gs. given by the N.S.W. Cricket Association.
The Combined team made a good showing, 302, of which
Pye, of Central Cumberland, scored 138. For the Pad-
dington team, Trumper, M'Kenzie and Noonan ran up
172 among them, and then a rot set in, and the innings
closed at 186 for six wickets.
• * * *
Even Sandow hardly seems to be “ in it ” with
Oscar Saxon, now exhibiting with his brothers in London.
Though only 10, he scales 15st., and can, while raising
2611 b. at arm’s-length overhead, bend down and lift with
the other hand an additional trifle of 1121 b. Lying on his
back, he can raise at arm's-length a 2611 b. bar-bell, with
over 26st. of his two brothers erected on it—in all 681 b.
over the quarter-ton.
• • • •
Michael, the celebrated cyclist, talks of desert-
ing the wheel and taking to horse-jockeying; and the
papers are discussing the matter seriously. That the fact
of a man being light, and able to ride a bike better than
other men, should make him a jockey ready to jump into
the first flight is too ridiculous to justify the serious com-
ment which Yankee and British papers are giving the
matter.
• • * *
Four hundred money-orders, payable to “ Tat-
tersall ” Adams, went down with the steamer Manawatu,
in Hobson’s Bay, last week.

Bob : “ Constable Maginnity, having re-
cently raided a Melb. public-house and arrested a
roomful of Sabbath gamblers, took away their
braces and cut off the top buttons of their pants,
to make sure that no stampede should take place.”
It is an old dodge with Parisian scrgents-de-ville
to thus necessitate the holding-on of an arrested
rough’s “ bags.” Like most things French—neat,
simple, and effective.
Croson : “ What is the attraction between
women and funerals ? An ordinary-sized cortege
in Simpson’s-road, Collingwood, the other Saturday
afternoon, attracted pretty well all the women
within cooee, and everyone passing brought
her pram and babies to a dead stop and waited
half-an-hou'r in drizzling rain to see the dead
brought out. Then she remarked that it was a
lovely funeral, and passed on to beat the grocer
out of a half-penny on the week’s provisions.”
A corpse arrived at a Q. town lately under
circumstances which made medical enquiry neces-
sary. The local doctor, when applied to, was
found to be very hiccuppy; but the corpse wouldn’t
wait, so the constable led the doctor into the room,
him to make thorough examination,
and left him alone with the body. After half-an-
hour outside bobby thought the enquiry should be
nearly over, so he cautiously opened the door and
peeped in. There was the doctor fast asleep bv
the stretcher-side, with one hand firmly'clutching
deceased’s wrist. “Arrah, now, wake yerself
doctor,” said the constable, shaking him, “an’
tell me what did it, there’s a dear ! ” The doctor
rubbed his eyes and stared owlishly at the officer.
“ Hie ! ” he said. “Hie ! allsh I can shay ish, if
he doeshn t pull through before to-morrow
morningsh I won’t ansher for hish life.”

In the very small hours of a recent morn-
ing two Carlton (Melb.) police heard shrieks of
murder from a wooden shanty in a lane, and they
promptly burst through the door, and charged
upstairs to investigate. It was the domicile of
some Indian hawkers, and two shuddery “ cops ”
afterwards related in court what they found
inside. In a small room, with a ceiling about sft.
bin. high, they discovered four Asiatics and two
white women, all drunk, and mid nodings on. In
two other small rooms there were 10 Asiatics, with
very little on. The 10 Asiatics with very little on
requested that the six people with nothing on
should be removed, as their row made it quite im-
possible for the others to sleep, and if they didn’t
get rested they couldn't go out peddling the brass
jewellery in the morning. The law sentenced one
lady to three months, and the other—who said she
had a good home with a Chow, in Little Bourke-
street—got off with one month, apparently out of
consideration for the Chow. Then the court
asked some questions about the landlord who let
the house to the swarm of heathen with so very
little on, but his name didn’t transpire. Some-
how, the name of the respectable Christian gent,
who owns the house where six naked people howl
“ murder ” in the early morning never does trans-
pire.

NERVOUS AND DESPONDENT.
That man can sit in his office day after day
throughout the year and attend to the important
duties of a merchant, financier, departmental-
head, mine-manager, banker, expert, and politician,
&c., and at the same time be tormented by some
ailment which has baffled the MEDICAL PRO-
FESSION. Too close attention to business,
being confined too much indoors, and the worry
and trouble of everyday mercantile, professional
or official life, will knock up the strongest man,
who is suffering from one of the many NER-
VOUS COMPLAINTS which the flesh is heir to.

“ Mr. W. G. HEARNE.—Dear Sir, —I had a bad attack

WALES OF THE CONVICI’ SYSTEM, by

At the New York Morgue, where, in addi-
tion to casualty subjects, the dead of the poorer
classes are received for free interment by the
State, there is an endless procession of weeping
women bearing boxes, portmanteaux, and even
paper bags, containing dead children. This cease-
less procession is one of the world’s most heart-
breaking spectacles.
A remarkable example of the influence of
vested interests was the violent opposition of the
business people of the Place de la Roquette, Paris,
to the proposal by Government to abolish public
executions. Landlords united with tenants, shop-
keepers with cabmen, and so powerful was the
protest that the Govt.; fearing it might even
become a barricade matter, backed down before a
petition which set forth that for the past 100 years
the whole of the locality had literally lived on the
crowds of decapitation-witnesses —from the capi-
talist owner of the block opposite the scaffold down
to the man who peddled fruit amongst the crowd
and the “ poet ” who hawked about the customary
history of the crime in execrable verse.
“ Ignotus, ” on the stamp-mania :
Collecting stamps rs a queer hobby. There are cata-
logues of all existing stamps, with prices. These, how-
ever, seem to express rakrtjve, and not actual, values.
The actual values of rare stamps may be got at from the
published results of auction sales. The value of common
stamps may be guessed at from an inspection of a stand-
ing advertisement from a leading organ of the hobby, in
which you see the common current issue of English
offered at £26 per ton ! The great bulk of the stamps in
any ordinary collection have next to no value. A friend
once took his collection to a big dealer, with an idea of
doing business. The dealer picked out stamps valued in
bis own catalogue at £B, and offered in exchange stamps
from same catalogue to valueBs. The word “ Philately,”
applied to the hobby, is of doubtful derivation. I was
told as a schoolboy that the first man who started
a stamp-collectors’ magazine applied to a classi-
cal friend for a swell Greek name, and was
given “Philatelist” in derision, the meaning being
“ a lover of the fruitless.” And, so far as Liddell and
Scott help, that is what it means. Serious philatelists,
however, say that “ Ateles ” means, not merely “ ob-
jectless,” but also “ untaxed,” “ free from tax.” Hence
“ a lover of what frees from tax, or makes to go post-
free.” Very small variations in stamps make differences
in value'. A week ago I came across a lot of used Maori-
land half-pennies all perforated 11. That means they
have 11 perforations to every two centimetres. They
were priced in the received catalogues at Is. 3d. each,
and I reckoned I could sell them for at least Is. a
dozen. Unfortunately, on examining one current issue, I
found they had the same perforation. The explanation is
that the 11 perforation had been long disused in favor of
12£, but had recently been resumed. You could make a
budget of stories, true or false, about fortunes made out
of stamps. Errors are valuable, Had I known as much
as 1 do now 20 years ago. I could easily have made my
pile. Nothing would have been easier than to persuade
the printers here to strike me off a number of sheets with
an unusual perforation, or in an odd shade of color. ‘ But
a few years ago the Newfoundland Govt, issued stamps
valued £68,200, of which £B2OO represents the expense
of production and the portion used for postage. The rest
were bought by collectors, and the colony thus defrayed
more than half the interest oujthe public debt for the
year. Of course, as soon as the determined quantity was
printed the dies were destroyed. This gave them their
value. The same scheme would not answer now, as col-
lectors refuse to buy “unnecessary” issues. P.S.—I
weighed some stamps and found they went about
30,000,000 to the ton. You can weigh, say, Joz. and
verify this in office. Yet the advt. offering current
English Jd. and Id. stamps at “ £26 per ton” is from
Stamp Collectors’ Fortnightly for 5/2/’9B, and appa-
rently genuine. ’
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all.other Australian gems.*
Some people are queer luck-bringers. The
other day a Brunswick (Yip.) girl saw a white
horse grazing in a paddock near a quarry-hole.
She made the most of the omen and cried out “ A
cross ! A cross 1 A lucky white horse! ” A few
minutes afterwards the horse fell over tlje quarry-
bank, broke his leg, and had to be shot. Evil eye,
or what? Fact, anyhow, and bad luck for the
white horse.

More Melbourne Marvels.
FOUR PEOPLE RESTORED TO HEALTH BY BILE BEANS FOR BILIOUSNESS.
MELBOURNE PEOPLE ASTOUNDED.
(Read what the Melbourne Advocate says of a prominent theatrical publisher, his wife’s, son’s,
and brother’B cure).
A most remarkable oase has been brought to our notice (says the Melb. Advocate) in that of
Mr. F. G. Salter, the well-known theatrical man, who resides at 15 Fawcett-street, Albert’Park To
let Mr. Salter speak in his own words “ For years I was a constant
sufferer from biliousness and its dreaded effects. Needless to remark,
various doctors and patent medicines were tried with the hope of
being cured, but little, if any, relief was experienced from these
sources. After an unsatisfactory night’s rest, I was wont to rise with
a nasty taste in the mouth and a feeling of intense weariness. The
throat was parched and dry, and the whole system was being gradually
undermined, from loss of appetite, one of the disastrous effects of
biliousness. Indeed, I turned away from meat, especially in the
morning, with disgust, and had to content myself with a bare collation
of a little bread and tea.
“ Such was the state of things when I happened to hear from a
friend about the efficacy of the newly discovered cure for biliousness,
Bile Beans. A first I was a little incredulous. Nevertheless, follow-
ing the motto, ‘try again,’l determined to procure a box, and put
their value to a test. Well, sir, my most sanguino anticipations have
been more than realised. The fact is that, after one or two doses, I
experienced a thorough change for the better in my system. My
former vigor began to return, the symptoms above enumerated gradu-
ally disappeared, and the whole man felt that he was in possession of
a new lease of life. More palpable evidence of restored health could
not better be seen than the fact of the return of my former healthy
appetite, to which I had been for a long time a stranger. Thanks to
the power of Bile Beans, I can now take and enjoy a hearty break-
fast. lunch and dinner. My former glow of health and lightness of
spirits have again returned. In one word, lam a new man.”

Subsequently Mm, Salter came into the drawing-room, and, in answer to a question from the
reporter, declared that she had experienced untold benefits from taking only a few Bile Beans, she,
like her husband, having been a sufferer from this complaint.
“ A fine three-year-old boy came bouncing into the room. ' Papa,’ cried the little chap, * give me
Bile Beans.’ Asked how his infant son was so fond of the medicine, Mr. Salter said that the little
fellow had not been well, and, finding the beans such an excellent restorative, he ventured to administer
half a one to the child. He pointed, with pardonable pride, to the bright eyes and glowing cheeks
of his erstwhile delicate boy as the soundest and most emphatic testimony of the remarkable virtue
of Bile Beans for young and old.
“After so gratifying a test of the value of this medicine, Mr. Salter sent a box to his brother,
Mr. Charles Salter, the well-known carrier and forwarding agent, of Bendigo, and a prominent member
of the Bendigo Football Club. This gentleman has written a letter to his brother in Melbourne, in
which lie cannot find,"words sufficiently strong enough to recommend Bile Beans to all who are, as he
himself once was, a sufferer from biliousness, indigestion, etc. He is fully prepared to substantiate
the remarks of Mr. F. G. Salter. If ever there was a strong case, this is one ; the whole matter may
be put in a nutshell —‘ facta non verba.’
A Valuable Discovery.
Scientists tell us that in this nineteenth century of ours about the greatest strides made in the
discovery world have been in medicine. We have to record a discovery by an American medical man
of a certain vegetable substance that lias the effect in the digestion organs of nature’s own animal-bile;
This discovery is revolutionising all the present methods of treatment for digestive ailments. The
old theory of pepsin, bismuth, starving, purging, etc., etc., is to be done away with. This great
vegetable substance is compounded witli other ingredients to facilitate the working, and to-day this
remedy can be purchased the world over and in Australia under the name of Bile Beans for Biliousness.
This great remedy possesses the peculiar properties of acting on the bowels without purging or other-
wise weakening the patient. This is because the substance that relaxes the bowels has about the same
action as the natural bile. Bile Beans are purely vegetable, and a course of them will probably suc-
cessfully oust the most chronic case of piles, indigestion, chronic headache, biliousness, rheumatism,
constipation, and a host of other ailments that owe their origin to defective bile flow, assimilation, and
digestion. These Beans are placed on the market in a form that anyone can take them without
m 'dical supervision, and, as Uic price is so very low, there are few homes that cannot afford to always

Johnson’s Deficiency.—A Bank-
ing Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Probyn Smith, teller of the Capital and
Investment Bank at Rod way, discovered, one busy
Saturday morning, that his copper would not last
out the day. Calling the junior, he gave him five
sovereigns, and sent him over to the Great
Southern Bank for an equivalent in copper. John-
son, teller at the latter office, always obliging,
and, on this occasion, glad of the opportunity to
reduce a heavy supply of copper, readily responded.
Entering his strong-room, he picked up, as he
thought, a £5 bag of copper, which he brought
out and passed over to the messenger. What he
did take up was a bag of silver coin, of the value
of one hundred pounds.
It may be explained that a <£s bag of copper
and a bag containing £lOO worth of silver are not
distinguishable, even to the practised eye of the
bank-teller. Placed side by side, the two bags
are of equal bulk, and, tried in the hand, of equal
weight.
It happened during the messenger’s absence that
a customer of the C. and I. Bank visited the office,
and paid in an amount of copper that relieved the
teller’s necessity, and carried him through the
morning. The supposed bag of borrowed copper,
not being wanted when it arrived, was put on one
side, unopened, and eventually placed in the
strong-room, to be counted at leisure.
It was Johnson’s misfortune, in view of the
blunder he had made, that, in making up his cash
at the close of business, he followed the practice
of most tellers who keep count in their heads of
what coin they may have in reserve in the safe,
and which, to save trouble, is not brought to the
counter each day. Johnson therefore deducted,
mentally, £5 of copper, and added in a £lOO bag
of silver, which was safely reposing in the strong-
room of the Capital and Investment Bank, at the
reduced value of £5, as counted—unluckily for
Johnson—by the teller there. It was also an
additional misfortune that copper flowed freely
into the till of the C. and I. Bank the next day,
so that Probyn Smith had no call to handle the
borrowed bag of coin. This was a fatal mischance;
for Probyn Smith, being lazy, was not likely to
disturb the unlucky bag till necessity should
compel him. It wiil be seen that Fate was dead
against Johnson, and his evil star was still on his
heels.
» * * *
Twelve miles from Rodway there was another
branch of the Capital and Investment Bank, the
manager of which occasionally visited the Rodway
office on business, or otherwise, Stanford not being
particular. Sometimes his business required that
h&hjhQuld obtain cash supplies for his own branch.
Such a mission drew him to Rodway a few days
subsequent to the incident of Johnson’s lapse.
Copper was not exactly the object of his visit, and
it was only a provident mind that prompted him,
on-passing through the Rod way office on his way
out, to casually enquire of Probyn Smith, “ How
are you off for copper ? ” offering to take any sur-
plus, up to 655. ; and the latter, being selfish as
well as lazy, immediately bethought him of the
bag received from Johnson, the counting of which
wdtild be thus passed on.
“ I can give you five pounds,” he said. “ It is a
bag I got a few days ago from the ‘ Southern.’
You can take it and go through it for me. I give
it to you as I got it! ”
Stanford was agreeable, and took the bag back
to his branch, where it was thrown into a corner
of the Btrong . room) unopened, as at Rodway.
Here it lay undisturbed for more than a month.
In the meanwhile Probyn Smith died of typhoid
fever.
Some seven weeks in all went by, that is from
the day on which Johnson made his blunder. All
this time the latter had been counting into his
cash a sum of ninety-five pounds for which there
was no equivalent in his safe, and the manager, on
the occasion of his several checkings, had taken
Johnson’s word that so many bags contained sil-
ver and so many copper—a trust and confidence
that expedited the work of auditing and saved
trouble. All of which goes to show that you can
multiply your checks ad infinitum, ajid the
friendly association of two or more men will ren-
J er them void. Of course, it will be patent that
if Johnson’s manager had performed his task with
some distrust of his subordinate, or with con-
scientious regard to duty, the error, or shortage,
would have immediately come to light, and John-
son’s character been saved. But if and if, and if
.', „ * , * , ,
3 now follows. The branch-inspector of
t.he Great Southern Bank honored the branch with
a . yisif that was as unwelcome as “surprise
] n P nva f e bfe usually are.
, Without ceremony an inspector demands from
the teller his keys and his cash, and then he sets
to work searching for discrepancies, whilst the
teller stands calmly by, knowing the former will
h ave his trouble for nothing. But a bank-
inspector never loses heart, and goes through the
same drudgery at every branch with the same re-
-B}dt, for bank-clerks are honest nowadays. Some-
times the teller is called upon to assist in the work
of counting the cash which he knows to be cor-
rect. Ihis is not so good as it looks for the
teller—for he is only permitted to count silver,
which he is to spread out on the counter before
the inspector in £1 heaps. Johnson was directed
to do this, and rapidly reduced the several bags
E™? hi , m t° symmetrical rows ; then waited
whilst the inspector took tally. This done, the
la *ter added them to his own sum, and then corn-
pared the result with the entry m Johnson’s book,
The tw o Bums did not tally. Without a word the
inspector went over the silver again; then he
looked up at Johnson. “Have you more silver m
the safe ?” he enquired,
s,r - n e r e>
You are £95 short, then,
R can f “ e > sir * R was correct last night,
replied Johnson promptly and positively.
The inspector scanned the teller’s face and saw
that he was in earnest,
“ Let me see you go through it, then,” he said
quietly.
Very carefully, and with no misgiving, Johnson
went over his cash.
“ Well? ” asked the inspector when the other had
made up the total of his count,
Johnson called the amount.
“ Yes, that agrees with me,” the inspector re-
marked in a voice that requested further informa-
tion before asking for it. “You are ninety-live
pounds short. How do you account for it ? ”
Johnson, having no explanation, offered many
in his nervousness. The manager was called and
informed of the deficiency. But he could only
look to his teller for an explanation, which he
sought by the silent enquiry of his eye, and John-
son replied in the negative by the same medium '
of speech. The possibilities of the situation had
opened out to his imagination and depressed him
to a mute. The inspector’s voice recalled him to
present realities.
“ The difference must be found, or I must have
a satisfactory explanation,” the latter remarked,
addressing the manager and clerk together.
In lieu of a “ satisfactory explanation ” the
manager bustled, too, at discovering the missing
amount, which he set about by thoroughly ran-
sacking the strong-room, pulling out books from
their places, opening Gladstone-bags, looking
down the narrow space of one inch, between the
wall and safe, for a bag of silver of the amount
involved, even lifting up sheets of paper that
could not have covered a five-shilling-piece with-
out betrayal. The inspector, seeing the state of
mind to which anxiety had reduced the manager,
relieved the pressure by saying, with deadly pre-
cision and in the teller’s hearing—
“ Mr. Johnson has no explanation to offer. His
cash is short by ninety-five pounds I ”
“ I know nothing about it, sir. It was right
last evening,” interposed Johnson excitedly.
The inspector observed in a chillingly official
voice which, it will be admitted, the gravity of
the situation permitted, “I shall have to put the
office under suspension.” Then he retired to the
manager’s sanctum to indite his report of the cir-
cumstance, whilst the manager took the oppor-
tunity to approach his teller and obtain a confes-
sion which he expected to get by asking Johnson,
kindly and sympathetically, to “ try and think
how it had occurred ? ”
Johnson couldn’t account for it, and said so in
a way that convinced his interrogator that he was
speaking the truth.
“Something may turn up before the day is
out,” the other said, hopefully.
Johnson hoped so, too, for his own sake, as well
as the manager’s, for the latter was a thoroughly
good fellow and might have to share in the office
disgrace.
* * • *
For the rest of the day Johnson racked his
brain for an explanation of his shortage. That it
was an error, and not a theft in the office, he did
not doubt for a moment. There were many prob-
able causes, principally that of over-payment.
First he tried mentally to recall all the large
transactions of the past few days; then he went
through the week’s vouchers, taking them day by
day. A bank’s vouchers bear on their face a
history of the transaction in which they have
figured. Readers whose fortunate lot it may be
to visit a bank with a cheque, and not a deed, in
their hands, will observe that the teller records on
each cheque, as he pays it, the nature of such pay-
ment. Many a bank-teller has owed to this hasty
record the recovery of moneys which would other-
wise have remained in the custody of a dishonest
payee. Johnson racked his brain till he became
stupid, and had to stop. His brain, nevertheless,
continued its labors independently of the living
will. Suddenly the man received an inspiration.
The brain had done its work successfully, and de-
sired no reward. Johnson took the credit. With
a confident smile, he rushed in to the manager.
“ I have it,” he said, and related the transaction
of the bag of copper given to the messenger sent by
the late Probyn Smith. “ I remember,” Johnson
rattled on excitedly, “ his saying to me, a day or
two after, that he could have done without the
copper after all, but would keep it for future use
and count it then ; and I remember I said, * Don’t
trouble, it is all right.’ I gave him silver instead,
not a doubt about it. I had a lot of silver by me
at that time!” Johnson was almost incoherent
from excitement.
“Johnson ! that’s your ninety-five pounds,” the
manager replied, with a solemnity that nearly
upset Johnson’s decorum, much as the situation
weighed upon him. Then the other , spoilt the
bright prospect by continuing : “ But Smith is
dead, and the new fellow would be sure to have
found it out if it had been as you say. At any
rate, I’ll go across and see. There is no knowing. ”
Johnson was in as great a tremor during the
manager’s brief absence as if he had a dozen
parlor galvanic batteries toying with his nerves,
The return of the latter quickly relieved the
tension. _ )
“No good,” he said briefly, answering Johnson’s
look. “ Cullen himself (the manager of the 0.
and I. Bank) says that he counted every penny
of Smith’s cash', the day after he was taken ill,
and would have discovered any such error as
yours.” _
“That’s how I lost my ninety-five pounds!
replied Johnson, with quiet conviction.
“ And Smith is dead ! ” the other answered
grimly.
This very possible and likely solution was duly
imparted to the inspector, who admitted its pro-
bability, and nothing more. The only man who
could confirm the conjecture was dead, and —
“dead men tell no tales.” Thus left, Johnson
lost hope and resigned himself to fate,
* * * *
Act 4 sees Johnson in the head-office,
whither he had been reC alled. For a. week he re-
mained under suspension, pending inquiry and
examination into his books for the expected dis-
crepancies of a more “ ser ious nature.” .. Nothing
further turned up, which the reader will not be
surprised to hea £. btifc; being satisfied from the
discovery of the one item that a promising career
of embezzlement had been nipped in the bud, the
bank finally dealt with its luck i ess officer. Prose-
cution cou f d npt be ins tituted, because there was
no evidence of appropriation. After full con-
Oration, it was decided that Johnson’s case
would be met by the resignation of that unlucky
OU t of the banking chamber, and
, -v j- ® Lti,
stepped into Oollins-street a disgraced
the parting words of a Scotch d ! r^ t °yA n r ?' 11 ?,"

PELICAN.
The Bush Missionary.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'Twas on old M‘Carson’s station, near the finish
of the shearing,
We were seated round the table in the hut, and
playing 100 ;
An unrighteous occupation, nor particularly cheer-
ing,
When your tally’s only middling, and your luck
-is looking blue ;
But there’s nothing else to do,
So it’s poker or it’s 100,
In the afternoon of Saturday on Coolabungaroo !
Jack the Rat, who did the pressing, sat outside
the door a-smoking,
And a-telling all the rouseabouts of horses he
had “broke,”
And our sorrow grew distressing at the “ borak ”
he was poking,
When he put his head inside the hut and whis-
pered, ‘ * Holy smoke !
Here’s a sanguinary joke ! ”
And he chuckled fit to choke,
“ Here’s the lanky Scotchbyterian, the mis-.
Bionary bloke ! ”
Well, he looked to see him coming, and he “ took
him out o’-winding,”
He was long, and he was lanky; he was freckle-
some and fair,
And a hymn he was a-humming, just as if he
wasn’t minding,
And he asked if any shearer had a mind to cut
his hair /
Wo could only gape and stare,
’Cause we didn’t like to swear;
But the ringer said he’d do it, with a bucket for a
chair!
So the ringer started quickly (with the shears he
was a dandy),
But he clipped a kind of pimple and the parson
gave a bound!
Then the ringer tarred it thickly and confessed he
felt “unhandy ”
The position, for a shearer, “ rather awkward-
ish ” he found !
Then he downed him on the ground,
And he whipped his neck around,
And he “ pinked ” him like a leather-neck when
squatters paid a pound !
Now, the ringer ’d just got through this un-
accustomed operation,
When M‘Carson, who’d been mustering, arrived
upon the scene,
And die shearers they were treated to a masterly
oration
By the choleric M‘Carson, whose vocabulary
keen,
As was easy to be seen,
W as more forcible than clean —
And remarkably distasteful to the Reverend
M ‘Lean !
So the parson he suggested, as a means of recon-
ciling
(Not indeed that he objected to the way they’d
cut his hair !)
That the parties interested should agree to his
beguiling
All the station-hands and rouseabouts with ser-
vices of prayer;
Which the squatter thought was fair,
He was fond of praise and prayer !
And, the station-hands consenting, service
started then and there !
Now, the preaching it was splendid, but the
shearers jibbed at singing,
Though the squatter joined the preacher, not
another soul would sing !
Then the service was upended, and M‘Carson’s
arms went swinging,
And he raved and stamped and cursed and
swore and called us everything !
“Sing, yer blanky beggars, sing 1
Make the blanky welkin ring !
Won’t you blanky sons op blankers help
THE BLANKY MAN TO SING ! ”
We were sorry for the parson, though he was a bit
erratic,
’Cause he was an all-right preacher and a decent
fellow, too;
But, you see, he found M'Carson so ferociously
emphatic
He concluded that the services in future
wouldn’t do.
So the shearers play at 100,
And at whisky-poker, too,
And the parson is a scarcity at Coolabungaroo !
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.
Melb. Herald suggests that the local
police should be taught the Scandinavian method
of dealing with drunks. It it alleged that when a
drunk is arrested in Sweden, and becomes violent
in his cell, the constable in charge wheels in the
lockup-piano and starts to soothe him by singing
and playing operatic selections. This may act all
right with the Swedish boozer, but if any such
game were tried with the proud Australian drunk
he would probably do his best to smash up the
fiiano and its policeman-player. He may be
oaded up with liquor, but he has his feelings.
The local constable knows this and soothes him
with a baton —not a musical conductor’s baton.
Summer at Dawson City.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sounds of revelry have subsided -into the
occasional whoops of the belated and hilarious
digger, who is-spending his hard-won dust' in the
drinking-gambling-dancing saloons of Dawson
City, the new metropolis of the golden north. The
long, low, log cabins facing the principal street are
ablaze with torches, lamps and candles. The
wheezy strains of “fiddles” come from a half-
dozen barn-shaped edifices, bearing gaudily-
painted signs announcing that “Lucky Lew” or
“Placer Pete” dispenses “finest old whisky”,
within this “palace” or that “emporium.” In
front rolls the mighty Yukon. With her nose
buried in the mudbank, the little Bella steamboat
struggles hard to keep her head up-stream. Three
Indians and a repentant toper slowly and wearily
carry cord-wood aboard her for a wage of ss. an
hour. In the mellow light of the “ Midnight
Sun ” the flood of the Klondyke shines golden, as
it joins the darker sweep of the Yukon. The
streets are rivers of mud and moss. The rest of the
flat point of land on which this newest of cities is
located is quiet and lonely.
At midnight breaks out a chorus of baying from
the hundred dogs of Dawson City, and then, echo-
like, far away to the north —another dog-chorus,
sharper and more wolfish. Then there is a strange
sight. Over the brow of the hill, clearly outlined
in the bright northern light, appears a gaunt,
shaggy form, more like a giant wolf than anything
else, walking slowly and sedately towards us, and
followed by another and another, till sixty and
more are seen gravely stepping one behind the
other, after the manner of their Indian masters.
The town dogs meet them at the foot of the hill,
and for an hour the two sets yelp and bark and
howl, till a shot from an irate digger scatters the
lot. Then the Siwash canines file away as quietly
as they came, over the hill to the Indian village a
few miles down the river.
The Indians of the Yukon Valley are as peace-
able as they are unclean, and dirt is their strong
point in more senses than one. They are lazy
also, but fairly reliable; expert river-men and
good trappers and hunters. White men who
understand their peculiarities grow rich trading
with them, while those unaccustomed to their
ways can make no headway at all. The rule, is
pay the price they ask for their furs, moccasins,
&0., without attempting to chaffer, and in return
they will pay your prices without demur. Of
course the great profit in trading with them comes
from their childlike fondness for trifles that catch
the eye or please their fancy. One often gets a
really valuable fur for a trinket of little or no
value, yet the Indian knows as well as you the
value of that fur in dollars and cents ; but he
measures the price of the trinket by his desire
for it.
The selling of provisions, clothing, tools,. etc. ,•
was a monopoly, last summer, in the hands of two
companies which owned all the river boats, but
the coming season will see a number of rival lines,
and competition will ensure plenty of grub at fair
prices.
The moss which overlies all that region is at
once the curse and the salvation of the miner.
Very useful in filling the chinks in the log side-
walls of the houses, and for a roof-thatch. Also,
in winter it makes an even, smooth bed for the
snow, and thus greatly facilitates travel. But in
summer I You sink to your knees in it, and it
grips you like glue, and impedes travel so much
that carriage of .supplies has to be made before the
thaw in May,' or after the frost - in September.
Men of long experience in that region even prefer
winter to summer. No pen will ever be able
to do justice to the subject of the pestering
mosquitoes and flies. All ordinary precautions
fail against the Yukon skeeter. Nothing but tar
or buckskin leather will keep him off.
There was a jolly lot of journalists in Dawson
last August; men from the big dailies of the
States, armed with cheques signed in blank and
unlimited letters of credit, with instructions to
spare no expense to get news out to the world
before winter set in. A picturesque figure was
Joaquin Miller, the “ Poet of the Sierras.” Miller
is old and gray and feeble, but his spirit is un-
quenchable and his humor and gaiety are inex-
haustible.
About a year ago a theatrical troupe of four
women and two men came in over the Chilkopt
Pass. They had started for Circle City, not hav-
ing heard of Klondyke or Dawson until
well on their way. But of course they
stopped at Dawson, and they “struck it rich.”
Three of the four actresses (song - and - dance
species) married lucky miners, and the fourth
is coining money in a dance-hall, singing senti-
mental ditties, of which her audience shows its
appreciation by showering bags of gold-dust upon
her. They say she has 40,000 dollars locked up in
the A.C. Co.’s safe. Prodigality in Dawson last
summer was confined principally to wage workers;
few owners of claims on Eldorado or Bonanza did
much “shouting.” Exceptions were “Swift-
water Bill” “Nigger Pete,” and Portuguee
Manuel. Manuel paid the singing-woman men-
tioned above 100 dollars per day not to dance with
anyone but him. This bargain the girl hersplf
broke at the end of ten days, because receipts were
falling off. , ; ■ ■ _
